# Chapter 1

## **CHAPTER 1**

The whisky was a peat-bog ghost, haunting the glass. Isla MacLeod held it up to the weak Glasgow light bleeding through the pub window, watching the amber liquid cling to the sides like a confession. It was ten in the morning. The pub, *The Hanged Man*, smelled of last night’s spilled lager and this morning’s despair, a perfume she was coming to know intimately.

“He’s late,” she said, to no one.

“He’s a concept, hen,” Morag replied from behind the bar, polishing a pint glass with a cloth that had seen more battles than the standard at Culloden. “Concepts are never late. They just are, or they aren’t.” Morag was seventy if she was a day, her hair a vibrant, improbable aubergine, her arms a roadmap of faded blue tattoos. She ran *The Hanged Man* with the serene brutality of a pirate queen overseeing a derelict ship.

Isla’s concept was Professor Alistair Finch. Emeritus Chair of Comparative Metaphysics, University of Glasgow. Author of *The Architecture of Unseen Bridges: A Treatise on Interstitial Realities*. He had written her a letter on thick, cream paper that felt like pressed bone. *Your thesis on the gendered cartography of liminal spaces in Scottish folklore shows a… disturbing acuity,* it read. *Disturbing, because it hints at doors better left painted shut. Come and discuss. Bring a bottle of something that tastes of earth and endings.*

So here she was, with a bottle of 25-year-old Laphroaig, in a pub that felt like the waiting room for a ferry no one wanted to board.

The door groaned open, admitting not a man, but a disturbance in the air. A tweed overcoat that seemed to be consuming its wearer, a shock of white hair like arctic moss, and eyes the colour of a winter sea under a storm. He paused, sniffed the air as if tasting its composition, and beelined for her table.

“You have the peat,” he stated, his voice a dry rustle of pages. “Good. The cheap stuff is for tourists and the spiritually bankrupt.” He slid into the booth opposite her, shedding his coat to reveal a cardigan of such profound, cable-knotted complexity it could have been a topological map of a nervous breakdown. “Isla MacLeod. You look less like a vandal than I’d feared.”

“A vandal, Professor?”

“Of boundaries, lass. You’re poking at the mortar between worlds with your feminist critique. Dangerous masonry. It can fall on you.” He produced two crystal tumblers from a leather satchel, placing them on the sticky table with ceremonial care. “Pour.”

She did. The whisky released its smoky breath into the space between them.

Finch took a sip, closed his eyes, and shuddered. “Aye. That’s the stuff. The memory of drowned forests. Now. Your paper. The *Bean Nighe*.”

The washerwoman. The omen of death, seen at fords, cleaning the blood-stained clothes of those about to die.

“You posit,” he continued, “that she is not merely a harbinger, but a *custodian*. That the ford, the *linne*, is not just a geographical threshold, but a gendered one. A space where the work of women—cleaning, grieving, preparing the body—bleeds into the metaphysical. That her weeping is a solvent, washing away the boundary between this life and the next.”

“It’s an interpretation,” Isla said, her own whisky untouched.

“It’s a bloody crowbar,” he corrected, opening his eyes. They were alarmingly bright. “And you used it to jimmy open a window I’ve spent forty years trying to keep locked. Tell me, have you ever *seen* a liminal space… shimmer?”

The question was so absurd she almost laughed. But his face was granite. “Shimmer?”

“Aye. A visual stutter. A place that looks back. Most people, their minds just skate over it. They see a misty glen, an old doorway, a stretch of river, and think ‘pretty’ or ‘dull’. But sometimes, for some people, the veil is thinner. The mind’s eye gets a splinter of the real picture. Women, often. The perpetually grieving, the furious, the ones who listen to the silence until it starts to hum.”

Isla thought of the standing stone on the moor near her grandmother’s house. How, as a child, she’d sometimes see it not as grey, but as a deep, vibrating indigo, like a thumb pressed against a closed eyelid. She’d told her mother, who’d called it a migraine coming on.

“Suppose I have,” she said carefully.

Finch leaned forward, his scent a blend of mothballs, old books, and that profound whisky. “The world is a layer cake, Miss MacLeod. Geology, biology, history, society. All sensible strata. But between the layers… there’s the icing. The sticky, irrational, *symbolic* filling. Most cultures learn to ignore it. We Scots, we’re cursed with a sweet tooth for it. We built our national identity on the stuff. And it’s thickest here. In the thresholds. The doorways. The fords. The moments between sleeping and waking.”

He drained his glass. “There’s a place. A *linne*. Not on any modern map. It’s in a valley north of Glencoe. The locals, what few are left, call it *Linne nan Daoine Sgàthach*.”

“The Ford of the Shadow People.”

“A poetical translation. More accurately, The Ford of the *Partial* People. The Unfinished. The Maybe-Men and Perhaps-Women.” He fixed her with his storm-sea gaze. “I believe it’s a nexus. A place where the symbolic filling of the world has… congealed. Become semi-permeable. I believe your *Bean Nighe* doesn’t just work there. She *guards* it. And I believe the guard is changing.”

Isla felt a cool finger trace her spine. “Changing how?”

“The old stories are gendered because the old world was. The woman weeps by the water. The man is the one who dies. But what happens, Miss MacLeod, when the symbols get… confused? When the roles won’t stick? What comes to wash the clothes then?” He pushed a folded, yellowed piece of vellum across the table. It was a hand-drawn map, all frantic lines and archaic notations. In the centre, a ford was marked not with an X, but with a small, intricate symbol: a circle divided by a wavy line, one half stippled, the other blank.

“What is that symbol?”

“It’s not a symbol. It’s a question.” He tapped it with a bony finger. “*An e fir no boireannach a th’ ann?* Is it male or female that is in it? That’s the question the ford is asking now. And the answer is… both. And neither. And it’s causing a traffic jam in the metaphysics.”

Isla finally took a drink. The whisky was a fire that burned clean, cutting through the surreal fog of his words. “Why are you telling me this? I’m a postgraduate with a thesis and a mountain of debt, not a… a metaphysician.”

“Because you *see* the shimmer,” he said simply. “And because you think in terms of custodians, not monsters. And because I’m too old and too tired to go. My bones would turn to chalk in a place that uncertain. But you… you’re unfinished yourself, aren’t you? A maybe-woman in a world that demands a certainly.”

It was a brutal, perfect insight. Isla felt it land in her gut. The unfinished thesis, the relationships that dissolved like sugar in rain, the constant sense of being a rough draft of a person.

“What would I do? Take a water sample?”

Finch’s laugh was a short, dry bark. “Observe. Document. Use that crowbar mind of yours. Take this.” He slid a small, cloth-wrapped bundle from his satchel. Unwrapped, it was a camera. An old Rolleiflex, twin lenses like insect eyes. “It uses film. Slow. Deliberate. The digital stuff is too certain, too full of ones and zeroes. This… this captures ghosts better. The light that was, not the light that is.”

“You want me to photograph a superstition.”

“I want you to photograph the question,” he corrected. “The map is your guide. The whisky is for courage. The camera is for proof. For yourself, if no one else.”

“And what’s in it for you?”

“The satisfaction of a retired man who has passed on a particularly vexing puzzle.” He stood, pulling his consuming overcoat back on. “The ford is most active at the cusp seasons. The turn from spring to summer. The *Bealltainn* threshold. You have three weeks. Don’t dally. And Isla?”

She looked up.

“If you see the washerwoman… mind her tears. They’re not just salt and water. They’re the solvent.”

He left then, a disturbance exiting, leaving the air in the pub feeling flat and stale. Morag ambled over, collecting the empty glasses. “Told you. A concept. Did he pay his tab?”

“He didn’t order anything.”

“Aye, but he occupied the space. That’s a penny a minute.” She looked at the map and the camera on the table. “Off to find the maybe-folk, are you?”

“You heard?”

“I hear most things that matter. The rest is just noise.” She wiped the table with a final, decisive swipe. “Take salt. And a knife that’s tasted your own blood. Not for fighting. For remembering what side of the skin you’re on.”

Isla packed the camera and the map into her bag. The weight of them felt absurd. She was a scholar, not an adventurer. Yet, underneath the academic’s scepticism, a deeper, older part of her was stirring. The part that had seen the standing stone shimmer. The part that wrote about weeping women at fords not as folklore, but as fractured truth.

She left *The Hanged Man*, stepping out into the Glasgow drizzle. The city was all solid stone and rushing certainty. Cars, schedules, concrete. But in her bag, a piece of vellum whispered of a place where solid things turned soft, where questions took up space, and where the work of women might be the only thing holding the layers of the world apart.

She didn’t go home. She went to the university library, to the dusty back shelves where they kept the parish records and the unsorted folklore collections. She needed to know what, if anything, lay behind the professor’s madness. She pulled down a volume of 19th-century Highland surveys, its leather spine cracking in protest.

Hours later, her eyes gritty, she found it. A passing mention in a minister’s journal from 1823. *Came upon the valley they call the Sgàthach. A queer, close air. Met a solitary figure by the stream, neither plainly man nor woman, who spoke in riddles of ‘the wash that never ends’. Gave me a profound unease, as if I had looked upon my own soul and found it unfinished.* The minister had drawn a shaky little sketch in the margin. It was the same symbol from Finch’s map.

The cool finger on her spine became a hand, gripping.

Her phone buzzed. A text from her thesis supervisor. *Isla. Progress report? The committee is asking about your field research chapter. We need something tangible.*

She looked at the ancient book, at the minister’s terrified scrawl. She looked at her bag holding the camera and the map.

*Something tangible.*

She texted back: *Beginning field research tomorrow. Remote location. May be out of touch for a week or two. Following a lead on custodial figures in liminal spaces.*

The reply was almost immediate. *Custodial figures? Isla, this needs to be academically rigorous, not a ghost hunt. Keep it grounded.*

Too late, she thought. The ground was already shifting.

She left the library as it closed, the dusk settling over Glasgow like a damp shroud. In her flat, she packed a rucksack with practical things: boots, waterproofs, a sleeping bag, a torch. And then the impractical: the salt, a small pocket knife from her kitchen. She pricked her thumb, a bright bead of blood welling up. She smeared it on the knife’s blade. *For remembering what side of the skin you’re on.*

Lastly, she loaded film into the Rolleiflex, the mechanism smooth and heavy. She looked through the viewfinder, the world appearing upside down and reversed. A disorienting, truthful perspective.

That night, she dreamed of a river. The water was the colour of slate and milk. On the far bank, a figure knelt, scrubbing at a pile of cloth that leaked a colour darker than black. The figure’s shoulders shook with sobs, but when it turned its head, the face was a smooth, blank oval, like an egg. From it came a voice that was both deep and high, a chord of grief. It said, “The basket is full. Who will carry the load?”

Isla woke at 4 a.m., her cheeks wet. She hadn’t wept in years. The room felt different. The shadows in the corner were not just absences of light, but presences of something else. She could almost see them… shimmer.

She got up, made tea, and watched the night fade. When the first grey light touched the tenements across the street, she shouldered her pack. She touched the camera, a hard, real shape under the cloth.

The concept had been planted. The maybe-woman was going to the Ford of the Maybe-People.

She locked her flat, a definitive click in the quiet hall. She was not certain of what she was doing. But she was, for the first time in a long time, in motion.

# Chapter 2

## **CHAPTER 2**

The train north was a worm of stale air and soft complaint. Isla watched the central belt’s urban clutter give way to the green ache of the Highlands. The map lay open on the tiny table, Finch’s frantic lines superimposed over the calm, authoritative greens and yellows of the Ordnance Survey. His ford was a ghost in the grid, a whisper in the contour lines. It existed in a blank space between two named lochs, a place the mapmakers had deemed unworthy of note.

A man in a cycling jersey sat opposite, his knees almost touching hers. He’d been trying to catch her eye since Perth.

“Walking holiday?” he asked, his voice too loud for the carriage.

“Research,” she said, not looking up.

“Ah! Biologist? Geologist?”

“Folklorist.”

His smile faltered, reconfigured into something patronising. “Ghosts and ghoulies, eh? Good luck with that.” He returned to his phone, the conversation deemed unproductive.

Isla traced the route with her finger. The train would take her as far as a village called Invermaire. From there, it was a fifteen-mile hike into a glen that, according to the library’s geology survey, was a glacial cul-de-sac, a place the ice had scooped out and forgotten.

She took out the Rolleiflex, feeling its weight in her hands. She aimed it out the window at a blur of heather-clad hillside, not to take a picture, but to see the world through its eyes. In the viewfinder, the rushing landscape was a silent, inverted ballet. It felt more real, somehow. Less cluttered with meaning.

At Invermaire, the station was a single platform and a rusting sign. The air was cold and clean, scoured by wind that smelled of pine and distant rain. The village was a clutch of whitewashed houses, a closed post office, and a pub called *The Stag’s Head*. It was the kind of place that seemed to be holding its breath.

She needed supplies. The pub was the only obvious source.

Inside, it was dark, the air peat-sweet from a smouldering fire. A few old men sat at the bar, their conversation a low murmur like river stones. They fell silent when she entered. The barman, a broad man with a beard that seemed to be devouring his lower face, nodded.

“Aye?”

“I’m after some things. Tinned food. Matches. Maybe a gas canister for a camp stove.”

He looked her up and down, her city jeans, her technical rucksack. “Walking?”

“Yes. Up towards Glen Sgàthach.”

The silence in the room thickened. One of the old men turned slowly on his stool. He had eyes the colour of old whisky, milky at the edges. “There’s nothing for you up that glen, lass.”

“Scenery,” she said, her voice sounding brittle to her own ears.

“Scenery,” the old man repeated, as if the word was a foreign currency of dubious value. “Aye. It’s scenic enough. It’s also… *faoin*.”

“Faoin?”

“Futile. Empty. A place that leads nowhere.” He took a slow sip of his pint. “The path’s gone. Sheep took it years ago. You’ll be climbing over bracken and bog. And the midges… they’ll eat you alive.”

“I’ll manage.”

The barman shrugged, a massive movement of shoulders. “I’ve got tins. No gas. You’d need to go back to Fort William for that.”

She bought what he had: tinned stew, beans, a box of damp matches. As he bagged them, he spoke without looking at her. “You’ll see a bothy. A shepherd’s hut. By the lochan at the head of the glen. Roof’s half gone. But it’s stone. It’ll keep the worst of the weather off. Better than a tent in that place.”

“Thank you.”

He met her eyes then. “If you hear singing by the water… don’t answer it. It’s not asking for you.”

A chill, separate from the damp of the pub, touched her. “What kind of singing?”

But he had turned away, wiping the bar with a cloth. The conversation was over.

The old man watched her leave, his milky eyes unreadable.

The track out of the village was a muddy rut between drystone dykes. It climbed steadily, and within an hour, Invermaire was a smudge of white in the valley below, and the silence was complete. It was not a peaceful quiet. It was an attentive one. The wind hushed through the grass. A grouse clattered out of the heather, making her heart slam against her ribs. Her boots were already soaked from crossing a dozen seeping burns.

Finch’s map was infuriatingly vague. It relied on landmarks that might have changed in two centuries: a split rock, a rowan tree struck by lightning, a bend in the river where the water ran “backwards over its own reflection”. She found the split rock, a giant slab of granite riven down the middle as if by a giant’s axe. It felt ominous, a doorway to nothing.

By late afternoon, the weather turned. A mist, fine and cold, descended from the higher peaks, blurring the world into shades of grey. The path, as predicted, vanished into a slope of tussocky grass and hidden holes. Her progress slowed to a clumsy, exhausting slog. The rucksack, which had felt manageable in the station, now seemed filled with lead weights and regret.

And the midges found her. A cloud of tiny, insistent agony, swarming her face, her ears, getting into her eyes and mouth. She smeared on repellent, but it was like trying to hold back the sea with a sponge. She pulled up her hood, tied a buff over her face, and stumbled on, a miserable, anonymous creature in the fog.

Just as the light was beginning to fail, the ground levelled. The mist thinned for a moment, and she saw it. A small, dark lochan, its surface like polished lead. And beside it, a low, rectangular shape: the bothy.

It was more ruin than shelter. One whole corner of the roof had collapsed, and the door was a rotten plank hanging from one hinge. But it was stone, and it had four walls. She pushed inside. The floor was dirt and old sheep droppings. The air was cold and smelled of decay and wet wool. But it was a refuge.

She cleared a space by the intact wall, using a piece of the fallen roof to sweep away the worst of the detritus. She was too tired to light the camp stove, too tired to eat. She drank some water, wrapped herself in her sleeping bag, and leaned against the cold stone. The mist pressed against the empty window hole, a swirling, silent white.

This was folly. Academic suicide. She was sitting in a ruined hut, being eaten by insects, chasing a senile professor’s delusion for a thesis chapter that would be laughed out of the examination room. She thought of her flat, her books, the electric kettle, the certain, solid world of footnotes and bibliographies.

A sound cut through the self-pity.

It was faint, carried on the damp air. Not singing. Not quite. A rhythmic, low-pitched *keening*. A sound of profound, unassuageable sorrow. It came from the direction of the lochan.

Isla froze, her breath held. Morag’s words: *If you hear singing by the water… don’t answer it.* This wasn’t singing. It was worse. It was the sound of a heart breaking, over and over.

Against every instinct of modern rationality, she found herself moving. She unzipped the sleeping bag, the cold biting instantly. She crept to the doorway of the bothy, peering out into the gloom.

The mist had lifted slightly over the water. On the near shore, a figure knelt.

It was androgynous, wrapped in what looked like a heavy, sodden plaid. Its head was bowed, and its shoulders moved with the rhythm of the keening. In front of it, in the peaty shallows of the lochan, was a pile of dark cloth. The figure’s hands worked at it, scrubbing, wringing. The water that dripped from the cloth was not clear. Even in the fading light, Isla could see it was dark, like ink, or blood.

The *Bean Nighe*. Here. Not at a ford, but at the source.

Isla’s hand went to the camera around her neck. This was it. The proof. The tangible thing. Her fingers were numb with cold and fear as she lifted the viewfinder to her eye.

The world flipped. The grey lochan, the dark figure, the weeping sky—all composed themselves into a perfect, inverted rectangle of grief. Her thumb found the shutter release. She took a breath, held it.

*Click.*

The sound was absurdly loud in the vast, silent landscape.

The keening stopped.

The figure’s hands stilled. Slowly, with a terrible, deliberate grace, it began to turn its head towards the bothy.

Isla didn’t wait to see its face. She dropped the camera, letting it swing against her chest, and scrambled back into the darkest corner of the hut, pressing herself against the cold stone. She clamped a hand over her own mouth to silence her breathing, which was coming in ragged, panicked gasps.

Minutes passed. An eternity. She heard nothing but the pulse of blood in her ears.

Then, a soft, wet sound at the doorway. A presence, blocking what little light remained.

Isla squeezed her eyes shut, a child’s logic: if I can’t see it, it can’t see me.

A voice filled the bothy. It was the voice from her dream—a impossible chord, a bass note of mountain and a treble note of wind through reeds. It spoke in Gaelic, then seemed to remember itself, switching to English with a soft, sibilant accent.

“You took a piece of the sorrow.”

Isla said nothing. She couldn’t have spoken if she tried.

“The old man with the map. He sent you. The one who thinks in layers.” The voice held no malice. Only a deep, weary curiosity. “He thinks I am a custodian. A symbol. He is… not wrong. But he is not right. I am the wash. And the water. And the cloth. And the stain.”

Isla forced her eyes open.

The figure stood in the doorway, silhouetted. It was not monstrous. It was blurred, as if seen through a rain-streaked window. Its features shifted subtly, never settling. For a moment, the line of a jaw seemed strong, masculine. Then it softened into a curve of cheek, feminine. Its height seemed to change. The only constant was the profound, radiating sadness.

“Why are you here?” Isla whispered, the words tearing from her throat.

“The basket is full,” it said, echoing her dream. “The old patterns are stained through. They will not come clean. The men will not die neatly. The women will not weep correctly. The roles are tangled. The threads are crossed.” It took a step inside. The air grew colder, heavier, smelling of damp earth and ozone. “The ford is choked with unfinished business. Maybe-men. Perhaps-women. They cannot cross. They linger. They… shimmer.”

Isla thought of Finch’s words. *A traffic jam in the metaphysics.*

“What do you do?”

“I wash. I weep. I try to separate the threads. But the solvent… my tears… they are changing. They used to wash away the mortal stain, to prepare the soul for the next shape. Now… they sometimes mix with the stain. Create new colours. New… possibilities.” The figure seemed to look down at its own hands. “I am becoming uncertain.”

The statement hung in the cold air. A cosmic horror, delivered with simple, devastating fatigue.

“What happens if you… become uncertain?”

“The ford becomes a mirror. It reflects back what you bring to it. Not what you are, but what you *might* be. The unbearable potential. For most, it is madness. For a few… it is a door.” It tilted its head, the shifting face regarding her. “You are unfinished. A maybe-woman. You feel it. The old man knew. That is why he sent you. You are a question walking towards a question. It is… polite.”

Isla found a sliver of courage, the academic part reasserting itself. “What is the question?”

The figure was silent for a long time. “*An e fir no boireannach a th’ ann?* That is the old question. The simple one. The new question is… louder. What happens when ‘either/or’ drowns in the ford? What is born in the ‘both/and’? What world grows in the space between the threads?” It took a step back towards the door. “Stay until dawn. Do not follow me to the water. The reflection there is… acute.”

“Wait,” Isla said, pushing herself up. “What’s your name?”

The figure paused. “I was called *Màili* when I was a woman who died in childbirth. I was called *Coinneach* when I was a man who drowned saving a stranger. Now, I am the one who washes. A verb. Not a noun.” It gestured towards the lochan with a dripping hand. “The ford is a day’s walk north, following the stream from this lochan. You will know it. The water runs backwards over its own reflection. Go there at noon, when the sun is highest and the shadows are shortest. The light will show you the truth. Or it will break you.”

Then it was gone, melting into the mist as if it had never been.

Isla slumped against the wall, trembling violently, not from cold, but from a seismic shift in her understanding of reality. She had spoken to a myth. And the myth was having an existential crisis.

She fumbled for her torch, her hands shaking. She shone it around the empty bothy, then on the ground where the figure had stood. The dirt floor was wet. Not with water, but with a dark, iridescent patch, like a spill of oil and pearl.

She took the camera. Her hands steadied as she wound the film to the next frame. She focused on the patch of wet, alien earth, and took another picture.

*Click.*

The sound was a period at the end of a sentence. The first chapter of her old life was closed. She was in the story now, up to her neck. And the water, she knew, was much, much deeper than she had ever imagined.

She spent the night awake, back against the stone, the knife with her blood on it held tight in her hand. The keening did not return. Only the wind, and the vast, listening silence of the glen, and the terrifying, exhilarating sense that all the layers of the world had just grown thin as gauze.

# Chapter 3

## **CHAPTER 3**

Dawn came as a slow dilution of grey. Isla emerged from the bothy stiff and soul-weary, but crackling with a wired, unnatural alertness. The world looked different. Not transformed, but *revealed*. The grass wasn’t just green; it was a thousand shifting shades between yellow and blue, each blade holding a minute bead of mist-light. The lochan was no longer leaden but a deep, swirling violet-black, like a bruise on the skin of the earth. She could see the shimmer.

It wasn’t a visual effect. It was a cognitive one—a sense of latent meaning, of stories trapped in the landscape, pressing to get out. The split rock on the hillside now seemed less a geological accident and more a deliberate punctuation mark in a sentence written in stone.

She ate a cold tin of beans, the taste of tin and salt a grounding banality. Her encounter felt like a dream, but the memory was crystalline, and the dark, iridescent stain on the bothy floor was still there, though it seemed to be fading, soaking into the earth like a secret.

Following the stream from the lochan was easy. It was a cheerful, chattering thing, oblivious to the metaphysical weight it carried towards the ford. The walking was harder, through pathless, boggy ground that sucked at her boots. The midges were mercifully absent, chased away by a cool breeze, but the air remained close, thick with the smell of wet peat and something else—a metallic tang, like ozone after lightning.

Finch’s map became slightly more coherent as she walked. The “rowan struck by lightning” was there, a blackened skeleton of a tree, one bright red berry cluster clinging to a dead branch like a defiant drop of blood. She passed it, feeling a superstitious urge to touch the wood for luck, but resisted.

The valley narrowed, the hills crowding in on either side, steep and scree-strewn. The sound of the stream grew louder, bouncing off the rock. And then, around a final, jutting bend, she saw it.

*Linne nan Daoine Sgàthach.*

It was not dramatic. A widening in the stream, perhaps twenty feet across, where the water slowed and deepened over a bed of smooth, multi-coloured stones. The banks were flat, grassy. A perfectly pleasant spot for a picnic, if not for the atmosphere.

The air here was dead still. The sound of the stream seemed muted, as if heard through glass. And the light was wrong. The sun, nearing its zenith, should have been throwing sharp, short shadows. Instead, the light was diffuse, directionless, coating everything in a flat, pearlescent sheen. It made the world look two-dimensional, a painted backdrop.

And then she saw the water.

Finch’s phrase had been precise. The water ran backwards over its own reflection. It was impossible, a glitch in reality. The stream flowed left to right, as water should. But on the surface, the reflection of the opposite bank—the heather, the rocks, the sky—flowed *right to left*, a perfect, contrary mirror. The effect was nauseating, a visual contradiction that made her brain itch. It was like watching a film where the actor’s lips moved out of sync with the words.

This was the place. The nexus. The clogged drain of the symbolic world.

She stood on the bank, well back from the edge, and simply watched. The longer she looked, the more she saw. Or felt. Flickers at the corner of her vision—shapes that weren’t there when she turned her head. A sense of being watched from multiple points, none of them visible. A low hum, not a sound, but a vibration in her teeth.

She unslung the Rolleiflex. Through the viewfinder, the ford was even more unsettling. The inverted, reversed image somehow made the backwards flow of the reflection seem *more* logical. She took several pictures, the *click-whirr* of the advance lever absurdly profane in the holy silence.

What now? The washer-being had said to come at noon. The sun was almost directly overhead. Was she supposed to… cross? To step into that contradictory water?

A movement on the far bank caught her eye.

A figure emerged from the heather. It was a man, or the shape of one. He was dressed in walking gear—a bright blue waterproof, sturdy boots—but they looked wrong, ill-fitting, as if he’d borrowed them from a larger person. His face was pale, strained. He stood at the edge of the far bank, staring into the water with a look of profound confusion.

He didn’t seem to see her.

He took a step into the ford. The water swirled around his ankles. He flinched, looked down, and his mouth opened in a silent cry of shock. Isla followed his gaze.

Where his boots met the water, the reflection wasn’t of his legs. It was the reflection of a woman’s bare feet, pale and slender, disappearing into the dark water.

The man stared, frozen, at this impossible reflection. He raised his hands. In the water, the woman’s hands rose to meet them. He turned his head to the side. The woman in the water turned her head the opposite way.

He was looking at his *potential*. The other thread in the tangled skein of himself. The maybe-woman he also was.

He began to shake his head, a frantic, denialist motion. *No, no, no.* The reflection mimicked the gesture, but its face held an expression not of denial, but of sad recognition.

Then he did the thing she feared. He looked up, and his eyes met Isla’s across the ford.

He saw her. Really saw her. His expression shifted from personal horror to a desperate, pleading hope. “You!” he called out, his voice cracking. “You see it, don’t you? Tell me you see it! Tell me I’m not mad!”

Isla said nothing. Her role was to observe. To document. Not to intervene.

“It’s in the water!” he screamed, pointing down. “It’s me, but it’s *not* me! What is this place?!”

He took another stumbling step forward, deeper into the ford, now up to his knees. The reflection deepened accordingly. The woman in the water was now visible to her thighs, wearing what looked like a simple, dark dress. The man’s movements became jerky, uncoordinated, as if he was fighting his own body. The reflection’s movements were fluid, graceful.

“I can feel it…” he moaned. “The pull… it wants me to… to *be* that…”

He was breaking. The unbearable potential was unmaking him.

Isla forgot her mandate. She was a human being before she was a folklorist. “Get out!” she shouted, her voice startlingly loud in the muffled air. “Come back to this side! Don’t look at it!”

He focused on her, a lifeline. “Help me.”

He tried to turn, to wade back to his own bank. But his legs wouldn’t obey. They remained locked, knee-deep in the contradictory stream. He struggled, panicked, thrashing. The water churned, but the reflection remained calm, serene, watching him with its sad, knowing eyes.

“I can’t… it won’t let go…”

From upstream, a new sound cut through his panic. The keening. Low, sorrowful, inevitable.

The washer-being was coming.

The man heard it too. His head snapped towards the sound, upstream on his side of the bank. His face drained of all remaining colour. “No… no, not that… I’m not ready… I’m not finished!”

The keening grew louder. And



---

# Chapter 4

## **CHAPTER 4**

The city’s old quarter smelled of wet stone and forgotten purposes. Leo’s footsteps echoed in the close, a frantic counter-rhythm to the thrumming in his veins. The explorer’s notes, written in a tight, anxious script, had led him here: *‘For corroboration, seek the Silent Chapter, Catheart’s Wynd. Ask for nothing. She will know if you are worth the knowing.’* It was less an address and more a koan, and Leo was in no state for philosophy. He was in a state for answers that didn’t dissolve upon examination, for solid things in a world that had turned to peat-smoke.

Catheart’s Wynd was a gullet between two towering tenements, so narrow the sky was a bruised slit overhead. The shops here were the kind that sold things nobody needed anymore: watch parts for timepieces long shattered, single buttons, faded postcards of a city that had erased itself. Desperation, Leo was learning, had its own taxonomy. The frantic, shrieking kind he’d felt at the ford was one species. This, the slow, sinking kind that came from hunting a ghost through the rain, was another.

He found it tucked where the wynd made a dog’s-leg turn, a window so grime-caked it seemed a deliberate occlusion. Behind the glass, a vague tumble of leather spines and faded gilt. No sign. The door was black oak, scarred by generations of damp, its brass knob green with verdigris. It looked less like a shop and more like a door that had been left behind when the world moved on.

He pushed it open. A bell, high and thin as a toothache, chimed somewhere in the depths.

The air inside was a physical presence. It was the scent of paper decaying with dignity, of old leather holding its shape against time, of dust that had settled into permanence. It was silence given a smell. The shop was a canyon of books, shelves rising into a gloom where the ceiling was lost. Volumes were stacked in leaning towers on the floor, crammed into every crevice, a geography of imminent collapse. Light came from a single, green-shaded banker’s lamp on a vast, claw-footed desk at the room’s heart.

Leo stood just inside the threshold, dripping onto a threadbare rug that depicted some heraldic beast being quietly consumed by moths. He felt assessed, not by a person, but by the accumulated weight of all the unread words.

“You’re letting the damp in.” The voice came from behind a fortress of folios to his right. It was a dry voice, papery, without warmth but without malice. A statement of fact.

He shut the door, and the silence sealed itself again.

From behind the book-fort emerged a woman. She was perhaps sixty, perhaps a hundred; it was impossible to tell in the kind light of decay. Her hair was a long, silver braid over one shoulder, severe as a cable. She wore a high-necked dress of charcoal-grey wool, utterly plain, and her eyes were the colour of flint. She held a small, brass-bound volume in one hand, a feather duster in the other, an absurd juxtaposition. She did not smile.

“I’m looking for…” Leo began, the prepared lie dying on his tongue.

“You are looking,” she interrupted, setting the duster aside with a precision that suggested it was a ceremonial act, “for confirmation that you haven’t lost your mind. A common enough request. The stock is depleted.” Her gaze travelled over him, not like a shopkeeper sizing up a customer, but like a cartographer noting erosion on a familiar coast. It lingered on the bulge in his waxed jacket pocket, where the compass lay against his chest. “You’ve brought something with you. Something that doesn’t belong to you.”

It wasn’t a question. Leo’s hand went to his pocket, protective. “It was left to me.”

“By Alistair Foyle.” She said the name not as a question, but as a verdict. She turned and walked towards the desk, her movements fluid and silent. “He was a regular customer. A man who asked better questions than most, which is why he found better answers. And worse trouble. Come here.”

Leo approached the desk. It was a landscape of organised chaos: sheaves of parchment weighted with smooth river stones, inkwells that looked like they held dried blood, a magnifying glass with a handle of yellowed bone. She did not sit, but stood behind it, a commander at a map-table.

“Show me.”

He hesitated, then drew the compass from his pocket. In the gloom of the shop, the brass seemed to drink the lamplight, glowing with a subdued, inner warmth. He placed it on the blotter between them.

The woman—Elara, he presumed, though no names had been offered—did not touch it. She leaned forward, her flinty eyes narrowing. She didn’t look at the face, or the needle. She looked at the space around it, as if reading its aura. After a long moment, she let out a breath, a soft exhalation that was the first human sound she’d made.

“A Key,” she said, the word capitalised by her tone. “And not a minor one. Foyle had no business carrying this. No more than a child has business carrying a lit torch into a powder magazine.”

“A key to what?” Leo’s voice was barely a whisper, afraid to disturb the gravity in the room.

“To the cracks,” she said, looking up at him. “To the places where the wallpaper of our world has peeled back. You’ve seen one, haven’t you? A crack. A… thin place.”

The ford. The screaming man. The thing in the water. The keening. The memory was a shard of ice in his gut. He nodded, once.

“And you used this.” She gestured to the compass. “Or it used you. They have a sympathy for the turmoil they cause. It pointed you true, I assume. They always point towards the disturbance. It’s their function.”

“What is it?” Leo asked, his hand hovering over the compass, wanting to reclaim it.

“It is a piece of a world that isn’t yours,” Elara said bluntly. “A piece of the Other Side. The world behind the Veil.”

The word hung in the dusty air. *Veil*. It felt right, a word for the fragile film that had seemingly torn at the ford.

“The Veil is… what? A border?”

“A membrane. A story we tell ourselves to make the chaos bearable.” She finally moved, circling the desk to stand closer to him. She was taller than he’d realised. “On one side, here. The rational, the solid, the *explained*. On the other… everything else. The old things. The potential that didn’t make the cut. The echoes of choices unmade. The folklore your academic is so fond of documenting isn’t fiction, Mr.…?”

“Leo. Just Leo.”

“Leo. It isn’t fiction. It’s leakage. Reportage from the front lines of a perpetual, quiet war.” She picked up a river stone from the desk, hefting its cool weight. “This world, our world, is under a kind of siege. Not by armies. By… influence. By reflection. The Veil is thinning. Has been for centuries. And there are those who work to keep it patched, and those who work to rip it wide open.”

Leo’s mind raced, trying to fit the impossible into the frame of the real. “Who? Why?”

“The Wardens,” she said, with a faint, grudging respect. “A scattered, secretive lot. They maintain the old protections, reinforce the weak points. They understand that some mysteries should remain mysterious. That a door exists for a reason: sometimes to be opened, but often to be kept shut.” Her flinty eyes hardened. “And then there are the Harvesters. They see the Veil not as a protection, but as a prison. They believe the power, the pure potential, from the Other Side should be theirs to wield. They pick at the threads. They create… incidents. Like your ford.”

“The man… the reflection…”

“A Harvester’s work,” Elara said, her voice cold. “A deliberate thinning. They create a resonance, a sympathetic wound, to draw the potential through. What happens to the poor soul caught in it is collateral damage. The reflection he saw? That was a potential self, a life he could have lived, bubbling up from the other side. Most people can’t hold that contradiction. It unmakes them. The washer-being… that’s a Warden artifact. A cleaner. It comes to sever the connection, to tidy up the mess. Often, it tidies up the witness, too.”

Leo remembered the keening, the unbearable sorrow in it. A cleaner. The thought was more chilling than any monster.

“Why tell me this?” he asked, suspicion flaring. “Why not just call the… cleaners?”

“Because Foyle gave you the Key,” she said, as if it were obvious. “And because you saw the crack and didn’t immediately go mad. You have a certain… permeability. Rare, and usually unfortunate.” She looked at him, and for the first time, he saw something like curiosity in her face. “And because the Wardens are losing. They are archivists in a fire. The Harvesters are aggressive, organised. They have a taste for the power they steal, and appetite only grows. Your ford was a minor event. A test. There will be more. Bigger.”

She walked back to the shelves, running a finger along a row of spines. “Foyle was tracking them. He believed they were converging on something here, in the city. A weak point not just in space, but in time. A confluence. He was using the Key to map their activity.” She turned. “He is gone. The Key is now active for you. Which means you are now a point of interest. For both sides.”

The weight of it settled on Leo, heavier than the damp in his jacket. He was a man who fixed boilers, who understood pipes and pressure and the tangible. This was a leak in reality itself. “What do I do?”

“First,” Elara said, a hint of a pragmatic, grim teacher in her tone, “you learn to see. Properly. The Key is a crutch. It points, but it doesn’t interpret. You’re blundering about half-blind. In this work, that gets you harvested.” She gestured for him to follow her to a clearer space of floor between the book-towers. “The Veil isn’t a wall. It’s a weave. And where it thins, the light from the other side… leaks. It has a texture. A taste to the mind. You felt it at the ford. That pressure. That sense of *wrongness*.”

He had. The air had been thick with it.

“I want you to close your eyes,” she instructed. “And forget what you know about seeing.”

It felt absurd. But the authority in her voice was absolute. Leo closed his eyes. The darkness was immediate, punctuated by the afterglow of the green lamp.

“Breathe. Listen to the silence here. It’s not true silence. It’s layered. The dust settling. The pages contracting. The building’s old bones settling. Now… feel for the silence beneath that. The one that doesn’t belong.”

He tried. He heard his own heartbeat, the distant drip of a gutter. The dense, woolly quiet of the book-choked room. He strained, feeling foolish.

“Stop trying,” Elara’s voice cut in, gentle for the first time. “It’s not a search. It’s a surrender. Let the room come to you. The way you might notice a change in the hum of a machine you know well.”

He let his focus soften. Let the sounds wash over him without grabbing at them. He breathed in the smell of decay and memory. And then, at the very edge of his perception, he felt it. Not a sound, but a… vibration. A faint, almost musical dissonance, like a single note from a broken piano string sustained underneath everything. It came from the far corner of the shop, behind a tapestry that depicted a tree with black, barren branches.

“There,” he said, opening his eyes and pointing. “A kind of… hum. Sour.”

Elara’s eyebrows lifted a millimetre. Approval. “Good. That is a threadbare spot. A place where, long ago, someone tried to read something best left unread. The psychic stain remains.” She walked to the corner and pulled the tapestry aside. On the stone wall behind it was a dark, water-stain-like patch. But as Leo stared, letting his eyes lose focus, he saw it wasn’t a stain at all. It was a faint, shimmering tracery, like heat-haze, emitting that same subtle, wrong vibration. It was beautiful and sickening.

“This is nothing,” she said, dropping the tapestry. “A scar. The active leaks are brighter. Hungrier. They pull at you. The Key will scream for them. But you must learn to feel them without it. To know the landscape. A Harvester can mask a Key’s reaction. They cannot mask the leak itself to a trained sense.”

For hours, it felt like, she put him through his paces. She had him find other faint, old thin spots in the shop—a corner where a melancholy poet had spent his final years, a patch of floor where a dangerous book had been burned. Each had its own signature: a cold spot, a smell of ozone, a feeling of vertigo. She was a brutal, efficient tutor, offering no praise beyond a curt “adequate” when he correctly identified a subtle, whispering drain of energy near the door.

“The world is riddled with these,” she said finally, as the single lamp seemed to burn brighter in the deepening gloom of late afternoon. “Most are inert. Historical. But new ones are opening. Faster. The Harvesters are not just exploiting weak points; they are creating them. They use human pain as a chisel. Betrayal, grief, madness—these emotions resonate powerfully with the Other Side. They thin the Veil like acid.”

Leo thought of the man at the ford, his personal horror. Had that been natural? Or had it been *cultivated*?

“Why are you telling me all this?” he asked again, exhaustion making him bold. “You’re not a Warden. You’re a bookseller.”

For the first time, Elara smiled. It was a thin, sharp thing, like a paper-cut. “I am a librarian. The Silent Chapter is an archive of the conflict. I preserve the records of both sides. Knowledge is the only weapon that doesn’t blunt. Foyle was a researcher. He is gone. You have his Key. You have the sensitivity. You are, whether you wish it or not, the next data point.” She went to her desk, opened a drawer, and pulled out a small, cloth-wrapped bundle. “Take this.”

He unwrapped it. Inside was a notebook, very old, bound in dark leather. And a pen with a nib of a strange, dark metal.

“Foyle’s field journal. Blank from the midpoint. Where he stopped writing. Or where he was stopped. The pen’s nib is iron-alloy. It will write true, even near strong influences. Your assignment, Leo, is to become a better folklorist than the academic at the ford. Document what you find. Follow the Key. Map the new thin places. Not to intervene. To *witness*. That is the first, and only, rule of the archivist. We observe. We record. We do not get involved.”

“Isla,” Leo said, the name coming out unbidden.

Elara froze. “You know her?”

“I saw her. At the ford. She was watching. She didn’t help.”

“Isla MacLeod,” Elara said, and the name was a sigh. “A gifted observer. A frustratingly rigid student. She follows the old Warden protocols to the letter: witness, document, withdraw. A cleaner was dispatched. The incident is contained. In her view, the man was already lost the moment he saw his reflection.” She fixed Leo with her flinty gaze. “Do not seek her out. Her allegiance is to preservation, not understanding. She would take the Key from you and shut you out for your own good. And her definition of ‘good’ is very, very narrow.”

Leo tucked the journal and pen into his inner pocket. The compass, he returned to its place over his heart. It felt warmer than before. “What do I do now?”

“Go home. Sleep. If you can. The seeing will leave you raw. Tomorrow, start small. Walk your city. Don’t use the Key. Just feel. Listen for the sour notes. Note where the streetlights flicker for no reason, where the shadows don’t match the light, where a feeling of dread pools in a seemingly ordinary alley. Map the cracks in your mind.” She turned back to her desk, dismissal in her posture. “The Silent Chapter is always here. But do not come often. Do not be followed.”

Leo hesitated at the door. The world outside seemed louder, brighter, more fragile than when he’d entered. “Thank you,” he said, though he wasn’t sure what he was thanking her for.

“Don’t thank me,” she said without looking up. “I have just handed you a shovel and pointed you at a minefield. Gratitude is premature.” She glanced at him, a final, assessing look. “And Leo? Trust the feeling in your gut before the thought in your head. The old brain, the limbic sludge, it remembers the Veil is a lie long before the clever part catches up.”

He stepped back into the wynd. The drizzle had hardened into a proper rain, needling his face. The door closed behind him with a soft, final click, and the bell did not chime. He looked at the grimy window, but could see no movement within. The shop might have been empty for a century.

He walked, aiming for the main thoroughfare and the bus back to his flat, to his life of boilers and tangible problems. But he tried to do as Elara said. He softened his gaze. He let the city in.

And he heard them. Faint, but unmistakable.

A low thrum of anxiety from the mouth of a subway station, where a thousand lonely journeys crossed. A cold, sucking emptiness from the doorway of a shuttered charity shop. A shimmer, like a mirage, around the old, condemned church on the corner, where the stone seemed to weep even in the dry. They were not events, not like the ford. They were scars, stains, whispers. But they were everywhere. His city was not a solid thing. It was a lacework, worn thin in countless places, and through the holes he could now sense the constant, whispering draft from the other side.

He fumbled for his keys at the door to his tenement, his hands unsteady. The familiar lock, the scuffed linoleum of the stair—they felt like a stage set, flimsy props against the vast, humming darkness he now knew lay behind them. He climbed to his flat, shut the door, and leaned against it, breathing hard.

In the quiet of his own home, he took out Foyle’s journal. He opened it to the last written page. The handwriting, once tight and controlled, degenerated into a frantic scrawl.

*‘They are not just making cracks. They are making a pattern. A sigil. The city itself is the ritual site. The confluence approaches. The Heart of the Veil is here. They will tear it open and all the old night will come pouring in. I have seen the design in the—’*

The sentence ended in a long, ink-blotted dash, as if the pen had been snatched from his hand.

Leo slowly closed the book. He looked out his window at the glittering, rainy cityscape, the streetlights smearing gold on the wet pavement. A pattern. A sigil. The city itself.

He understood Elara’s final warning now. He wasn’t just holding a key. He was standing on the diagram. And somewhere out there in the damp, gleaming night, Isla MacLeod was watching, documenting, waiting for the cleaners to be dispatched. And others, with a taste for stolen power, were drawing their lines, using human pain as ink, preparing to tear the world he knew right down the middle.

The compass against his chest gave a single, distinct pulse, like a dying man’s heartbeat. It was not pointing anywhere. It was trembling.



---

# Chapter 5

## **CHAPTER 5**

The rain had settled into a fine, persistent mist, the kind that didn’t fall so much as hang in the air, beading on wool and stone alike. Inside *The Cracked Spine*, the world was reduced to the smell of old paper, damp earth from a potted fern that was losing its fight for life, and the sharp, coppery scent of Leo’s fear. It clung to the back of his throat, a taste more potent than Morag’s whisky.

Elara was a statue by the grimy front window, her fingers barely parting the lace curtain. “Two of them,” she said, her voice a low filament in the quiet. “By the newsagent’s. They’re not even pretending to look at the headlines.”

Leo didn’t need to ask who. The Harvesters. The name felt wrong in his mind, too clean, too agricultural. It suggested a seasonal, natural thing. There was nothing natural about the two figures he’d glimpsed. They wore the city like a cheap suit—standard-issue anoraks, sensible shoes—but they didn’t *fit*. It was in the stillness, the way their gazes swept the street not with curiosity, but with the methodical, blank assessment of a scanner. They were looking for a barcode. His.

“The back?” he whispered, the words dry as tomb dust.

“Watched. They’ve triangulated. They know we’re in the honey pot.” Elara let the curtain fall. She turned, and in the grey light, her face was all planes and shadows, a cliff-face of resolve. “They’ll come in quiet. Try to contain. Minimise the… mess.”

“Mess.” Leo’s laugh was a short, sharp exhalation. He thought of Foyle’s journal, the ink-blotted dash. A different kind of mess. “What do they want? The compass? The journal?”

“The anomaly,” she said, moving past him, her hand brushing a shelf of leather-bound sermons. A faint shimmer, like heat haze, followed her touch. “You. Me. The knowledge. All of it. They tidy up loose threads.”

The bell above the shop door jingled, a sound usually as rare and precious as a gold coin in here. Now, it was the pin-pull of a grenade.

A man entered. He was mid-forties, wearing a NHS-style fleece under his open anorak, a lanyard tucked into his pocket. He could have been a council surveyor, a social worker. He smiled, a bland, professional flex of the lips that didn’t touch his eyes. They were the colour of a frozen puddle.

“Afternoon,” the man said. “Terrible weather. Looking for a local history, something on the old guilds?” His voice was perfectly pitched, a practised, harmless monotone.

Leo’s mouth was desert. He saw the second figure slip in behind the first, a woman with a severe, efficient bob and a large canvas tote bag. She closed the door softly. The *click* of the latch was final.

Elara didn’t bother with the performance. “You’re a long way from your chapter house, Harvester. The air here must taste sour to you.”

The man’s smile solidified, became a thing of porcelain. The woman set her tote bag gently on the floor. From it, she drew not a weapon, but a small, polished brass bowl and a bundle of what looked like grey, knotted twine.

“The artefact and the aberration,” the man said, his gaze settling on Leo. “Standard recall. No need for fuss. You’ll come to no lasting harm.” The promise was colder than a threat.

“Aberration,” Leo echoed. The word buzzed in his skull. A flaw. A glitch. Isla MacLeod’s voice ghosted through his memory: *They clean up the mess.*

Elara took a step forward, placing herself between the Harvesters and Leo. It was a small movement, almost casual, but it altered the geometry of the room. “You use your little tools to skim the surface scum,” she said, her voice gaining a melodic, dangerous edge. “You’ve never had the stomach to dive into the deeps. You don’t know what you’re trying to contain.”

The woman began unknotting the twine, her fingers moving with swift, ritualistic precision. She hummed a low, atonal note that made Leo’s teeth ache.

“Last chance,” the man said, his hand dipping into his fleece pocket.

Elara moved. It wasn’t an attack, not in any way Leo understood. She swept her arm in a wide, graceful arc, as if gathering cobwebs from the air. But what she gathered was the *potential* of the place—the latent energy of ten thousand unread stories, the quiet desperation of Foyle’s final days seeping from the walls, the focused, terrified concentration Leo was radiating like a fever.

The air in front of her rippled, then *sheared*.

It was a soundless, shivering tear. The space between her and the Harvesters didn’t explode; it *unfolded*. The world there became a kaleidoscope of fractured perspectives—Leo saw the man’s frozen smile from three angles at once, saw the spines of books behind him jutting into the space like broken teeth, saw the woman’s humming face superimposed over a flash of the rainy street outside. It was a localized concussion of reality.

The Harvester man staggered back, a line of blood trickling from his nose as if the pressure inside him had been forced out. The woman dropped her twine, her atonal hum cutting off in a gasp. The brass bowl rang like a bell where it hit the floor.

“Run,” Elara said, and the word was a whip-crack.

Leo ran. He bolted for the narrow passage behind the counter that led to the back storeroom and the rear exit. He heard not a bang, but a wet, *unzipping* sound, and a shelf of Victorian encyclopaedias to his left simply ceased to be, replaced by a brief, terrifying glimpse of a swirling, starless violet void before the space snapped back, books raining down as pulp and splintered wood.

The woman was chanting now, real words in a guttural tongue. The air grew thick, resistant. Leo’s legs felt like he was pushing through tar. He glanced back.

Elara stood her ground, her hands weaving patterns that left after-images of silver light. She was pulling threads from the very fabric of the shop, stitching together a shield of fragmented history and forgotten prose. A projectile of condensed, humming silence launched from the male Harvester’s hand. It didn’t fly; it *appeared*, eating the distance instantly. It struck Elara’s makeshift shield.

The sound was the death-cry of a hundred libraries. A shockwave of silent force, visible as a distorting bubble, radiated outwards. It hit the walls. The brick didn’t crack. It *shimmered*, turning briefly translucent, revealing the skeletons of pipes and lathe behind it before solidifying again with a deep, groaning ache. Every pane of glass in the front window webbed into a million fractures, holding its shape for a surreal second before collapsing inward in a slow, glittering cascade.

The Harvester woman had her twine now, stretched between her hands. It was vibrating, emitting a high-pitched whine that sought to cancel out Elara’s weaving. The conflict wasn’t fire and lightning. It was erasure versus creation, a silent, brutal war over what was *allowed* to be real.

Leo hit the back door, shoulder-charging it open into a dank, brick-lined close. The misty air was a slap of cold relief. He turned, shouting for Elara.

She emerged backwards, still facing the ruin of the shop. Her expression was ferocious, a mask of effort, but as she crossed the threshold, she flung a final, contemptuous gesture behind her. It was a dismissal, a sealing.

Inside *The Cracked Spine*, the two Harvesters were converging. The man raised both hands. The woman’s twine glowed white-hot.

Then the world *folded*.

It was as if a giant, invisible hand took the interior of the shop and crumpled it like a sheet of paper. Space compressed, walls met in impossible geometries, the ceiling kissed the floor. It happened in utter silence, a breathtaking, nauseating contraction. For one heartbeat, the shop was a condensed cube of nightmare geometry. Then it *unfolded* again with a deep, sub-audible **thump** that Leo felt in his marrow.

What was left was not rubble. It was a perfect, empty shell. The books, the shelves, the desk, the Harvesters—all of it was gone. Just bare, dusty floorboards, exposed brick, and the gaping maw where the window had been. A smell of ozone and burnt vanilla lingered, the ghost of a spell.

Elara sagged against the close wall, her breath coming in ragged gulps. Sweat plastered dark hair to her temples. “Tidy,” she spat, the word dripping with venom. “They love things tidy. So I gave them… nothing.”

Leo stared at the void where his sanctuary had been. His past, his profession, his last tether to a normal life—sublimated into the aether. There was no anger yet, only a hollow, ringing shock. He fumbled inside his jacket, his fingers closing around the cold brass of the compass. It was bucking in his hand, a live fish desperate for the stream.

“Which way?” he demanded, his own voice strange to him.

Elara pushed off the wall, her eyes on the compass face. The needle was spinning, then catching, then spinning again, as if equally repelled and attracted by everything around them. “They’ve stirred the currents. It’s confused. But the thin places… they’ll call to it. Like to like.” She pointed a trembling finger as the needle gave a violent shudder and held, pointing southwest. “There. Go.”

They fled through the labyrinth of back lanes and service alleys, two ghosts in the afternoon gloom. The compass led them not by streets, but through cracks in the city’s plan: a rusted gate in a high wall, a forgotten pedestrian tunnel under a railway arch dripping with condensation, a gap between two warehouses choked with nettles. Leo’s mind raced, a frantic counterpoint to the pounding of his heart. *Aberration. Recall. Tidy.* He was a spill to be mopped up. Elara was a corrupted file to be deleted. The clinical, impersonal violence of it was worse than any monster. It denied them even the dignity of being a threat. They were a discrepancy.

He glanced at Elara as they ran. Her face was set, but a fine tremor ran through her frame. The display in the shop hadn’t been cost-free. She was draining a battery he couldn’t see.

“The park,” she gasped, as they emerged onto a quiet, tree-lined street of crumbling Georgian townhouses. “Kelvinvale. It’s a… soft spot. A blister on the Veil. The compass knows.”

Kelvinvale Park was less a park than a forgotten memory of one. Locked iron gates, hung with signs warning of unsafe structures, enclosed a wilderness of skeletal trees and rampant rhododendron. The ornate Victorian glasshouses were webs of broken iron and jagged glass. It was a place the city had turned its back on, a pocket of decay.

The compass needle was rigid now, pointing straight through the rusted gates. The air above the park quivered, like tarmac on a hot day.

“The lock,” Leo said, rattling the heavy chain.

Elara placed a hand on the cold iron. She murmured something, a single word that sounded like a sigh. The metal of the padlock didn’t break. It *rusted*, centuries of decay accelerating in seconds, flaking away into orange dust. The chain slithered loose like a dead snake.

They slipped inside. The atmosphere changed instantly. The city’s sounds—distant traffic, a siren—muffled, swallowed by a dense, mossy quiet. The light was different too, greener, older. The path was cracked and veined with weeds. In the centre of the overgrown lawn stood a ruined bandstand, its paint peeled away to grey wood, its roof sagging like a tired mouth.

“Here,” Elara said, stopping in the bandstand’s shadow. She took the compass from him. Her touch was fever-hot. “This will be a door. A short one. Just a step through the curtain. To a… between-place. A bolt-hole they don’t know.”

“A between-place,” Leo repeated, the hollowness in him growing. He was leaving the world. Not in theory, not in a journal. In practice.

Elara was already working. She placed the compass on the damp earth. From her pocket she produced a small, worn blade—not metal, but a shard of dark, glossy obsidian. Without flinching, she drew it across her palm. Blood, shockingly red in the green gloom, welled up. She let it drip onto the compass face, over the sigils.

The blood didn’t smear. It was absorbed, drawn into the engraving like ink into blotting paper. The brass began to glow with a low, internal light.

“Your hand,” she commanded, not looking up.

“What?”

“The door needs a key for each of us. A blood signature. Or it snaps shut behind the first one through.” Her eyes met his, fierce, impatient. “Unless you want to stay and discuss philosophy with the cleaners?”

From the far end of the park, near the gates, there was a flash of unnatural light—a brief, clinical white flare that left no afterimage. They were here. The Harvesters had called for backup. The pursuit was no longer a quiet recall. It was a sweep.

Leo took the obsidian blade. It was colder than ice. He sliced his palm. The pain was sharp, clean, clarifying. He clenched his fist over the compass, his blood joining hers.

The effect was immediate. The compass’s light intensified, beaming upwards, not as a ray, but as a column of shimmering, liquid air. Within the column, the world behind it warped and melted. Leo saw the bandstand, the trees, but they were smeared, dreamlike. And beyond them, he glimpsed something else: a landscape of low, rolling hills under a bruised purple sky, dotted with standing stones that pulsed with a soft, amber light.

Elara began to chant, her voice weaving with the light. The column widened, forming a rippling, vertical pool—a gateway. The air smelled of ozone, of thyme, of turned earth.

A projectile, a sphere of crackling, hungry silence, tore through the rhododendron bushes and impacted the base of the bandstand. The wooden post it hit didn’t shatter. It simply *vanished* in a two-foot section, causing the whole structure to lurch sideways with a groan of protesting timber.

Another flash. Two figures in anoraks, moving with terrifying, coordinated speed across the lawn. A third, taller figure stood at the broken gates, hands raised. The air around him began to *dim*, as if he was drawing all light and sound into himself to forge another weapon.

“It’s open!” Elara screamed over the rising hum of the portal and the deafening silence of the Harvesters’ magic. “Go! Now!”

The gateway shimmered, a mirage made solid. The between-place beckoned, strange, silent, unknown. Leo looked at the lunging Harvesters, their faces blank of everything but purpose. He looked at Elara, her body a taut bowstring, holding the portal open with sheer will, her blood dripping onto the hungry earth.

His hesitation was a fraction of a second. A lifetime. The male Harvester from the shop was ten feet away, his hand extended. From his fingertips leapt not a projectile, but a *line* of nullity, a filament of un-being that snaked towards Leo’s chest, seeking to sever his connection to the world, thread by essential thread.

In that moment, the lens through which Leo saw everything shattered.

Elara was not a guide. She was not a protector. She was a force, spent and straining, holding a door open in a hurricane. The Harvesters were not monsters. They were a system, sterile and efficient, enacting a protocol. He was not a hero, nor a victim. He was a variable. A problem to be solved or a key to be used. The old dynamics—the man leading, the woman supporting; the pursuer and the pursued; the saviour and the saved—were lies. Here, at the fraying edge of everything, there was only power and consequence, will and cost. And choice.

The nullity-line was a foot away. He felt the cold of it, the promise of an end to fear, to confusion, to the terrible, crushing weight of knowing.

Leo did not leap for the portal.

He turned sideways, letting the deadly filament whisper past his ribs. And he grabbed Elara’s **wrist**—not her hand, a deliberate, firm grip on her arm—and *pulled*.

It was not a gesture of rescue. It was an act of equalization. A decision made for both of them. He was not following her. He was ensuring they crossed *together*.

Her chant broke off in a gasp of surprise. Her eyes, wide and furious and perhaps, for the first time, seeing him, met his. The portal wavered.

Then they fell, not into the shimmering light, but *through* it, a tangle of limbs and shared momentum. The last thing Leo saw of his world was the bandstand collapsing in a spray of rotten wood, and the tall Harvester at the gate, lowering his hands, his expressionless face tilting in what might have been, against all odds, a faint nod of professional respect.

The world dissolved into a scream of colour and a silence so profound it was a noise in itself.

***

They landed hard on springy, grey turf. The air was cool, still, and carried the scent of heather and stone. The purple sky was starless, but illuminated by a sourceless, twilight glow. The standing stones formed a loose circle around them, their amber pulses slow and rhythmic, like sleeping hearts.

The gateway winked out behind them, leaving only a faint shimmer in the air that quickly faded.

Leo lay on his back, gasping. His cut hand throbbed. Elara was already pushing herself up onto her elbows, then to her feet. She stared at the spot where the portal had been, then down at her wrist, where his fingers had left red marks on her pale skin. She looked at him, lying in the alien grass.

Her expression was unreadable. Not gratitude. Not anger. A reassessment, sharp and clinical.

“You pulled me,” she said, her voice flat in the dense air.

“You were spent. You’d have been last. They’d have had you.” He sat up, wincing.

“I had calculated the risk.” She took a step towards him, looking down. “I had the timing. My choice. My cost to bear.”

“And my choice was to leave you to it?” He got to his feet, meeting her gaze. The strange light hollowed her eyes. “That’s not how this works. Not anymore.”

A long silence stretched between them, broken only by the soft, pulsing hum of the stones. The dynamic had shifted, irrevocably. He was not cargo. She was not a shield. They were two people in a between-place, one holding a key, the other knowing the locks, both of them bleeding onto the same strange soil.

Elara’s lips tightened. She gave a single, sharp nod. Not agreement. Acknowledgement. “The bolt-hole is a few hours’ walk,” she said, turning to survey the melancholy landscape. “We should move. Gates leave echoes. They might find the resonance.”

Leo followed her gaze. The rolling hills seemed to go on forever, a limbo of stone and twilight. He fished in his pocket, his fingers finding the cool brass of the compass. He pulled it out. The needle was motionless, dead. Here, between places, it had nothing to point to.

He looked back the way they had come. Nothing. No shimmer, no Glasgow rain, no sound of pursuit. Just the silent, watching stones.

Elara was already walking, a dark shape against the purple gloom. Leo took a deep breath of the strange, thin air, and followed. Not behind her. Beside her.

The hook for the next chapter hung in the silent air between them, unspoken: In a place with no maps, with the old rules stripped away, what were they to each other now? And what, in this silent, ancient between-place, was listening to the echo of their footsteps?



---

# Chapter 6

## **CHAPTER 6**

The trees were not trees. They were memories of trees, sketched in charcoal against a sky the colour of a fading bruise, their branches holding globes of cold, green fire. The Glimmerwood. The name was a courtesy. It was a cathedral of sighing shadows and watchful, phosphorescent life. The air tasted of ozone and damp earth, of a world held in a perpetual, indrawn breath.

Leo walked beside Elara, their footsteps silent on the moss that swallowed sound. The compass was a dead weight in his pocket. Here, there was no north. There was only the soft, sickly glow ahead, and the deeper, velvet black behind. The dynamic between them had settled into a wary, functional silence. He was not cargo. She was not a shield. They were two points of tension on a single, fraying wire.

“The rules are softer here,” Elara said, her voice low, absorbed by the hanging moss. She didn’t look at him. Her eyes tracked the shifting patterns of light in the undergrowth. “Physics is a suggestion. Intent is a weapon. Do not think of predators. They taste thought.”

“What do we think of, then?” Leo asked. His own voice sounded alien, stripped of its Glasgow grit by the consuming quiet.

“The path. Only the path.” She paused, her head tilting. A cluster of violet fungi pulsed at the base of a trunk, a slow, rhythmic beat like a sleeping heart. “The bolt-hole is a fixed point. A knot in the looseness. We aim for the knot.”

They moved on. The forest was a labyrinth of soft light and softer dark. Bioluminescent vines dripped from branches like solidified moonlight. Flowers with petals of stained glass chimed softly as they passed, though there was no wind. It was beautiful, in the way a painted poison was beautiful. It was a beauty that did not care for them.

The attack came not from the dark, but from the light.

They were crossing a small clearing where a pool of silver liquid—water, or something like it—caught and held the forest’s glow. A stand of reeds with stems like crystal and heads of blue flame trembled.

One of the reeds *unfurled*.

It was not a plant. It was a thing of knotted, fibrous muscle, its crystal stem cracking open to reveal a mouth lined with shards of glowing silica. It moved with a whip-crack speed, not towards them, but towards the pool. It struck the silver surface.

The pool *screamed*.

The sound was high, crystalline, a shattering of frequencies that lanced through Leo’s teeth. The liquid light convulsed, and from its agony, the thing drank. It grew, its form bloating, distorting, pulling more substance from the reeds around it, which shrivelled and died into black dust. It was a corruption, a patch of reality where the rules had not just softened, but festered. A wound in the Veil.

“Don’t run,” Elara breathed, frozen. “Running is prey.”

The creature—a Glimmerghast, the name surfaced from some cold corner of Leo’s mind—turned. It had no eyes, but the front of its bulbous, pulsing body oriented towards them. It tasted their stillness, their shock. It began to flow across the moss, a wave of cancerous light and snapping crystal.

“Ideas,” Leo muttered, the word a curse. “You said intent.”

“Your intent is to *not be eaten*,” Elara shot back, her clinical tone fraying at the edges. “Focus it. Shape it. A wall. A cliff. Something it cannot cross.”

He thought of stone. Not just any stone. The black, wet cobbles of the alley behind *The Hanged Man*. The immovable, grimy, centuries-old foundation of a city that refused to be pretty. He fixed the image in his mind, the smell of damp and rubbish, the unyielding solidity of it. He pushed the idea out in front of them, a mental barricade.

The Glimmerghast hit an invisible boundary five feet away. It recoiled, a sizzle of corrupted energy rising from its front. It probed again, confused. The air shimmered with the heat of a summer pavement after rain.

“Good,” Elara said, the word tight. “Now, while it’s confused. A diversion. Something bright and loud. The opposite of us.”

Leo’s mind scrabbled. Bright and loud. A pub fight. The crash of a bottle, the sudden flare of shouted fury. He imagined it off to the left, behind a thicket of pulsing ferns—a burst of violent sound, a flash of aggressive light.

The creature rippled, then slithered towards the phantom noise, its hunger redirected.

“Now we *do* run,” Elara said, and she was moving, not in a panicked sprint, but in a low, swift glide towards the far tree line.

Leo followed, the image of the cobbles dissolving in his mind. His head throbbed, a deep, unfamiliar ache behind his eyes. Using his mind as a tool felt like trying to write with a broken arm. They plunged back into the deeper wood, leaving the clearing and its corrupted guardian behind.

After a hundred yards, Elara slowed, leaning against a trunk whose bark was etched with softly glowing runes. She was breathing hard.

“You used thought as a tool,” she said, watching him. “Instinctively. That is… uncommon.”

“I used a memory as a tool,” he corrected, rubbing his temples. “There’s a difference.”

“Is there?” She pushed off the tree. “Here, memory has weight. Substance. Thank you.”

It wasn’t gratitude. It was an entry in a ledger. *Asset: capable of rudimentary psychic defence.* He gave a short nod, mirroring her earlier acknowledgement. The ledger was balanced, for now.

They had gone another half-mile when the arrow buried itself in the tree between them with a sound like a sigh ending in a thud.

Leo froze. Elara’s hand went to the dagger at her belt, her body coiling.

“Step apart. Hands where I can see them.” The voice came from above, male, laced with a weary cynicism that felt as permanent as the forest’s twilight.

A figure dropped from the high branches, landing in a crouch that absorbed the impact without sound. He straightened. He was tall, lean, wrapped in dun-coloured leathers that seemed to drink the light. His hair was shorn close to his scalp, his face all sharp angles and guarded eyes the colour of flint. A long, elegant bow was held loosely in his left hand, a second arrow already nocked but not drawn. A Warden.

“This path is closed,” he said, his gaze flicking between them, assessing, dismissing, reassessing. He lingered on Elara’s clothing—the practical, otherworldly cut of it—and on Leo’s Glasgow-pale face. “Turn back. Whatever you’re fleeing, it’s kinder than what’s ahead.”

“We are not fleeing,” Elara said, her voice reclaiming its flat authority. “We are seeking. The bolt-hole at Greywatch.”

The Warden’s expression didn’t change, but the cynicism in his eyes deepened. “Everyone seeks Greywatch. Most are lies. Some are traps. All are a pain in my arse. Who sent you?”

“No one sent us. We came through the Standing Stones gate. From the other side.”

Now he went very still. The forest’s ambient hum seemed to grow louder. “The Glasgow anchor,” he said, finally. “It’s been quiet for decades. Why now?”

“Because it’s failing,” Leo said. The words were out before he could filter them. “The Veil is thin. Things are coming through. We have a key. She knows the locks.”

The Warden’s eyes snapped to Leo. “A key.” He held out a hand, expectant. “Show me.”

Leo glanced at Elara. Her lips were a tight line, but she gave a microscopic nod. He reached into his inner pocket, drew out the cloth-wrapped bundle, and carefully unfolded it. The iron key lay on the cloth, inert, a piece of mundane craftsmanship.

The Warden did not touch it. He leaned close, his eyes narrowing. Then he breathed a single, soft word onto its surface. The key shivered. From its bow to its tip, a tracery of silver light flared—a map of tiny, intricate sigils—before fading back to black iron.

“Hmph.” He straightened. “Authentic. Stolen, I assume.”

“Retrieved,” Elara countered.

“Semantics are for people with time to spare.” He slung his bow over his shoulder. “I’m Kael. You’ve stumbled into my patrol sector. Greywatch is a day’s hard travel from here, through the worst of the soft zones. I’ll guide you. Not because I like you. Because the key cannot fall to the Glimmerghasts, or the Static, or whatever else is chewing on the edges of the world. You are cargo. I am the deliveryman. Try not to die before I can offload you.”

He turned and began walking without waiting for agreement. Leo and Elara exchanged a look. It was the same look they’d shared in the grass by the stones: a reassessment. They fell in behind him, not beside him. The hierarchy, it seemed, was a fluid thing.

Kael set a brutal pace. He moved through the Glimmerwood not as an intruder, but as a part of its grim ecology, avoiding the prettiest glades and the darkest hollows with equal suspicion.
