# Chapter 15

## **CHAPTER 15**

The fire in the grate at *The Hanged Man* was a sullen, resentful thing. It consumed the peat bricks with a grudging, smoky flicker, casting more shadow than light. The heat it offered was a thin and miserly veil against the deeper cold that had taken root in Isla MacLeod’s bones.

She sat in the same corner booth as she had that first morning, the one by the window where the Glasgow light had bled through. Now, it was night, and the window showed only the reflection of the pub’s interior: Morag behind the bar, Finch hunched over a ledger, Leo staring into the amber depths of a whisky he hadn’t touched. Isla’s own reflection was a ghost-pale smudge against the black glass. She held a mug of sweet, strong tea in both hands, not for the warmth, but to feel the solidity of the ceramic, to test the simple truth of an object that did not dream or bleed or keep accounts.

She was empty. That was the only word for it. A vessel scoured clean.

On the scarred table between them lay the arm ring. It was not a dramatic centrepiece. It simply *was*, a closed loop of twisted gold that seemed to drink the firelight rather than reflect it. A receipt. The word tasted of ash and bureaucracy.

“It’s not a trophy,” Isla said. Her voice was a stranger’s—hoarse, used up. “It’s a marker. A tag.”

Leo finally looked up from his glass. The stinging in his eyes from the moor had hardened into something flinty. “For what?”

“For me.” She took a sip of tea. It was tasteless. “The debt was mine. The ledger was in my name. They closed the account, but they’ve filed me. For future reference.”

Morag leaned her hips against the bar, her arms crossed over her chest. The serene brutality of her pirate-queen demeanour was gone, replaced by a watchful, maternal ferocity. “You burned out the node. You screamed ‘no’ in the face of God’s own bookkeepers. You think they just walk away from an investment like that?”

Professor Finch closed his ledger with a soft thump. He looked older, the lines on his face not just marks of eccentricity but of a profound, scholarly shock. He had spent a lifetime studying the architecture of unseen bridges. He had never imagined watching someone burn one down from the inside. “The gendered cartography,” he murmured, not to them, but to the ghost of his own theories. “You didn’t just refuse their transaction, Isla. You refused the very map. The *terms* of the terrain. You became a terra incognita they could not plot. A woman-shaped blank space on their parchment.” He took off his glasses and polished them on his tweed waistcoat. “They cannot abide a blank space. Nature, and supernature, abhors a vacuum. They will seek to… re-categorise.”

Isla’s laugh was a short, dry crack. “Let them try.”

The force of the statement hung in the smoky air. It lacked the incandescent fury of her refusal on the moor. This was colder, quieter. A statement of fact from the emptiness. She had given everything that was asked, and then she had given the asking itself. There was nothing left for them to take. The realisation was not a victory; it was a desolate plateau.

Leo’s gaze shifted from her to the heel-print in his mind’s eye. Crisp. Deliberate. A woman’s shoe, left in the soft peat after the cosmic ledger had slammed shut. “The auditors are gone. The… *concept* of them, the light, the geometry. That’s finished. But the imprint was fresh. Someone else was there. Watching.”

“A witness,” Morag said. “Or a quality assurance officer. You don’t send a team of celestial accountants without a supervisor.”

“Or a rival firm,” Finch offered, his academic mind grasping for frameworks. “The interstitial realities are not a monopoly. Your refusal may have… voided a contract with one party, only to make you a person of interest to another. The old stories are full of such things. A soul, escaping a fairy debt, only to find a devil waiting at the crossroads with a newer, shinier contract.”

Isla’s fingers tightened on the mug. The emptiness within her echoed. It was not peace. It was the silence after the explosion, where every sound, every new presence, was a potential threat. “What do they want with an empty account?”

Leo reached across the table and picked up the arm ring. It was heavier than it looked. Cold. He did not hand it to Isla. He held it, feeling the weight of its intention. “Maybe they want to see what you’ll become,” he said, his voice low. “You’re not what you were. You’re not a node, or a conduit, or a debtor. You’re something they haven’t seen before. A woman who looked at the price and set it on fire.” He met her eyes. “That’s a power they’ll want to measure. To control. Or to eliminate.”

The fire popped, sending a shower of sparks against the grate. Isla looked from Leo’s weary, resolute face to Finch’s troubled academic gaze, to Morag’s protective scowl. They were her crew. Her derelict ship on a sea of rules she had broken. The debt was paid, but the cost was ongoing. It was the cost of being witnessed. Of being known.

“Then we don’t wait for them to come auditing,” Isla said. The ghost in the window seemed to solidify as she spoke. “We audit them back.”

Finch blinked. “My dear, that’s… that’s not how it works. The unseen bridges have protocols, not addresses. You can’t… file a complaint.”

“I’m not talking about a complaint.” Isla set her mug down. The click of ceramic on wood was definitive. “The heel print. It’s a clue. It’s a *material* clue, left in *our* world. That’s a breach in their professionalism. It’s sloppy. It’s emotional.” She leaned forward, a faint, cold light returning to her eyes—not the supernova of before, but the steady, penetrating beam of a lighthouse on a barren coast. “They’re watching? Good. Let’s give them a show. Let’s find out who left their signature in the mud. Let’s follow the supervisor home.”

Morag’s mouth quirked, the first hint of her old, brutal smile returning. “Now you’re talking my language, hen. You don’t wait for the taxman. You go through his books with a red pen and a righteous fury.”

Leo turned the arm ring over in his palm. A receipt. A tag. But also a token. A piece of their world, given in exchange. “This is gold. Old gold. Not just a concept. It has a weight, a history. It came from somewhere.” He looked at Finch. “You have colleagues. Archaeologists. Metallurgists. People who don’t ask why, they just ask *when* and *where*.”

Finch nodded slowly, the scholar re-engaging, the shock receding before the lure of a new, terrifying puzzle. “I know a woman. At the museum. She has… a pragmatic relationship with provenance. She asks very few questions for a very high price.”

“Then we start there,” Isla said. She did not reach for the arm ring. She let Leo keep it, this physical proof of her hollow victory. “We use their own clue. We turn their audit into our inquest.”

The plan, such as it was, formed in the smoky gloom. It was not a grand design, but a series of small, probing actions. Finch would approach his contact. Leo would use his own, less academic networks to ask about women—specific, particular kinds of women—who might be interested in lonely moors and closed accounts. Morag would mind the pub, the fixed point in their turning world, and listen. The Hanged Man was a crossroads; information bled in with the drunks and the desperate.

Isla’s role was the hardest. She had to learn to be empty. To move through the world as a blank page, feeling for the pressure of any pen trying to write upon her. She had to be the terra incognita, the silent alarm, the human-shaped silence that would resonate if anything tried to fill it.

Two days passed. The cold in her bones did not lessen, but she learned its contours. It was a clean cold. It sharpened her senses. Colours seemed more vivid, sounds more distinct, but they were all at a remove, as if she observed them through a thick, perfect pane of glass. She felt no fear. No anxiety. Only a vast, crystalline clarity. She was the void after the negation. And the void was watching back.

On the third morning, Finch returned. He found Isla in the back room of the pub, cataloguing Morag’s stock of esoteric spirits—whiskies from closed distilleries, liqueurs infused with rowan and hawthorn, things that were less beverages than liquid folklore.

He did not speak immediately. He stood in the doorway, his usual academic flutter stilled. He looked afraid.

“Elspeth McCready will see you,” he said finally. “She’s the senior curator of Iron Age and Early Historic Collections. She’s… formidable. And expensive. I had to promise her a viewing.”

“Of the ring?” Isla asked, her voice flat in the dusty air.

“No.” Finch swallowed. “Of you.”

The museum was a temple of ordered silence. Its halls were a cartography of human time, everything labelled, dated, securely cased. Isla walked through the exhibits of Pictish stones and Viking hoards, feeling like an uncatalogued anomaly. Her emptiness hummed faintly here, a dissonant note in the chorus of settled history.

Elspeth McCready’s office was not in the public galleries. It was a labyrinthine journey through staff corridors, past crates and trolleys, into the heart of the building where history was raw and uninterpreted. The office itself was a controlled chaos of artefacts, books, and the faint, dusty smell of old metal and acid-free paper.

McCready was a woman in her late fifties, with a severe grey bob and eyes the colour of flint. She did not rise from her desk, which was a landscape of paperwork and tiny, tagged objects. She looked at Finch with a nod of bare recognition, then her gaze settled on Isla. It was not a human look. It was an appraisal. A dating technique.

“Professor Finch speaks in abstractions,” McCready said. Her voice was clipped, Edinburgh. “Interstitial realities. Gendered cartographies. A load of metaphysical wool-gathering. I deal in *things*.” She pointed a finger at a small, corroded piece of bronze on her desk. “That is a thing. It was found in a bog. It has weight, composition, corrosion patterns. It tells a story of soil and time. Not of concepts.”

Isla said nothing. She let the woman’s gaze wash over her.

“But he also showed me a photograph,” McCready continued. “Of another thing. A gold arm ring. Twisted type. Late Iron Age, possibly early post-Roman. Except the one in the photograph has no patina. No wear. It looks… newly made. Or newly *un-made* from time.” She leaned forward slightly. “And he told me a story about a woman who paid a debt with a negation. Which is, again, a concept. A story. I am not interested in stories.”

“Then why are we here?” Isla asked. Her voice did not echo in the cluttered room. It was absorbed by it.

“Because of the shoe,” McCready said.

A beat of silence. Finch stiffened. They had told no one about the heel print.

McCready allowed a thin, humourless smile. “You have your sources, Professor. I have mine. A man walks into a pub in Oban, three days ago. He’s a hillwalker, a reliable sort. He says he was on a remote stretch of moor, day after the last full moon. Saw nothing unusual. But he did find, and I quote, ‘a right fancy lady’s shoe stuck in the bog, like Cinderella had a bloody awful night out.’ He thought it was rubbish, left it. But he mentioned it. In my line of work, you learn to listen for the odd detail that doesn’t belong.”

She opened a drawer and placed a clear plastic evidence bag on the desk. Inside was a single shoe. A pump, with a slender, sharp heel. It was made of a deep, emerald-green velvet, now stained and stiff with black peat. It was elegant, expensive, and utterly anachronistic on that ancient moor.

“This was retrieved yesterday,” McCready said. “By a person in my employ. The hillwalker was persuaded to be more specific about the location. It matches yours.”

Isla stared at the shoe. It was more disturbing than the arm ring. The ring was a symbol, a token. This was an intimate, personal object. It spoke of a body, a gait, a choice. A woman who stood in the wet dark and watched her refuse a cosmic debt.

“You can’t carbon-date velvet,” McCready said. “But you can trace a maker. This is not off-the-rack. This is bespoke. There are perhaps two dozen places in the world that could make this shoe. One of them is in this city.”

The void inside Isla shifted. Not with feeling, but with direction. A coordinate had been plotted. “Who?”

McCready’s flinty eyes held hers. “First, I see the ring. The real thing. Not a photograph.”

Isla glanced at Leo, who had been standing silently by the door. He hesitated, then reached into his inner pocket and drew out the twist of gold. He placed it on the desk beside the bagged shoe.

The effect was immediate. McCready’s professional detachment shattered. She did not gasp, but her breath caught. She pulled a pair of white cotton gloves from a box and put them on with ritual care. She did not pick the ring up immediately. She simply stared.

“My God,” she whispered. It was not a prayer. It was a curse of professional awe. “It’s… perfect. It’s not a reproduction. It’s not a fake. The twist pattern… it’s identical to the example from the Norrie’s Law hoard. But that’s in the National Museum. And this…” She finally lifted it, turning it in the light from her angle-poise lamp. “There’s no seam. No tool mark a modern eye can see. It’s as if it was *willed* into this form. The gold is pure. Too pure. Ancient gold has impurities. This is… theoretical gold.”

She looked up at Isla, her earlier disdain gone, replaced by a hungry, desperate curiosity. “Where did it come from?”

“They gave it to me,” Isla said. “A receipt.”

“They?”

“The ones the woman in the green shoes works for.”

McCready placed the ring down as if it were radioactive. She looked from it, to the shoe, to Isla’s empty face. The pieces, the tangible, physical pieces, were telling a story she could no longer dismiss. A story that broke her beloved rules of soil and time.

“The cobbler,” she said, her voice hushed. “His name is Alasdair Byrne. He has a workshop in the Merchant City. He makes shoes for theatre, for film, for… certain private clients with unusual tastes. He is a craftsman who believes his work is a kind of summoning. That the right shoe can help a person walk into another story.” She pushed a slip of paper across the desk. An address. “He will not speak to you. He is notoriously private. But he will speak to the ring. And to the woman who carries it.”

Isla took the paper. The address was in her hand. A point on the map. A thread pulled from the velvet.

“What do you want in return?” Leo asked, his voice gravelly with distrust.

McCready’s gaze was still locked on the arm ring. “I want to study it. For one night. Here, in my lab. I want to take measurements, photographs, spectrographic analyses. I want to know what a receipt from God looks like under a microscope.”

Isla nodded. It was a fair trade. A night of the ring’s time, for a name. “Done.”

They left the ring in that room of ordered history, a perfect, impossible anomaly among the broken shards of the past. As they walked back through the silent halls, Isla felt the first tremor in her emptiness. Not a feeling, but a pressure. A sense of being *followed* not by a person, but by an attention.

The heel-print was no longer just a clue. It was a challenge. A green velvet gauntlet thrown down in the peat.

Outside, the Glasgow afternoon was a dull, metallic grey. Leo lit a cigarette, the smoke whipped away by a cold wind. “Byrne,” he said. “A cobbler who thinks he’s a sorcerer. Of course.”

“He’s a link,” Isla said, folding the address into her pocket. “A human link. They used a human craftsman. That’s a vulnerability. It means they need our world, too. Its materials. Its skills. They can’t just concept everything into being.”

Finch huddled into his coat, looking back at the museum’s imposing façade. “McCready will learn nothing definitive from that ring. It will defy every analysis. It will break her machines with its perfection. It will haunt her for the rest of her career.”

“Good,” Isla said. She began to walk, her steps firm on the pavement. The void within her had a target now. A direction. The audit was turning. They were no longer just waiting to be assessed. They were following the evidence, from the supervisor’s signature in the mud, to the hand that made her shoe.

But as they turned the corner, Isla stopped. In the window of a closed charity shop, amid a jumble of old kettles and faded paperbacks, was a mannequin. It was headless, limbless, just a torso on a stand. It was wearing a jacket of a deep, emerald-green velvet.

It was just a coincidence. A trick of the light, of a mind now tuned to see patterns. Isla knew this. The rational part of her, the PhD candidate, catalogued it as such.

Yet, as she stood there on the busy street, the cold in her bones seemed to sharpen, focusing to a single, needle point between her shoulder blades.

The ledger was closed. The debt was paid.

But the woman in the green shoes was not just watching.

She was waiting. And she had exquisite taste.



---

# Chapter 16

## **CHAPTER 16**

The emerald velvet in the charity shop window was a vulgar echo. A cheap, moth-nipped imitation of the supervisor’s sumptuous green, but its presence was a punctuation mark in the grey sentence of the street. Isla stared at it, the cold between her shoulder blades a precise, academic pain. A hypothesis was forming, not in words, but in a visceral understanding of aesthetic language. The supervisor did not merely wear green. She curated it. She seeded it into the periphery as a signature, a taunt written in texture and hue.

“Isla?” Leo’s voice was a scrape of matchstick on sandpaper. He followed her gaze to the mannequin, his detective’s mind seeing a discarded prop, her academic’s mind seeing a footnote.

“It’s nothing,” she said, turning away. The lie was automatic, a membrane between her internal audit and the external world. She could not yet articulate the theory: that the supervisor’s power was not just bureaucratic, but artistic. That her cruelty had a palette.

Finch was already hailing a taxi, a frail arm raised against the Glasgow drizzle that had begun to bleed from the metallic sky. “Byrne’s address is across the river. Govan. A place where things are built, and things are broken.”

The taxi was a capsule of stale breath and synthetic pine. Isla sat with the address, a slip of paper now damp from her grip, feeling its ordinariness. 42b Keldon Lane. A human coordinate. Leo smoked out the window, the drizzle swallowing his exhales. Finch hummed a tuneless fragment of something old, his eyes closed, retreating into the cathedral of his own mind.

Govan unfolded outside the window, a landscape of muscular memory. The ghosts of shipyards loomed in vast, vacant lots, their skeletal gantries scratching at the bellies of low clouds. Here, the city’s pragmatism was laid bare—the making of tangible things, of hulls and engines, a philosophy of rivets and sweat. It felt like the antithesis of the supervisor’s realm of concepts and ledgers. And yet, a cobbler had bridged the gap. He had worked leather that walked between worlds.

Keldon Lane was a narrow street of two-storey workshops, their brickwork stained a perpetual industrial black. 42b was at the end, a unit separate from the others, its windows whitewashed from the inside. No sign announced a business. The only clue was a faint, acrid scent on the damp air—not just glue and dye, but something sharper, mineral, like ozone after a lightning strike.

Leo stubbed his cigarette on the wet pavement. “No bell. Of course.” He rapped his knuckles against the heavy steel door. The sound was swallowed, producing no echo.

They waited in the drizzle. Isla’s emptiness was a silent chamber, and into it poured the ambient pressure of the place. It was not the watchful attention of the museum, but a dense, focused silence, as if the air inside was too occupied to spare any for the outside world.

The door opened inward a crack, wide enough to reveal a single eye, the colour of old sherry, and a slice of a face webbed with deep, leathery lines.

“We’re closed,” a voice said, dry as dust.

“Mr. Byrne?” Isla stepped forward, placing herself in the narrow frame of his vision. “We’re not customers. We need to talk about a commission. A pair of shoes. Green kid leather. With a specific heel.”

The eye blinked, once, slow as a lizard. “Don’t know what you’re talking about.”

“The leather wasn’t from here,” Isla pressed, her voice low, a conspirator’s murmur. “It was brought to you. The design was… provided. The client was a woman. Unforgettable.”

A long pause. The drizzle pattered on Isla’s shoulders. Then, the door swung open fully, releasing a gust of that interior air—ozone, cedarwood, and the profound, soul-deep smell of worked hide.

“You’d better come in,” Seamus Byrne said. “Before the rain learns your business.”

He was a small man, compact as a root, dressed in a leather apron so old it had moulded to the shape of his body. His workshop was a cavern of ordered obsession. Lasts of every size hung from the ceiling like grotesque fruit. Tools were laid on benches with surgical precision—skiving knives, awls, lasting pincers. But along the far wall, on a separate bench of dark, oiled wood, lay tools that were different. Their handles were of a bone-pale wood, and their metal parts gleamed with a cold, blue-silver light that owed nothing to the single bare bulb overhead. On a shelf above them sat small, cork-stoppered jars. One held what looked like granules of solidified shadow. Another, a swirling, mother-of-pearl mist that moved with a life of its own.

Byrne did not offer seats. He stood before his sacred bench, a priest at his altar. “You mentioned her. So you’ve seen. You’ve been… assessed.”

“We have,” Finch said, his academic curiosity overriding caution. He peered at the jars. “Fascinating. Phasic resins? Condensed perceptual matter?”

Byrne ignored him, his sherry-coloured eyes fixed on Isla. “You carry the quiet about you. The hollow place. She leaves that.”

“You made her shoes,” Isla stated.

“I made *the vessels for her feet*,” Byrne corrected, his tone that of a theologian parsing dogma. “I did not *make her shoes*. The distinction is the contract. The leather was presented. The design was inscribed on a vellum that sang when you touched it. My role was application. Craft. The translation of concept into substance.” He walked to a small, heavy safe nestled beneath the bench. He worked the combination, not with his fingers, but with a series of precise, whispered taps from a small brass hammer. The door clicked open.

From within, he withdrew a ledger. Not black, like the supervisor’s, but bound in a mottled, tawny hide. He placed it on the bench and opened it to a page near the end. Isla saw a sketch, exquisite in its detail, of her own green shoe. Next to it, in a cramped, meticulous hand, were notes. *Client: The Assessor. Material: Kid, harvested from the grief-goats of the Silent Pastures. Dye: Verdure from the heart of a forgotten midsummer. Heel: To be shaped from a fragment of the Threshold Stone (provided). Payment: One (1) memory of my father’s voice, circa 1963. Delivered.*

“She pays in memories,” Isla breathed.

“She pays in *truths*,” Byrne said, a flicker of anguish in his deep-lined face. “The only currency that holds its value across the bridges. I asked for his voice. I got it. For three minutes, in this very room, I heard him call me ‘son’. Then it was gone. Spent. A coin in her infinite pocket.” He looked at Isla, his gaze probing her hollow centre. “What did she take from you?”

Isla said nothing. Leo answered for her, his voice a gravelly shield. “None of your damn business. You took a commission from a thing that audits human lives. You made the tools she uses to walk into our world.”

“I am a bridge-maker!” Byrne snapped, a flash of pride in his watery eyes. “The Interstitial Realities are not *other*. They are *between*. They require points of contact. Anchors. A concept must have a form to exert force. A law must have a ledger to be recorded. A supervisor… must have shoes to walk the floors of her domain and yours. I provide the points of contact.” He calmed, running a reverent hand over the sketch. “She is the most exacting client I have ever had. Her specifications are… sublime.”

Isla understood then. Byrne was not a collaborator out of malice, but out of a craftsman’s rapture. The supervisor had seduced him with the purity of her demands, with the opportunity to work with materials that sang of other worlds. He was in love with the making, blind to the purpose.

“Where does she go?” Isla asked. “When she’s not assessing. Where does she… exist?”

Byrne closed the ledger softly. “She has a place. A gallery. She calls it her *Salon of Finalities*. A space between spaces where she… curates the outcomes of her audits. The particularly poignant closures. The elegantly balanced debts.” He looked at them, and for the first time, a sliver of pity entered his expression. “You wish to find her. To plead? To negotiate?”

“To audit the auditor,” Leo said.

Byrne let out a short, dry laugh, like stones rattling in a tin. “You are moths seeking the architect of the candle flame. You cannot go to her Salon. It is not a location. It is a conclusion.”

“But you have a point of contact,” Isla said, her eyes going to the bench of strange tools. “You made her anchor. There must be a way to trace the line back. To follow the thread from the shoe to the foot.”

The cobbler was silent for a long moment, his gaze travelling from Isla’s determined face to Finch’s rapt one to Leo’s hardened scowl. He was weighing, Isla realised, not their chances, but the aesthetic merit of their desperation. Would it make a worthy exhibit in his own mind?

“There is a tool,” he said finally, his voice dropping to a whisper. “A needle, forged from the same fragment of Threshold Stone as her heels. I used it to stitch the soles. It carries a… sympathetic resonance. A hunger for its own kind.” He moved to the far end of the sacred bench and unlocked a slender drawer. From it, he lifted a long needle. It was the same blue-silver as the other tools, but darker, denser. It seemed to drink the light around it. “If you had a map of the in-between, this needle, laid upon it, would tremble in the direction of its source stone. It would point the way.”

“And where would one find such a map?” Finch asked, breathless.

Byrne’s lips thinned. “You have a metaphysician. He knows of the *Carta Incognita*. The map that is not a map.”

Finch went very still. “The Carta is a theoretical construct. A folio of pure potential. It’s never been seen.”

“It resides,” Byrne said, “in the Special Collections of the University Library. Locked in the Black Cabinet. A gift from a certain 18th-century nobleman who dabbled in more than whisky and wives.” He looked at Finch. “You are emeritus. You have the access. But the Cabinet… it requires a key of a particular nature. It reads intent.”

Isla took the needle from Byrne’s offered hand. It was colder than ice, a cold that seeped into her bones and made the hollow within her ache in sympathy. It felt alive, and hungry. “Why help us?”

Byrne looked at the safe where his ledger lay. “I have heard my father’s voice. It was a perfect payment. But perfection… it is a sterile country. I find I miss the noise. The mess. The uneven stitches.” He met her eyes. “And I am curious. I have made the shoes for the end of stories. I should like to see what happens when a story refuses to end.”

***

The rain had hardened into a persistent, needling downpour by the time they returned to the university. The Gothic spires of the main building speared the gloomy twilight, a fortress of known things. Inside, the air was warm and still, thick with the smell of old paper and dust.

Finch led them through the grand hall, past the marble stare of long-dead principals, to a discreet oak door marked *Special Collections – By Appointment Only*. His emeritus key card bypassed the electronic lock with a soft beep.

The room beyond was a twilight space, illuminated by pools of light from green-shaded lamps on long reading tables. Rows of dark-stained bookshelves receded into shadow, holding countless volumes sheathed in protective grey boxes. The air hummed with silence, the dense, absorbed quiet of secrets kept too long.

At the far end of the room stood the Black Cabinet.

It was an imposing piece, eight feet tall, of ebony so deep it seemed to be a slice of midnight given form. Its surface was inlaid with intricate marquetry of mother-of-pearl and silver, depicting constellations that were not of any earthly sky—swirling, non-Euclidean patterns that hurt the eye to follow. There was no visible keyhole.

“The Carta Incognita,” Finch murmured, approaching it with a scholar’s reverence. “Not a map of places, but of *possibilities*. Of the latent pathways that exist between states of being. It is the sub-basement of reality’s architecture.” He reached a trembling hand towards the cabinet’s central seam.

Before he could touch it, the mother-of-pearl constellations shimmered. A soft, silvery light traced the patterns, and a voice, androgynous and dry as parchment, filled the space around them, not through the air, but directly in the mind. *“Intent is key. Declare your purpose.”*

Finch flinched. Leo’s hand went instinctively to his coat, where no weapon resided. Isla stepped forward, the cold needle a secret in her fist.

“We seek the Carta,” Finch said, his voice unsteady.

*“Purpose is not acquisition. Purpose is application. Declare the use.”*

Isla spoke into the mental silence. “We seek a path. To a place between places. A Salon of Finalities.”

The cabinet’s light pulsed, a slow, rhythmic beat like a distant heart. *“The Carta does not chart destinations. It charts transitions. To find an interstitial gallery, you must name the transition you wish to make.”*

Isla thought of the green shoes, of the ledger, of the void inside her. “From debt to defiance. From audited to auditor.”

The silvery light flared, brilliant and cold. The central seam of the cabinet split soundlessly, the two doors swinging inward.

Inside, on a pedestal of clear crystal, lay the Carta Incognita.

It was not a scroll, nor a book. It was a folio of what appeared to be solidified light, or perhaps captured shadow, a material that shifted and swam as they looked at it. Within its depth, faint lines and symbols glimmered, constantly rearranging themselves—a living, reactive diagram of metaphysical potential.

“Extraordinary,” Finch whispered.

“Now the needle,” Isla said.

She approached, the Threshold needle held out before her. As she drew near to the Carta, the needle began to vibrate in her hand, a faint, high-frequency tremor that travelled up her arm. She held it over the surface of the shifting folio.

The reaction was immediate. The swirling lines on the Carta stilled, then rushed towards the point above the needle like iron filings to a magnet. They coalesced, solidified, drawing themselves into a stark, two-dimensional diagram. It showed a series of concentric circles, like a target, or the growth rings of an impossible tree. At the very centre was a small, pulsing dot of emerald green. Radiating out from it were three faint, dotted lines, like possible paths. One line was short, ending in a symbol that looked like a closed eye. Another spiralled out into a chaotic scribble before fading. The third, however, traced a slow, deliberate arc to the outer edge of the diagram, where it terminated at a symbol that made Finch gasp: a simple, stylised heel-print.

“It’s a protocol,” Finch breathed, leaning close. “Not a map of *where*, but of *how*. The paths represent different methodologies of transition. The closed eye… that is surrender. Acceptance of the finality. The chaotic path is madness, dissolution in the between-spaces. But this…” He pointed to the path leading to the heel-print. “This is the path of *the craft*. The path of the anchor. It uses the sympathetic link. It says that to reach her Salon, you must not travel through space, but through the medium of the artifact itself. You must… follow the making.”

Leo frowned. “What does that mean? Stitch ourselves a pair of shoes?”

“It means,” Isla said, her eyes fixed on the green dot at the centre, “we don’t go to the Salon. We get the Salon to notice us. Through the craft. Through a new point of contact.” She looked at the needle in her hand, then at the Carta’s precise, unforgiving diagram. “We have to make something. Not for her. But *of* her. And invite her to come and see.”

Finch paled. “Create an artifact tied to her essence? That is an act of profound aggression in her language. A forging of a bridge on our terms. It would be an audit of *her*.”

“Yes,” Isla said, the word final as a closing door.

The cabinet’s voice echoed in their minds once more, fainter now. *“The transition is defined. The path is perilous. The Carta warns: to craft a mirror for a supervisor is to risk becoming the reflection. The materials required are not of this world.”*

“We have a cobbler,” Leo said, his voice grim. “And we have a starting point.” He nodded at the needle. “We have a piece of her world.”

Isla carefully lifted the Carta from its pedestal. It was weightless, yet it felt heavier than stone in her hands, a condensed potential that made the air crackle. The diagram remained, frozen on its surface. A recipe for a confrontation.

As they turned to leave, the Black Cabinet doors swung shut with a soft, definitive *thud*. The silvery light in its inlay died, leaving it once more a slab of dormant night.

Back in the humid silence of the Special Collections room, with the living map in her hands and the hungry needle in her pocket, Isla felt the void within her shift. It was no longer just an emptiness. It was a mould. A negative space waiting to be filled not with what was taken, but with what she would now deliberately, ruthlessly create.

The supervisor collected finalities. She curated outcomes.

Isla MacLeod would offer her a new exhibit: the unfinished thing. The defiant draft. A shoe that did not fit, thrown at the head of the goddess of closure.

They moved through the library’s gloom, a trio of conspirators against conclusion. But as they passed a long, polished table, Isla saw it. Reflected in the dark, varnished wood, for the briefest fraction of a second, was not her own face, nor the bookshelves behind her.

It was a flash of emerald green, standing perfectly still, three paces behind Leo.

Isla froze. She did not turn. She held the reflection in the corner of her eye, her blood turning to ice water. The figure did not move. It simply watched, a splash of impossible colour in the monochrome reflection.

Leo, ahead of her, stopped. “Isla?”

She forced her eyes forward, breaking the connection. When she glanced back at the table’s surface, it showed only wood grain and shadow.

“It’s nothing,” she said again, the lie thicker now, a pill on her tongue.

But the hook was set. The audit was not a closed ledger. It was an open dialogue. They were crafting their challenge, and she, the woman in the green shoes, was already reading the draft.

The Salon of Finalities had just extended a new invitation. And the RSVP was written in the cold, hungry point of a needle, and the terrifying weight of a map that led not to a place, but to a precipice of their own making.



---

# Chapter 17

## **CHAPTER 17**

The map was a living, breathing accusation. It lay on the scarred oak table in the back room of *The Hanged Man*, its vellum surface faintly misting with each of Isla’s exhales. The ink—a concoction of oak gall, ground lodestone, and something Leo had called ‘moon-ash’—moved. Not much. A slow, capillary creep along the lines of force that underlay the city, a subcutaneous pulse. It was a map of Glasgow’s unseen ligaments, and in its centre, a void. A negative space shaped like the supervisor’s Salon of Finalities, a cartographic silence that swallowed the ink that ventured near its edges.

Morag leaned over it, her aubergine hair a violent splash against the nicotine-stained ceiling. She held a butcher’s cleaver, using the flat side to crush juniper berries into a mortar. The rhythmic *thunk-crack* was the room’s only steady heartbeat.

“So it’s a trap,” Morag stated, not asking. “You’re baiting a mousetrap with a ghost of a cheese that never was.”

“We’re offering an alternative narrative,” Leo corrected, his voice tight. He was dismantling a complex brass astrolabe he’d ‘liberated’ from the University’s archaeology store, his fingers precise and furious. “A story with the ending ripped out. A shoe that doesn’t fit.”

“Ach, spare me the poetry, son. It’s a trap. She’s the cat, you’re the mice with a wee, shiny bell you think is a weapon.” Morag swept the crushed berries into a small copper bowl. “And our Isla here saw the cat’s reflection, licking its paws, while you were drawing your wee battle plans.”

Isla said nothing. She was holding the supervisor’s needle. It lay across her palms, a sliver of distilled conclusion, colder than the Glasgow stone beneath the floorboards. The memory of the emerald green in the library’s reflection was a splinter behind her eye. It hadn’t been fear, precisely. It had been a kind of professional appraisal. The supervisor wasn’t angry. She was *curious*. A chef watching a sous-chef attempt a new, reckless recipe.

“She wants to see what we’ll build,” Isla said, her voice low. “The audit isn’t an inspection. It’s a… sourcing trip. We’re foraging for our own destruction, and she’s noting down the location of the best mushrooms.”

Leo looked up, his glasses catching the dull gleam of the single bare bulb. “Then we give her a poison one.”

“With what antidote?” Morag shot back. “You’ve got the poison needle, aye. You’ve got the map to the place you shouldn’t go. What’s the third thing? Every good story needs three. The weapon, the way, and the wager. What’s your wager, hen?”

Isla’s hand closed around the needle. The point was a perfect, hungry zero. It promised an end to all questions. “Myself,” she said.

The room went still. Even the creeping ink on the map seemed to pause.

“Explain,” Morag commanded, setting down her cleaver.

“The supervisor collects finalities. She curates outcomes.” Isla met the old woman’s gaze. “My thesis… it was a draft. An unfinished thing. She took it and finished it. Made it *hers*. Made it *done*.” She placed the needle on the map, its point hovering over the void of the Salon. “So I give her another draft. Me. The unfinished Isla MacLeod. The one with the void inside, the one who is all potential and no resolution. I walk into her Salon not with a finished story, but with a blank page. And I dare her to try and write an ending on it.”

Leo went pale. “That’s not a wager, that’s a suicide note. She’ll just… file you. Put you in a drawer labelled ‘Resolved: Pathetic’.”

“Will she?” Isla’s smile was thin, a crack in ice. “She’s a perfectionist. She needs the right ending, the elegant solution. A messy, defiant, ongoing draft? A woman who is a living ‘to be continued…’? That’s her antithesis. It’s the one thing she can’t curate without destroying the essential quality. It’s the shoe that doesn’t fit. Throw it hard enough, it might break her nose.”

Morag stared at her for a long moment. Then a slow, jagged grin split her face. “Ach, you’ve gone feral, hen. Proper feral. It’s a terrible plan. It’s all posture and no knickers.” She picked up the copper bowl and upended it over the map. The crushed juniper scattered across the vellum like coarse, fragrant dust. “But it’s the only kind of plan that has a heartbeat. So. We need to make you a proper draft. Unfinishable.”

She lumbered to a locked cabinet behind the bar, returning with a bottle containing a liquid the colour of a week-old bruise. “This,” she said, “is *Beatha na h-Òige*.”

“The Water of Life?” Leo frowned. “It looks like death.”

“Life usually does, to boys who read too many books. It’s not whisky. It’s a… suspension. Made from bog myrtle, rowan berries picked at the cross-quarter days, and the tears of a man who genuinely regretted something. Which makes it rarer than diamond dust.” She uncorked it. The smell was overwhelming: peat smoke, crushed green things, and a sharp, coppery tang like blood on the tongue. “You drink this, and for a time, you become a question mark. Your story… resists punctuation. It’s a hiccup in the grammar of reality. Ends slide off you. Conclusions find no purchase.”

“How long?” Isla asked, her eyes on the ominous liquid.

“Long enough to be profoundly irritating,” Morag said. “Not long enough to be safe. It’s a shield, not an armour. And it has a price. While you’re in it, you’re… unfixed. You might remember things that never happened. You might forget your own name. The world might try to write *other* stories on you, just to see what sticks.”

Isla took the bottle. It was warm, as if something inside was fermenting slowly, perpetually. “A living draft,” she murmured.

“Aye. Now, the map.” Morag jabbed a thick finger at the vellum. “It shows the way *to* her door. But her Salon isn’t on the other side of a normal door. It’s in the interstitial glue. The *what-comes-after* of things. To get in, you need to cross a threshold that’s already closed. You need a finality.”

Leo held up the dismantled astrolabe. From its guts, he extracted a delicate, spiralling filament of brass. “A closure key,” he said. “We tune it to a specific, completed story. We use it to unlock a door that only appears once the story is over. Then, we step through into the space that story left behind.”

“What story?” Isla asked.

Leo wouldn’t meet her eye. “Mine.”

He placed a small, cloth-wrapped bundle on the table. Unfolded, it revealed a child’s composition book, its cover stained with what looked like Ribena. A pressed hawthorn flower, brown and fragile, was tucked inside. Next to it, a single, worn-out football sock.

“The story of Leo Henderson,” he said, his voice stripped of its usual academic varnish. “Age eight. Concluded. The boy who believed in magic, who tried to summon a wood-spirit to make his dad come back from his ‘business trip’ in Aberdeen. The boy who failed. The boy who decided, with the absolute finality only a child can muster, that magic was a lie for the desperate, and that the only real things were facts, dates, and catalogues. That story ended. That boy was filed away. This,” he gestured to the pathetic relics, “is the cover of that book. Slammed shut.”

The room held its breath. Isla looked at the relics—not as artefacts, but as tombstones. This was Leo’s void. His mould. He had built his entire adult self, the meticulous archivist, around this one, curated conclusion.

“You’re going to use your own finished story as a ticket,” Isla said, understanding dawning with a cold horror. “You’re going to re-open it.”

“To create a door,” he nodded. “The moment we activate the key with these, a threshold will appear. The threshold of ‘Leo’s Childhood Belief’. It’s a finality. It’s exactly the kind of thing she traffics in. It should get us to the antechamber of her Salon.”

“And then?” Morag asked, her arms crossed.

“Then,” Isla said, picking up the needle once more, “I present my unfinished self. And while she’s… tasting the vintage, I use this. Not on her. On her collection.”

Leo blinked. “What?”

“You said it yourself. She’s the curator. The Salon *is* her. Its integrity is her power. What if I don’t try to stab the curator? What if I… re-catalogue the collection? Introduce an error. File a tragedy under ‘comedies’. Cross-reference a love story with a tax ledger.” She looked at the living map, at the void. “I introduce *draftiness*. A draft in a sealed room causes a terrible stir.”

Morag let out a low whistle. “Now you’re thinking like a proper anarchist, hen. Not breaking the windows, but fucking with the filing system so nothing can ever be found again. That’s a deeper mess.”

They worked through what remained of the day and into the Glasgow night. The *Beatha na h-Òige* was a fire in Isla’s veins, a confusing, vibrant storm. She felt unmoored. She looked at her hand and saw, for a flicker, an old woman’s hand, spotted and thin. She heard a snatch of a lullaby in Morag’s grumble, a song she was certain she’d never heard. She was a sentence missing its full stop, the words beginning to drift and rearrange.

Leo tuned his brass key, the process involving whispered incantations in dog-Latin and the careful heating of the filament over a candle fed with wax from a church dedicated to a deconsecrated saint. The air in the back room grew thick, charged with the scent of juniper, metal, and the cloying sweetness of the *Beatha*.

Finally, it was time. The map was rolled. The needle was sheathed in a lead tube Isla wore on a cord around her neck. The bottle of suspension was half-empty. Isla felt like a walking contradiction, a riot of potential shaped like a woman.

Leo held his childhood relics in one hand, the glowing closure key in the other. He stood in the centre of the room, near the stain on the floorboards that no scrub brush could lift. “This will work,” he said, to himself. “It’s a sound theoretical framework.”

“Theories are like concepts, son,” Morag said, not unkindly. “They just are, or they aren’t.”

He closed his eyes. He whispered a word. A name, perhaps. Isla didn’t catch it.

The key in his hand flared with a soft, gold light. The relics—the book, the flower, the sock—dissolved into motes of dust that swirled around the light, weaving into it. Before them, in the air above the permanent stain, a door began to paint itself.

It was not a solid door. It was the *memory* of a door. The ghost of a door that had once existed in a council house in Easterhouse, the door to a bedroom where a boy had cried himself to sleep and decided to be done with wonder. It was translucent, etched in light and regret. Through it, Isla could see vague shapes—a faded superhero poster, a model aeroplane hanging from a ceiling—but they were insubstantial as mist.

“The threshold of a finality,” Leo breathed, his face etched with a profound, personal pain. He was holding open a wound.

“Go on,” Morag said, giving Isla a shove that was almost gentle. “Before the lad’s heart breaks clean in two.”

Isla stepped forward. The world bent. The smell of *The Hanged Man*—stale beer, bleach, Morag’s perfume—was replaced by the scent of damp plaster, Imperial Leather soap, and the particular, piercing sadness of a hope that has been carefully, deliberately buried.

She passed through the door.

There was no corridor. One step she was in the back room of a pub, the next she was standing on a floor of polished black marble, veined with silver. The air was cool, dry, and utterly silent. It was the silence of a museum after hours, of a library in the dead of night—a silence that felt cultivated, expensive.

She was in a long, narrow gallery. The walls were a matte, dark grey. Set into them at regular intervals were shallow, illuminated niches. Each niche contained a single object, spotlit from above.

To her left, a perfectly ordinary china teacup, with a hairline crack. A card beneath it read: *First Marital Disappointment. J. & M., 1972.*
Further down, a child’s first tooth. *End of Innocence. A., 1998.*
A wedding ring, bent. *The Alternative Life. S., 1984.*
A single, grey woollen glove. *The One Who Got Away. Unknown.*

It was a gallery of endings. Small, personal, devastating finalities. Curated with a chilling, aesthetic precision. The silence here was not empty; it was thick with the echoes of these concluded stories.

Isla walked, the *Beatha* in her blood making her feel like a splash of colour on a greyscale photograph. She was the draft walking amongst the published, leather-bound volumes. She could feel the Salon resisting her presence. The air pushed back, a subtle pressure trying to force her into a narrative shape, to find her file.

At the end of the gallery was an archway, leading into a larger space. The light there was different—warmer, mimicking afternoon sun. She could see the edges of wingback chairs, the glint of glass.

This was the main Salon.

She took a deep breath, her hand finding the lead tube at her neck. She was unfixed. Unfinished. A question.

She stepped through the arch.

The room was a perfect replica of a 19th-century gentleman’s study, if that study had been designed by a god of closure. Floor-to-ceiling bookshelves held not books, but objects—thousands of them, each a curated finale. A fire crackled in an immense marble fireplace, though it gave no heat. In a chair by the fire, facing away from her, sat the supervisor.

She was wearing the green shoes. Emerald silk, the exact colour Isla had seen in the reflection.

“You’re late,” the supervisor said, her voice not unkind. It was the voice of a headmistress, a librarian, a surgeon. A voice used to stating facts about irreversible things.

“I’m a draft,” Isla replied, her own voice sounding strange to her, layered with echoes of other women, other possibilities. “Drafts are never late. They just are, or they aren’t.”

The supervisor’s chair slowly swivelled.

Isla had expected many things. A monster. A goddess. A withered crone. The supervisor was none of these. She was a woman in her late fifties, perhaps. Handsome. Severe grey hair swept into a flawless chignon. She wore a tailored suit of charcoal wool. Her face was intelligent, calm, with eyes the colour of flint. She looked like the most formidable academic Isla had ever seen.

“Isla MacLeod,” the supervisor said, looking her up and down. A slight frown touched her lips. “You are… smudged.”

“I’m unfinished.”

“So I see. You have ingested something. A resistance. How very proactive.” She steepled her fingers. “You have brought me a new submission?”

“Myself.”

The supervisor’s flint eyes gleamed with genuine interest. “The living subject as contested text. An intriguing proposal. But ultimately, a flawed one. Every life has an ending. I simply help it find its most… elegant form.” She gestured to the room. “I provide context. Resolution. I take the messy, screaming draft of a life and I give it a full stop. It is a mercy.”

“It’s a theft,” Isla said, the *Beatha* making her bold, her words slipping their leash. “You steal the potential. You trade in might-have-beens.”

“I preserve *what was*,” the supervisor corrected, standing. She moved to a shelf, running a finger along the spine of what looked like a violin with a broken neck. “The might-have-been is a phantom. It causes pain. I am a surgeon of the soul, Ms. MacLeod. I amputate the phantom limb.” She turned, her gaze sharp. “You, for instance. Your phantom limb is your thesis. Your ‘gendered cartography’. It was going nowhere. It was a messy, emotional, *unscholarly* draft. I gave it a clean ending. I filed it under ‘Academic Ambition: Unrealised’. It is at peace now. And you are free to move on to something more suitable.”

The words were meant to cut, to finalise. But Isla felt them slide off the suspension in her blood. They couldn’t find a purchase. “I don’t want peace. I want the mess. I want the draft.”

“Then you are a glutton for suffering.” The supervisor took a step closer. “And you are in the wrong gallery. This is a place for finished works.”

Isla felt the pressure increase. The Salon was trying to edit her. She saw flickers at the edge of her vision—possible Islas. Isla the failed academic, weeping at a library desk. Isla the wife, smiling emptily at a husband. Isla the ghost, haunting *The Hanged Man*. The Salon was offering her endings, trying to bind her into a story she could be filed under.

She clutched the lead tube. It was time.

“You’re wrong,” Isla gasped, fighting the pressure. “The most powerful stories… are the ones without endings. The ones that keep being told. The ones that are drafts forever.”

“A romantic notion,” the supervisor sighed, as if disappointed. “And a false one. Now, let’s see what you really are.”

She reached out a hand, not to touch Isla, but to *pluck* at the air around her. Isla felt a terrifying tug, as if the very narrative of her self was being unspooled.

In that moment of violent attention, Isla yanked the lead tube free, tore out the supervisor’s needle, and instead of driving it forward, she spun and slammed its point into the nearest shelf.

Into a small, framed photograph of a smiling soldier.

The effect was not a explosion, but a dissolution. The photograph didn’t shatter. It *blurred*. The soldier’ smile melted, reformed into a grimace, then a look of confusion. The frame rattled. The card beneath it, which read *Heroic Sacrifice. P., 1944*, flickered. The letters swam, rearranging. *Hesitant Choice… Regretful Duty… Possible Desertion…*

A low shudder ran through the Salon. The fire in the grate guttered.

The supervisor froze, her hand still outstretched. She turned her head, slowly, to look at the shelf. Her flint eyes widened, not with anger, but with a kind of pristine, professional horror.

“What,” she whispered, “have you done?”

Isla was already moving, the needle a dart in her hand. She stabbed it into a pressed rose labelled *First Love*. The rose bloomed, violently, in its case, petals bursting fresh and red, then withering to black dust, then blooming again in a frantic, endless, unresolved cycle.

She touched it to a sealed letter labelled *The Last Word*. The seal broke, the letter unfolded itself, and the words began to rewrite themselves, over and over, in different hands, in conflicting sentiments.

“Stop.” The supervisor’s voice was a crack of ice. The pressure in the room became physical, a weight trying to crush Isla to the floor.

But Isla was draft. She was potential. She slipped through the cracks in the certainty. She was a syntax error in the Salon’s perfect grammar. She ran along the shelves, the needle a wand of chaos, introducing typos, non-sequiturs, and maddening, looping footnotes into the collected works.

A sigh became a scream became a laugh.
A death became a departure became a delay.
A betrayal became a misunderstanding became a secret collaboration.

The Salon was no longer silent. It was a cacophony of rewriting stories, a pandemonium of altered endings. The air shimmered with conflicting narratives. The supervisor stood in the centre, her hands clenched at her sides. Her perfect Salon was unravelling. Her life’s work was being cross-referenced with madness.

“You can’t… this is anarchy!” the supervisor cried, her composure finally fracturing.

“It’s a *new draft*!” Isla shouted back, breathless, leaning against a shelf where a pocket watch labelled *Time Ran Out* was now ticking erratically, forwards and backwards.

The supervisor’s gaze locked onto her. The professional horror was gone, replaced by something raw and furious. “Then you will be the first exhibit I restore. I will finish you myself. I will write your ending in blood and bone, and it will be so final, so absolute, it will be a cornerstone of my new collection.”

She began to walk towards Isla, and as she did, she changed. The severe suit blurred. The handsome face sharpened, grew paler. The green shoes seemed to bleed their colour upwards, until she was clad in a gown of shimmering, venomous emerald silk. Her hair loosened, flowing like smoke. She was no longer the curator. She was the archetype. The Goddess of Closure. Her hands were empty, but Isla knew they could snap a story’s spine with a gesture.

The *Beatha* in Isla’s veins was fading. She could feel it—the unfixed, questioning state was wearing off. The world was beginning to harden around her again, to try and fix her in place. And before this furious, elemental version of the supervisor, a fixed place would be a coffin.

Isla turned and ran, not back towards the gallery of small endings, but deeper into the Salon, into the maze of shelves. The supervisor followed, her passage causing the rewritten stories to whimper and still, as if her very presence re-imposed a temporary, terrible order.

Isla was trapped in a library of endings, being hunted by the librarian. Her resistance was fading. Her draft was about to be submitted.

And in the pocket of her jacket, the living map, responding to the chaos, to the proximity of so much curated finality, grew warm. The void at its centre—the void of the Salon itself—began to pulse. Not like a silence anymore.

Like a heartbeat.

Like an invitation to a deeper, darker precipice.



---

# Chapter 18

## **CHAPTER 18**

The map in her pocket was a second heart, beating a counter-rhythm to her own frantic pulse. It was not a comfort. It was a demand. The void at its centre, the Salon’s own curated absence, had become a black star, pulling at her. Isla ran, her boots whispering over the marble that was no longer cold but fever-warm, as if the Salon were alive with a sickly, institutional heat.

Behind her, the susurration of emerald silk was a sound like pages being torn, one after another. The supervisor—no, the *Curator* now, in her true aspect—did not hurry. She walked. Her pace was the implacable advance of a final chapter. The air around her grew heavy, settled, *resolved*. Isla felt the *Beatha*’s last, defiant question in her blood being answered by that presence, a flat, declarative ‘no’.

“You cannot outrun a conclusion, Isla,” the Curator’s voice echoed, not loud, but perfectly clear, as if spoken inside Isla’s own skull. It had shed its bureaucratic crispness. It was a voice of velvet-wrapped stone. “Every path here leads to an end. You are merely choosing the scenery for yours.”

The maze of shelves turned treacherous. Where before they had held a chaotic potential, now they seemed to align against her. A shelf labelled *Lovers Parted by War* swung out, forcing her to stumble into an aisle where the air was thick with the scent of cordite and forgotten perfume. A case of *Small, Unmarked Graves* seemed to lengthen before her, its dirt-caked exhibits whispering of quiet, anonymous surrender. The Salon was no longer a repository. It was an instrument, and the Curator was its player.

Isla’s breath sawed in her throat. The *Beatha* was gone, fully metabolised into a crashing fatigue. The glorious, unfixed state—the ability to be a question mark in a world of full stops—had evaporated. She was solidifying. With every step, her own story felt more written, the margins narrowing. She could feel the weight of her own past decisions, her academic failures, her flight from Glasgow, settling on her shoulders like a leaden shawl. The Curator’s gift was to make you see your life not as a narrative, but as an argument—and one you were losing.

She ducked into a cul-de-shelf marked *Domestic Dissolutions*. The air here was close, smelling of stale tea and unsaid words. Miniature dioramas showed couples frozen at kitchen tables, their silence a tangible, jagged thing. Isla pressed her back against the wood, the living map burning against her thigh. Its pulse said: *deeper, go deeper*. The void was not just at the Salon’s heart. It was a potential in her own chest. A way out, or a way in.

The emerald silk whispered to a halt at the aisle entrance.

“There you are,” the Curator murmured. Isla risked a glance. The goddess stood silhouetted, her form both terribly sharp and unnervingly fluid. Her hands were held before her, fingers moving as if she were knitting with invisible yarn. With each motion, the aisle behind Isla seemed to grow shorter, the shelves at its end sealing together with a soft, final *click* like a book closing. “This one has a certain aptness, don’t you think? Not with a bang, but a whimper. The slow, quiet ending that happens in rooms everyone has left.”

Isla’s mind, trained for patterns, for the architecture of unseen things, scrabbled. The Curator was closure. She was the period, the full stop, the ‘The End’ stitched in gold thread. Her power was narrative finality. Isla had fought with chaos, with the draft, with the un-made. And she had lost. The *Beatha* was a temporary rebellion. This was the eternal regime.

But the map beat on. *Void. Precipice.*

And then she saw it—not with her eyes, but with the part of her that had always mapped the spaces between what was said and what was meant. The Curator’s power was absolute, but it had a grammar. It was *declarative*. It stated. It concluded. It was the voice of the published text, the mounted exhibit, the verdict. It was the voice that had dismissed her thesis, that had filed Morag away as a colourful anecdote, that had tried to fix Finch as a doddering fool. It was a masculine grammar wearing a feminine gown. It imposed order. It did not *listen*.

Isla had nothing left to declare. But she could still ask.

She pushed away from the shelf, standing straight in the dead-end aisle. She did not face the Curator with defiance, but with a raw, exhausted curiosity. Her voice, when it came, was not a shout, but a rasping question.

“Why do you get to choose the ending?”

The Curator’s knitting fingers stilled. A faint line appeared between her brows. The question was a pebble dropped into the still pond of her certainty. It was an improper form.

“It is my function,” the Curator stated. “My nature. I am the resolution of all narratives brought here.”

“Says who?” Isla asked, another question, stepping forward. The weight on her lessened, just a fraction. She was not arguing the conclusion. She was questioning the premise. “Who made you the arbiter? Who gave you the pen?”

“I *am* the pen,” the Curator said, but her tone had shifted minutely from statement to explanation. A crack in the grammar.

“No,” Isla said, understanding dawning like a cold, terrible light. “You’re the hand that holds it. But the hand was taught. Who taught you to end stories this way? To pin them down, to drain the blood out and leave the pretty, mounted husk?” She took another step. The domestic dioramas around her seemed to lean in, their silent couples listening. “You end every romance with a parting or a death. Every quest with a hollow prize or a fallen hero. Every rebellion with a crushing defeat or a corrupted victory. It’s so… *predictable*. It’s not closure. It’s just a tired habit.”

The Curator’s face, a masterpiece of composed finality, flickered. For an instant, Isla saw not a goddess, but the furious, handsome supervisor, over-invested in her cataloguing. “I impose meaning on chaos. I give them shape. Without my ending, they are nothing. Noise.”

“Without *your* ending, they might become something else,” Isla pressed. The map was a furnace now. The void-pulse synced with her own heart. She was at the precipice. “You don’t finish stories. You *interrupt* them. You see a woman running and you call it a flight, never a pursuit. You see a woman asking questions and you call it a flaw, never a method. You see a woman like Morag, who lives in the glorious, messy draft of her own life, and you’d file her under ‘Local Colour’ and close the drawer. You’re not the goddess of endings. You’re the goddess of *interruptions*. And you were hired for the job.”

The air trembled. The shelves rattled. The Curator’s emerald gown seemed to dim. “You dare…”

“I *question*,” Isla snarled, finding a last reservoir of strength in the act. It was her only weapon. It was the one thing the declarative voice could not assimilate. “Who built this place? Who set the rules? Was it a ‘he’? Was it a man who thought stories were things to be solved, like equations, and who thought women in stories were problems to be resolved—by marriage, by death, by silence?”

The accusation hung in the thick air. The Curator did not move, but Isla saw the truth in the sudden, perfect stillness. It was a confirmation. This place, this terrible, beautiful library of finalities, was built on a foundation of a single, arrogant assumption: that a story was a possession to be completed, not a conversation to be had. And the Curator, for all her power, was its chief librarian, enforcing a canon she did not create.

It was a false victory, this understanding. It did not disarm the goddess. It enraged her.

The flicker of doubt vanished, swallowed by a tsunami of cold, professional wrath. “You insignificant *draft*,” the Curator whispered. The words were ice. “You think your critique matters? You think naming the architect changes the prison? I am the law here. And your insubordination merits a definitive edit.”

Her hands came up, not knitting now, but shaping—as if she were crumpling a sheet of paper. The space around Isla began to compress. The air itself hardened, pressing in on her ribs, seeking to fix her final pose: crouched, defeated, in a dead-end aisle. This was not a narrative ending anymore. This was a deletion.

The pressure was immense. Isla’s bones groaned. She couldn’t breathe. The world became a narrowing tunnel, and at its end was the Curator’s cold, finalising gaze. She had seen the deeper truth, and it had shattered any hope of reprieve. The system was rigged. The house always won. The critic with the pen always got the last word.

Her hand, crushed against her side, felt the map. It wasn’t just warm; it was vibrating, a hum that travelled up her arm. The void at its centre wasn’t pulsing. It was *gaping*.

The Curator’s ending was a wall. The void was a hole.

There was no choice. To be fixed here was to become an exhibit. A little diorama: *The Girl Who Asked Too Much*. Preserved forever in the moment of her silencing.

Isla stopped resisting the pressure. Instead, with the last of her will, she reached into her pocket and wrapped her fingers around the living map. She didn’t pull it out. She *pushed* into it. She focused not on the inked lines of Edinburgh, but on the perfect, consuming blackness at its heart—the representation of the Salon’s own un-recorded core.

She offered it a question, her final one: *What’s in the dark?*

The map answered.

The void erupted. It did not swallow her; it unfolded. The pressure, the aisle, the shelves of *Domestic Dissolutions*, the emerald glow of the Curator—all of it tore like wet paper. Isla fell, but not down. *In*. Into the heartbeat.

There was a sound like a universe sighing, and then there was silence. Not the curated silence of the Salon, but a deep, primeval quiet. And darkness.

She landed on her hands and knees. The surface was not marble, nor earth. It was soft, cool, and slightly resilient, like packed ash. The air was still and scentless. The oppressive, narrative weight of the Salon was gone. In its place was a profound, unsettling neutrality.

Isla gasped, drawing breath into her aching lungs. She was unfixed again, but not by the *Beatha*. This was different. This was not a state of potential, but a state of *absence*. She looked up.

She was in the heart of the Salon. The void on the map.

It was not a room. It was a cavern, so vast the ceiling and walls were lost in a gloom that seemed to drink the light. And the light came from the only features in the emptiness: thousands upon thousands of faint, shimmering threads. They hung from the unseen ceiling, drifting gently like ghostly kelp in a still sea. Each thread glowed with a soft, dying light—silver, gold, pale blue, rose. At the end of each thread, suspended a few feet above the ashen floor, was an object.

A single, frozen tear. A wedding ring, cracked in two. A key, bent beyond use. A child’s wooden soldier, its paint worn away. A lock of hair, grey and braided. A fountain pen, snapped. A symphony score with the final bar missing.

Isla stood, her legs trembling. She walked slowly, her boots making no sound on the ash. She passed under a thread from which dangled a pair of spectacles, one lens shattered. From another, a dried sprig of heather. From another, a soldier’s medal, green with tarnish.

This was not the gallery of endings. This was the repository of what was *cut*. The moments deemed too messy, too unresolved, too *un-final* for the Curator’s collections. The discarded drafts of emotion. The ambiguous moments that refused to cohere into a clean moral. The unanswered questions. The tears shed not in catharsis, but in confusion. The love that did not end in marriage or death, but simply… changed. The courage that looked like fear. The defiance that looked like silence.

This was the subtext the Curator’s narratives had erased. The *her*story filed under ‘irrelevant’.

And at the very centre of this forest of severed threads, there was a clearer space. A single thread hung there, thicker than the others, glowing with a weak, stubborn light. It was not silver or gold, but the colour of old whisky. At its end was not an object, but a small, shimmering, amorphous cloud—a condensation of memory and feeling. As Isla approached, she heard a faint echo, a voice both familiar and almost forgotten: *“The whisky was a peat-bog ghost, haunting the glass…”*

Her own voice. The opening line of her own story, the one she’d lived since Glasgow. It was here. Suspended. Unfinished.

A soft footfall echoed in the ash.

Isla turned.

She was not alone in the void.

A figure sat on a low, rough-hewn stool made of dark wood, beside a thread from which hung a thimble. She was mending a shawl, her needle moving in and out of the grey wool with a slow, timeless rhythm. She was old, older than Morag, her face a landscape of gentle wrinkles, her hair a long, silver cascade over her shoulders. She wore simple clothes of undyed wool. Her eyes, when she looked up, were the colour of a winter sky just before snow.

“She cannot come here,” the old woman said. Her voice was the sound of pages being turned softly, one at a time. “Closure has no jurisdiction in the Unfinished.”

“Who are you?” Isla asked, her voice small in the immense quiet.

“I have had many names in many stories,” the woman said, returning to her mending. “The One Who Remembers. The Keeper of Loose Ends. The Spinner Who Unravels. You might call me the Archivists’ Neglect.” A faint, sorrowful smile touched her lips. “I am what she edits out.”

Isla looked around at the hanging threads, the galaxy of cut moments. “All of this?”

“All of this. Every emotion that did not fit the arc. Every character who refused their assigned role. Every middle that was too interesting to be resolved. She harvests the endings, but she is wasteful. She throws away the best parts.” The old woman knotted her thread and bit it off with teeth that were still strong and white. “The doubts. The questions. The ‘what ifs’. They have a power she does not understand. A sustaining power.”

“They’re… alive?”

“In a manner of speaking. They are *potential*. A story that is truly finished is dead. It is a specimen. But a story with a loose thread…” She gestured to the forest around them. “It can yet be pulled. It can unravel. It can become something else.”

Isla looked at the whisky-coloured thread, her thread. “Why is mine here?”

“Because you are a question, Isla MacLeod. You always have been. Your thesis was a question. Your flight was a question. Your draft was a glorious, chaotic question. She could not end you because she could not *answer* you. So she was going to delete you. A messy, inelegant solution to a problem she could not categorise.” The old woman set her mending aside. “But you asked the right question of the map. You asked what was in the dark. And the dark is where she does not look. It is beneath her notice.”

A terrible, wonderful hope began to ache in Isla’s chest. “Can I leave? Can I get out of the Salon?”

“You already are ‘out’,” the woman said. “You are in the place she built this palace upon, and then sealed away. The foundation of all stories. The muddle. The draft.” She stood, her movements fluid. “But you cannot stay. The Unfinished is not a place for the living. It is a limbo. To stay is to become a thread yourself—a beautiful, suspended potential that never touches the world.”

“Then what do I do?”

The old woman walked to Isla’s thread. She did not touch it, but she breathed upon the shimmering cloud at its end. The cloud solidified, just for a moment, into an image: Morag, behind the bar of *The Hanged Man*, polishing a glass. She looked up, directly at Isla, and winked.

“You pull the thread,” the old woman said. “You pick up your own story. But you must do it consciously. You must take all of this with you.” She swept a hand around the cavern. “Not the objects. The *principle*. The question. The right to be unresolved. The courage of the loose end. If you go back out there with only your own anger, she will crush you. Her grammar is too strong. But if you go out carrying the weight of all she has discarded… you become something new. You are no longer a draft.”

“What am I?”

“A counter-narrative.”

The words hung in the ash-scented air. Isla looked from the old woman’s calm, winter-grey eyes to the countless glowing threads. She thought of all the stories trimmed to fit, all the lives edited for coherence, all the women forced into endings that were not their own. The weight of it was oceanic. It would crush her.

“I can’t,” she whispered.

“You already are,” the old woman replied. “You walked in here. You saw her truth. You carry the *Beatha* in your memory, and the map in your hand. You are a scholar of liminal spaces. This…” She gestured to the void. “This is the ultimate liminal space. The space *between the lines*. Your thesis was not wrong. It was just… small.”

Isla laughed, a short, desperate sound. It echoed among the threads, and some of them brightened in response. The sound of unresolved laughter.

“How?” she asked.

The old woman pointed to the floor at Isla’s feet. The ash was stirring. It was not being blown by any wind, but was moving of its own accord, swirling and rising. It coalesced, not into an object, but into a shape. A door. A simple, wooden doorframe, standing freely in the ash. Through it, Isla saw not the cavern, but a blur of other places, other times—a rain-slicked Glasgow street, the heather on a hillside, the warm, peat-smoke gloom of *The Hanged Man*. The door was a question made manifest.

“Walk through,” the old woman said. “But do not go to a place. Go to a *moment*. A moment that was not an ending. A moment that was a beginning disguised as something else. Your thread will follow you. And the others… they will know. They will resonate.”

Isla stepped towards the door. She placed her hand on the rough wood of the frame. It felt real. Solid. An anchor.

She looked back at the Keeper of Loose Ends. “What will happen to you? When I go?”

The old woman had retrieved her mending. She smiled, and this time it was a smile of deep, timeless satisfaction. “I will continue my work. She will continue hers. It is the oldest story. But perhaps, if you are loud enough, if your counter-narrative is sufficiently… *irritating*… she will drop more threads. My collection will grow.”

Isla nodded. She turned to the door. She thought not of escape, but of a specific moment. The moment in the Glasgow pub, after Finch’s letter, when Morag had slid the glass of whisky towards her and said, *“Concepts are never late. They just are, or they aren’t.”* It was not an answer. It was a permission slip.

She took a deep breath of the scentless air, and stepped through the door.

The world did not spin. It *stitched*.

One moment she was in the ash-filled void, the next she was standing in the middle of an aisle in the Salon of Finality. But it was not the same.

The emerald glow of the Curator was before her, the goddess’s hands outstretched, frozen in the act of crumpling the space where Isla had just been. Her face was a mask of triumphant finality. Time had not passed. Isla’s flight into the void, her conversation, had happened in a space between seconds.

Isla stood, solid, real. The map in her pocket was cool and inert, its work done. But she was different. She felt the weight the old woman had spoken of—not a burden, but a density. She carried the silence of the void within her. She was a walking loose end.

The Curator’s eyes focused. The triumph shattered into pure, unprofessional shock. “You… you are *fixed*. You should be…”

“I am,” Isla said. Her voice was quiet, but it did not echo. It absorbed sound. “I’m fixed as a question. A question with consequences.”

She did not attack. She did not run. She simply began to walk, not away from the Curator, but past her, down the aisle towards the heart of the Salon. And as she walked, she did what the Curator could not. She *listened*.

She listened to the whimper of the rewritten stories on the shelves. But now, she heard not just their pain, but their stubborn, unfinished core—the love that remained in the betrayal, the hope that lingered in the defeat. She heard the subtext.

And the Salon heard *her*.

Where her feet touched the marble, the perfect, glossy surface grew faint lines, like cracks, or like the beginnings of handwriting. The shelves she passed shuddered. A book labelled *The Hero’s Fall* fell open, and a new paragraph, messy and ink-smudged, began to write itself at the bottom of the final page. A diorama of *The Bitter Parting* flickered, and for a second, the two figures turned not away from each other, but looked back, their faces etched with a complicated regret that was not an ending, but a change.

The Curator stared, aghast. “Stop.” The word was a command, but it cracked in the middle.

Isla did not stop. She was pulling her thread. And hers was not the only one. A faint, silver light began to seep from the shelves, from the cases, from the very air. It was the light from the void, leaking back into the finished world. Loose ends seeking their stories.

“You are contaminating the collection!” the Curator shrieked. The goddess aspect was fraying, revealing the furious, meticulous bureaucrat beneath. She raised her hands, not to knit or crumple, but to claw. She lunged at Isla, her fingers aimed to tear, to physically rend this anomaly from her perfect system.

Isla turned to face her. She did not raise her hands in defence. She just looked at the Curator, and in her winter-grey eyes (had they always been that colour?) was the patience of the ash, the silence of the void, the immense, quiet weight of everything the Curator had ever thrown away.

The Curator’s clawing hands stopped an inch from Isla’s face. They trembled. She could not touch her. Isla was no longer a story to be ended. She was a condition. A critical footnote that had swollen to consume the text.

“You’re not the author,” Isla said softly. “You’re just a critic with a very sharp pen.”

She turned and walked on, leaving the Curator standing, frozen, in an aisle that was now subtly, irrevocably *unfinished*.

Isla walked towards the centre of the Salon, following the now-warm pulse of her own thread. She knew where it led. Not out. Not yet. To the source. To the place where her story had been intercepted, where the Curator had first tried to write her ending. She had a debt to pay, and a draft to recover.

Around her, the Salon of Finality whispered with new, unapproved potential. Endings were unspooling into middles. The grammar was breaking down.

And high above, in her office of polished despair, the Curator lowered her shaking hands. She looked at them as if they were foreign objects. Then she looked at the spreading stain of ambiguity infecting her perfect aisles. A single, perfect tear of rage—a tear that would have made a beautiful, definitive exhibit—welled in her eye.
