But before it could fall, it hesitated. It held within it a flicker of doubt, a terrifying, unresolved question: *What if I am wrong?*

It was the first loose thread in her own impeccable story.

She blinked it away, viciously. But it was too late. The thread had been spun. It hung in the air between heartbeats, a tiny, shimmering filament of chaos.

She turned her gaze towards the retreating back of Isla MacLeod, a figure now glowing faintly with borrowed void-light, walking into the heart of the museum.

“This,” the Curator whispered, the word a vow, a curse, and a promise, “is not over.”

But for the first time in eternity, it sounded less like a statement of fact, and more like a hope.



---

# Chapter 19

## **CHAPTER 19**

The centre of the Salon of Finality was not a place, but a process. A slow, cold digestion. Isla walked towards it, the soles of her boots making no sound on the marble that was now the colour of a fading bruise. The void-light she carried was not an illumination; it was an exposure. It did not show things as they were, but as they had been interrupted. The polished cases lining the aisles gleamed with a sickly, hesitant sheen. Within them, the curated endings—the knight’s last breath, the city’s final stone, the love letter’s unread signature—stirred. Not with life, but with the memory of motion. A suspended animation was thawing into a terrible, unresolved present participle.

Her own thread, warm and insistent as a vein in her wrist, pulled her onward. It did not lead to a grand arch or a vaulted dome. It led to a service door. A plain, grey metal door set into an alcove, so mundane it was the most shocking thing in the museum. The label beside it, in small, functional type, read: *Acquisitions & Initial Processing*.

This was where stories were brought to be stripped. Before they were mounted, before they were labelled, this was the abattoir of narrative.

The door was unlocked. It opened onto a corridor of sterile white tile and humming fluorescent lights. The smell was antiseptic, overlaying something older and more metallic. The silence here was different from the hush of the galleries. This was the silence of a vacuum jar, of something being held in perfect, airless stillness. The Curator’s aesthetic of beautiful despair did not extend back here. This was pure function. This was the machinery behind the art.

Isla’s thread trembled, a plucked string, and led her to a room on the left.

It was a laboratory. Or a taxidermist’s workshop. Steel tables ran down its centre, under the glare of surgical lamps. On one table lay the gossamer ghost of a covenant between kingdoms, pinned at its corners by crystal weights, a scalpel resting beside a clause that had been deemed ‘redundant’. On another, the shimmering, liquid shadow of a forgotten sea god was being siphoned into a series of glass vials, each labelled with a date of irrelevance. The tools were not hammers and chisels, but fine-tuned instruments of critique, of deconstruction: a thesaurus that replaced vibrant words with pallid synonyms, a set of calipers for measuring emotional resonance, a silver needle and thread for suturing shut plot holes with elegant, suffocating closure.

At the far end of the room, under the brightest light, was her story.

It was laid out like a patient on an autopsy slab. Not the physical manuscript from her bag—that was gone, consumed—but the living shape of it. She saw the topography of her thesis, the rolling hills of her research on the *bean nighe* and the *gruagach*, the sharp cliffs of her arguments about gendered passage. She saw the coastline of her personal journey, the inlets of grief for her mother, the stubborn headlands of her own defiance. It was all there, rendered in faint, gold-and-silver light, a shimmering cartography pinned to the steel.

And it had been edited.

Black lines, thick and final as tar, scored through entire passages. Margins were filled with a tight, vicious script that she recognized from the Curator’s labels: *Sentimental. Derivative. Structurally unsound. Inconsequential.* A great, black ‘X’ had been drawn over the entire central chapter—the chapter about her mother’s stories, about the way women pass knowledge not in books, but in whispers, in lullabies, in the quiet shaping of bread dough. That chapter was marked: *Anecdotal. Lacks rigour. The personal is not universal.*

But they had not finished. A long, silver instrument, like a poet’s stylus dipped in ink made of nothingness, hovered over the final section. The conclusion. Where she had dared to suggest that a liminal space, traditionally a place of danger for women, could be re-mapped as a place of agency. That the door left ajar could be a threshold one *chose* to cross, not a trap one fell into. The stylus was poised to write the ultimate critique: *THE END.*

Isla did not shout. She did not run. She walked to the table, her void-light flaring, pushing back against the sterile glare. She placed her hands on the cold steel, on either side of her shimmering, condemned story.

“I’ll be taking that back,” she said, her voice calm in the humming room.

The stylus did not move. But from the shadows behind the table, a figure resolved. Not the Curator. This was a Preservationist. A tall, slender being in a dove-grey smock, its face a smooth, androgynous oval devoid of features save for two dark lenses where eyes might be. It held the stylus with long, delicate fingers. It did not speak. It tilted its head, the lenses focusing on Isla, then on the void-light that haloed her. A low, clicking sound emanated from it, like a clockwork beetle assessing a threat.

*It is scheduled for termination,* a voice said, not in the air, but directly inside Isla’s mind. The voice was dry, precise, and cold. *The analysis is complete. The narrative is flawed. Its preservation would introduce instability.*

“Your analysis is wrong,” Isla said, meeting the blank lenses. “You measured the wrong things. You looked for universals in the particulars. You searched for rigour in the rhythm of a breath.”

*The metrics are absolute. Emotion is a variable to be controlled. Personal history is bias. This narrative is a failed draft.*

“It’s not a draft,” Isla said, her fingers curling against the steel. “It’s a testament. And it’s mine.”

She reached for the shimmering map of her story. Her hands passed through the light. It was like dipping them into a cold stream. She felt the texture of her own thoughts, the ache of her research, the warmth of her mother’s voice woven through the text. She began to gather it, not to roll it up like a scroll, but to *re-collect* it. To pull it back into the vessel of herself.

The Preservationist moved. It was faster than thought. The silver stylus lanced down, not towards the story, but towards Isla’s hands, aiming to pin them to the table with a period of final pain.

Isla did not pull back. She turned her right hand, the one that had plunged into the void, the one still faintly streaming with borrowed negation. She caught the stylus.

There was no clash. No spark. The stylus, designed to inscribe endings onto the essence of things, met the void-light and… hesitated. It could not find a purchase. It could not define the edge of Isla’s hand. She was, in that moment, an unended thing. A woman in the middle of her sentence.

She tightened her grip. The void-light swirled up the stylus, a creeping frost of anti-meaning. The Preservationist made a sound like a shattering wine glass inside her skull. It released the tool and stumbled back, its blank lenses flickering with static, with chaotic, unprocessed data.

Isla took the stylus. It was heavier than it looked, dense with intent. She looked at it, then at her story on the table. Then she did not break the stylus. She did not throw it away.

She used it.

Leaning over the table, she began to write. Not in the margins. Not as a correction. She wrote directly over the black, tar-like deletions. She wrote with the void-light that now infused the instrument, a silver-black ink that did not obscure, but *converted*. Where the Curator had written *Sentimental*, Isla wrote ** *Felt*. Where *Derivative* was scrawled, she inscribed * *Inherited*. Over *Structurally unsound*, she layered * *Built to withstand*. She wrote not in sentences, but in pulses. In breaths. She wrote the memory of Morag’s aubergine hair and battle-cloth. She wrote the taste of peat-bog ghost whisky at ten in the morning. She wrote the weight of the *geata* stone in her pocket, and the sound of a man who was a concept laughing softly in a dusty office.

She was not repairing her thesis. She was fortifying it. She was grafting the living, uncurated details of her journey—the very details the Curator would discard as clutter—back into its bones. She was making it *specific*. She was making it *hers*.

And as she wrote, the story on the table changed. The gold-and-silver light deepened, gaining the warmth of honey and the cool sheen of a Glasgow rain. The black scoring lines didn’t vanish; they became part of the texture, the scars that proved a thing had been fought for. The map of her story grew, not in size, but in depth. It became less an argument to be assessed, and more a territory to be inhabited.

The Preservationist watched, its internal mechanisms emitting a low, distressed whine. It could not process this. This was not editing. This was contamination. This was a narrative refusing its assigned category.

From the doorway, a voice spoke. It was not the dry, mental voice of the Preservationist. It was a voice of polished despair, now cracked with a hairline fracture of fury.

“Stop.”

The Curator stood in the threshold. She was immaculate, her suit sharp, her hair a frozen wave. But the single, perfect tear she had blinked away in the gallery had left a track. A tiny, almost invisible line down the powder on her cheek, like the first crack in a porcelain mask. In her hands, she held a new tool. It looked like a pair of shears, but the blades were made of crystallized silence, and the handles were wrapped in the leather of old, forgotten prologues.

“You are vandalizing a catalogued item,” the Curator said, her voice trembling with the effort of control.

Isla finished her last inscription. She straightened up, the void-stylus still in her hand. Her story, now glowing with a robust, defiant light, lifted from the table of its own accord and floated towards her, folding itself seamlessly back into her chest, into the space behind her ribs. It was not gone. It was integrated. She felt its weight, not as a burden, but as a foundation.

“It was never yours to catalogue,” Isla said. “You collect endings. I am in my middle.”

The Curator’s eyes flickered to the Preservationist, still shuddering in the corner. “You have infected my staff. You have introduced… variables.” She said the word like it was a disease. “This cannot be borne.”

“Then don’t bear it,” Isla said, taking a step forward. “Let it go. Your museum is a beautiful, terrible lie. You don’t preserve stories. You embalm them. You trade in the comfort of the full stop because you’re terrified of the ellipsis.”

The Curator’s knuckles whitened on the shears. “The ellipsis is chaos. It is suffering without resolution. It is hope deferred until it becomes a kind of torture. I offer peace. I offer certainty.”

“You offer a cage,” Isla shot back. “You saw my mother’s story—a woman waiting, a man lost at sea—and you called it a ‘quintessential tragedy of feminine patience.’ You pinned it like a butterfly. You never asked if her patience was active. If her waiting was a form of navigation. You never saw the stories she told me in the meantime, the world she built in the ellipsis. You just saw the ending you expected.”

For the first time, the Curator’s composure slipped. Not into rage, but into a flash of something raw and bewildered. “It… it is the cleaner narrative.”

“It’s the simpler one,” Isla corrected, her voice softening, almost pitying. “You’re not a guardian of stories. You’re a critic with a god complex. And you’re lonely. So lonely you’ve made a heaven out of a cemetery.”

The barb struck home. The Curator flinched as if struck. The crack in her mask widened. The shears in her hands lowered, just a fraction.

“I am order,” she whispered, but it sounded like a question.

“You’re a sentence,” Isla said. “A beautiful, perfectly-constructed, dead sentence.”

Isla moved then, not towards the Curator, but past her, towards the door. She had what she came for. More than she came for. The void-light around her was fading, its energy spent in the re-writing. She was just a woman again, but a woman who had reclaimed her own text.

The Curator did not move to stop her. She stood, holding her shears of crystallized silence, staring at the now-empty autopsy table. Staring at the space where a flawed, inconsequential story had been, and where now only the impression of its defiant, messy, unresolved life remained.

As Isla reached the corridor, the Curator spoke again, her voice hollow, echoing in the sterile room.

“Where will you go? This is all there is. The Salon of Finality is the last room in the house of existence.”

Isla paused at the threshold. She looked back, not at the Curator, but at the grey metal door to the galleries beyond. She thought of the *geata* stone, warm against her leg. She thought of the thread in her chest, which now pulsed with a new, forward momentum. Not back the way she came, but *onward*.

“You’re wrong,” Isla said, and her voice held the ghost of a smile, the first true one in what felt like centuries. “You told me yourself. This is a museum of *literary* endings. You only collect the ones that have been written down.”

She met the Curator’s wide, shattered eyes.

“My story isn’t finished being lived.”

She walked out into the corridor, leaving the Curator standing amidst the tools of her trade, the shears hanging uselessly at her side. The Curator looked at the Preservationist, who was now slowly, mechanically, trying to clean the empty table, wiping away the lingering resonance of Isla’s presence. It was a futile task. The resonance was not a stain. It was a scent. A perfume of peat smoke, whisky, and unresolved potential.

It would not come out.

The Curator’s gaze drifted to the doorway through which Isla had disappeared. The vow, the curse, the promise, died on her lips. All that was left was the terrifying, unresolved question, now grown too loud to blink away. It echoed in the silent, sterile room, in the humming of the lights, in the very marrow of her polished bones.

*What if I am wrong?*

***

The main gallery was different. The whisper had become a murmur. The aisles were no longer straight. They curved, subtly, as if around an unseen gravity. Exhibits were not quite as they had been. The knight’s final breath now seemed to hitch, as if on the verge of a second word. The city’s last stone had a hairline fracture, from which a tiny, stubborn shoot of something green and impossible was trying to emerge.

Isla paid them no mind. Her own thread was a compass needle, pulling her not towards the grand entrance, but deeper into the heart of the museum, towards a wing the Curator’s map had labelled *Restricted: Proto-Narratives & Unrealized Potential*.

The door to this wing was not grey metal. It was old oak, blackened with age, bound in iron that was red with rust. It was ajar.

On the threshold, Isla stopped. She could feel it. A draft. Not of air, but of *possibility*. It smelled of damp earth, of ozone before a storm, of ink not yet dry on a page. It was the smell of a story just beginning.

She looked back once. The Salon of Finality stretched behind her, its perfect order now softly, irrevocably warped. The Curator was nowhere to be seen. She was in her office, perhaps, staring at a single, hesitant tear, watching her world become ambiguous.

Isla turned her back on the endings. She pushed the oak door open.

Beyond was not a gallery. It was a landscape.

A twilight moor stretched before her, shrouded in mist that glowed with a soft, internal light. The sky was a deep, velvety purple, shot through with threads of silver like unspooled plotlines. In the distance, hills rolled like the spines of great, slumbering beasts. The path under her feet was not marble, but packed earth and heather. The air was cold, and alive.

This was the place before the story. The place of first inspiration, of gathering clouds, of the first note of a tune that had no name yet. It was chaos, but a fertile chaos. It was the rough draft of reality.

And there, in the middle of the path, waiting for her, was a figure.

He was sitting on a large, moss-covered stone, smoking a pipe. The smoke curled up into the mist, forming vague, shifting shapes—a castle, a ship, a face that melted into another. He wore a tweed jacket that was more patch than original fabric, and his eyes held the weary, knowing glint of a man who had read too many first chapters and not enough last pages.

It was Professor Alistair Finch. Or rather, it was the *concept* of him, the essence that had written the letter on pressed-bone paper. Here, in this place of raw potential, he was more real than he had ever been in *The Hanged Man*.

He took the pipe from his mouth and smiled, a network of wrinkles spreading like a map around his eyes.

“Took your time, hen,” he said, his voice the sound of pages turning in a quiet room. “I was starting to think you’d accepted the footnotes.”

Isla walked towards him, the weight of her reclaimed story solid and sure inside her. “I had some editing to do.”

He chuckled, nodding. “I can see that. You’ve got the look of a woman who’s just rewritten her own terms of engagement.” He gestured with his pipe stem towards the vast, murmuring moor behind him. “Welcome to the rough copy. Bit messy. Full of false starts and abandoned subplots. But it’s where the work is done.”

“Is this the way out?” Isla asked, though she already sensed the answer was more complex.

“Out?” Finch mused. “This isn’t a prison, lass. It’s a library. The Salon is one reading room. This… this is the writer’s shed. The way *on*.” He looked at her, his gaze piercing. “Your thread led you here. Not to an exit, but to a beginning. Specifically, I believe, to hers.”

He pointed.

There, by a small, dark pool that reflected the silver-threaded sky, stood a woman.

She was tall, with hair the colour of winter fire, streaked with ash. She wore simple, sturdy clothes, and she was staring into the water, her expression not one of sorrow, but of fierce, concentrated contemplation. In her hand, she held not a pen, but a smooth, grey stone from the shore.

Isla’s breath caught. She knew her. From a dozen half-remembered lullabies, from the shape left in a chair by the fire, from the feeling of hands braiding her hair.

It was her mother. Not as she was at the end, waiting. But as she was in her own middle. A woman in the process of inventing herself, of weaving a world from the threads of loss and landscape. This was the author of the stories that had shaped Isla’s bones. This was the source.

The debt was here. The draft to recover was not on paper. It was in this woman’s hands, in the story she was about to cast, like a stone, into the pool of the unrealized.

“She’s why you’re here,” Finch said softly. “Not to save her. You can’t. Her ending, in one form, is already written. But to *witness* her. To carry forward the story she began but never got to finish living. The Curator took that from you. From her. She tried to make it a closed loop. A tragedy.”

He knocked his pipe out against the stone. The embers died with a hiss.

“You have a choice, Isla MacLeod. You can take your reclaimed story and find a way back to your own world. It will be changed. You will be changed. But you can go. Or…”

He nodded towards the woman by the pool.

“You can step into the rough draft. You can meet her. Not as a daughter to a mother, but as one storyteller to another. You can learn the question she was asking before the world wrote its answer for her. And you can carry *that* question home.”

Isla looked at her mother’s back, straight and strong against the vast twilight. She felt the warm pulse of her own thread, now stretching taut, connecting her to that figure by the water. It was not a thread of fate. It was a thread of understanding. A bridge across time, built not from grief, but from craft.

The Curator had believed every story had one true, final shape. Isla now knew that was the greatest fiction of all.

She turned to Finch. “If I go to her… can I come back?”

He smiled, a sad, wise smile. “Every choice is a door, hen. Some you can’t close once opened. This place… it gets into your blood. It’s the addiction of the unwritten. You might find the way back to your Glasgow. Or you might find you’ve outgrown it. That your story needs a bigger sky.”

Isla looked at her hands. One still faintly shimmered with the afterglow of the void. The other was just a hand, capable of holding a pen, of touching a stone, of reaching out.

She had come to recover a draft. She had found the source.

High above, in a tower of polished despair that was now reflected in a pool of primal potential, the Curator watched from a window that was not there. She saw Isla hesitate on the threshold of the moor. She saw the woman by the pool. And she felt, for the first time, not the satisfaction of an ending, but the terrifying, exhilarating pull of a *prelude*.

The single, perfect tear finally fell. It traced the crack in her mask and dropped, not onto a ledger, but into the palm of her own hand. It was warm. It was alive. It was the first sentence of a story she had never dared to tell.

Down on the twilight path, Isla MacLeod made her choice.

She took a step forward, off the path and onto the heather, towards the pool, towards the woman, towards the unwritten question that had always been waiting for her.

Behind her, the oak door, the boundary between the curated and the possible, began slowly, silently, to swing shut.



---

# Chapter 20

## **CHAPTER 20**

The heather was not a carpet. It was a conspiracy. Each sprig, purple-black in the gloaming, hooked at Isla’s ankles with the tenacity of a living question, trying to root her to the spot. The air here was different. It did not have the curated, archival stillness of the Garden, nor the damp, diesel-soaked breath of her Glasgow. It tasted of cold water and crushed green things, of peat-smoke and a profound, listening silence. It was the air of a place that had never been written down.

The woman by the pool did not turn.

Isla moved forward, the thread in her chest a warm, insistent compass needle pointing true. With each step, the sound of her own passage seemed to leach away into the ground, swallowed by the moss and the thick twilight. The door, when she risked a glance back, was a diminishing rectangle of softer dark, a bookmark closing in a story she was no longer reading. She faced forward.

Up close, the pool was a disc of obsidian, holding the bruised sky in a perfect, unrippled grasp. The woman knelt at its edge, her back a curve of worn wool, her hair a long, grey-streaked river down her spine, loosely bound with a strip of leather. Her hands were busy. They moved with a rhythmic, patient certainty over a half-formed shape cradled in her lap. A basket, Isla saw. She was weaving a basket from the dark, sinewy rushes that grew at the water’s margin.

The craft of it was a quiet thunder.

Isla stopped a few paces away, the heather releasing its grip. She could hear the soft, wet *shush* of the rushes as they were drawn through, the minute creak of tension being shaped into purpose. This was not the grand, theatrical craft of the Curator, bending narrative like steel. This was older. It was the craft of making a vessel to hold water, of weaving a boundary to contain something necessary. It was the craft of making a thing to be used.

“You’re here,” the woman said. Her voice was not what Isla had expected. It was not the echo of her own, nor the spectral whisper of a memory. It was low, grainier, textured by wind and use. It was the voice of someone who spoke to the weather.

“I am,” Isla said. The words felt absurdly small, pebbles dropped into the pool’s perfect silence.

The woman’s hands did not cease their work. “You took your time.”

“The path was… complicated.”

A soft, dry sound escaped the woman. Not a laugh. An acknowledgement. “Paths usually are. They’re made by folk who want you to walk them. The moor doesn’t care for paths.” She lifted the nascent basket, turning it slightly, judging its symmetry against the sky. “You carry a thread. I can feel it from here. Pulling at you. A tidy, anxious little stitch.”

Isla’s hand went to her sternum. “It led me to you.”

“It led you to a question,” the woman corrected. She finally set her work aside on the moss and turned.

Her face was a map of a different life. Wind had written lines at the corners of her eyes, which were the same clear, peat-water grey as Isla’s, but set in a network of seams earned by squinting at distant horizons. Her skin was weathered, not old, but *used*, like good leather. There was a quiet ferocity in her bearing, the stillness of a hawk on a branch. This was not a ghost, not a reflection. This was a woman who had built her life from the raw materials of this unwritten place, stone by stone, stitch by stitch.

“You’re her,” Isla breathed. The words were inadequate, but they were all she had. “The one who… didn’t drown.”

The woman—*her mother*, a fact that was not emotional but geological, a mountain suddenly revealed by shifting fog—regarded her with a piercing, appraising look. “I’m the one who chose the deeper water,” she said. “There’s a difference. Drowning is something that happens to you. A choice is something you make.” She gestured to the ground beside her. “Sit. You’re standing like you’re waiting for a bus.”

Isla lowered herself onto the cool, damp moss, the scent of it rising around her. She was acutely aware of her own clothes—the city jeans, the practical boots—next to this woman’s thick skirts and sturdy, handmade shoes. She was a modern annotation in a primal text.

“My name is Isla.”

“I know what you’re called.” Her mother picked up the basket again, her fingers finding their rhythm. “Names are for other people. Out here, you are what you do. The work tells the tale.” She glanced at Isla’s hands. “What’s your work?”

Isla looked at her own palms. One still held the faint, cosmic shimmer of the void she had touched. The other was empty. “I… I was writing a thesis. About spaces between things.”

“Between things,” her mother echoed, the words a neutral tool in her mouth. “And did you find them? These between-places?”

“I found a prison made of them. A garden where stories go to be perfected. I found a man who thinks he’s a concept.” Isla heard the exhaustion, the frayed wonder in her own voice. “I found a door where my grief used to be.”

Her mother nodded, as if Isla had reported on the quality of the rushes. “Grief’s a poor lintel. It cracks under the weight of a real life.” Her eyes flicked to Isla’s face, reading the lines there. “You came looking for a why. Why I left. Why the story ended the way it did for you.”

“Yes.”

“Stories don’t end,” her mother said, her voice flat. “They get abandoned. Or they get curated.” She said the last word like it was a curse, a bitter, foreign thing. “That woman in her tower. She wanted my story. Wanted to press it flat between the pages of her ledger, give it a neat, tragic ending. Drowned woman. Lost mother. A clean, sad punctuation mark.” Her fingers pulled a rush through with a sharp, final tug. “I was a weaver. In your world, I wove cloth. But I was weaving something else, too. A… sensitivity. A thread of my own. I could feel the other looms, the ones working on a different pattern, in a different room. This place.” She gestured to the vast, listening moor with her chin. “It called. Not with words. With a *pull*. Like a stitch dropped in a tapestry, tugging the whole weave out of true.”

She paused, her gaze going distant, seeing not the pool but another shore. “Your father was a good man. But he loved the world he could see. He loved its solidity. Telling him I heard the loom of the moor… it would have been a cruelty. It would have been a story he couldn’t bear to live inside. So I crafted a different ending for him. One he could understand. One that would hurt, but cleanly. A mystery. A loss. An ending.”

The confession was delivered not with tears, but with the grim practicality of a surgeon describing a necessary incision. Isla felt the old, familiar ache—the child’s loss—rise up. But beside it, something new grew: the horrifying, adult understanding of the choice. The craft of it.

“You didn’t drown,” Isla said, the words reshaping the bedrock of her life. “You walked. You just walked into a different story.”

“I followed my thread,” her mother affirmed. “It’s the first rule of craft. You have to be true to the material. I was the material. And my material was pulled taut towards *here*.” She looked at Isla, her gaze softening by a degree. “Leaving you… that was the unweaving. The hardest thing I’ve ever made. Or unmade. I told myself a child needs a solid world. A world with bus routes and school bells and a father who comes home smelling of sawdust and certainty. Not a mother who listens to the spaces between the rain.”

Isla stared into the black mirror of the pool. Her own face, pale and drawn, looked back from its surface, superimposed over the endless sky. “I got the solid world. And I spent my whole life feeling the draft from the door you left open.”

Her mother was silent for a long time, the only sound the whisper of the rushes. “I know,” she said, finally. And in those two words, Isla heard an ocean of unspoken vigil, a mother’s watch from a distant shore. “I felt you feeling it. Every time you stood at a window, restless. Every time you chose the book with the map on the endpaper. The thread you have now… it’s my thread. The one I left behind, anchored in you. I felt it when you picked it up. It’s what finally pulled you through.”

“Finch said I might not be able to go back,” Isla said, the fear tasting metallic. “That this place gets in your blood.”

Her mother gave a short, sharp nod. “He’s a clever ghost. He’s right. This is the source. The unwritten page. The Garden you came from… it’s a museum. A beautiful, terrible museum built downstream from here. This,” she said, lifting her face to the vast, star-pricked twilight now enveloping them, “is the river. You can visit a museum. You can study its displays. But you can’t drink from them.”

“What do I do?” The question was not a child’s plea, but a crafter’s query. *What is the next step in the making?*

Her mother put down the basket. It was finished, a dark, elegant vessel. “You have a choice. You can use that thread,” she said, pointing to Isla’s chest, “as a lifeline. Follow it back through the door, to your Glasgow, to your thesis, to the life that knows how to mourn a drowned woman. It’s a good life. A real one. It has whisky and pubs and the love of a good, solid man, if you’ll let yourself see it.”

Isla thought of Morag’s aubergine hair, the smell of *The Hanged Man*, the weight of a pen in her hand. She thought of Ewan, his quiet, steadfast presence, a bulwark against the strange drafts of her mind.

“Or?” Isla asked.

“Or,” her mother said, and her voice changed, deepening with a note of fierce, wild pride, “you can take up the shuttle. You can learn the weave of this place. Not to curate it. To *inhabit* it. Your thread isn’t just a connection to me. It’s a connection to the loom itself. You have a craft in you, Isla. A different one from mine. You don’t just feel the between-places. You *map* them. You give them names. In that tower, you didn’t just break a rule. You wrote a new one. You turned a prison into a possibility.”

She reached out, not to touch Isla, but to the space between them. Her work-roughened fingers traced a shape in the air, and for a moment, Isla saw it: a faint, shimmering schematic, a bridge of light and intuition, spanning the gap between their two worlds. It was the ghost of her thesis, made manifest not in ink, but in pure potential.

“The Curator fears you,” her mother said, letting the vision fade. “Not because you’re powerful. But because you’re *generative*. You don’t want to perfect a story. You want to start a new one. That’s the one thing her Garden cannot hold.”

High above, in the tower that was and was not there, the Curator watched. The single, living tear in her palm had dried, leaving a faint, salt-crystal sentence on her skin. She saw the two women by the primordial pool, not as a concluded tragedy, but as a binary star system, their combined gravity warping the narrative space around them. She saw the door to the Garden, now almost closed. And she saw something else.

A ripple.

It began at the pool’s edge where Isla sat. A single, concentric ring moving out across the obsidian surface, untouched by wind. It was a pulse of pure, unchanneled potential. Isla’s potential. It was the first tremor of a story deciding to be born.

The Curator’s breath hitched. This was not a thread to be clipped. It was a root, pushing through the foundation of her perfect world. She could feel it in the ledger in her hands. The page for *Isla MacLeod* was no longer accepting ink. The words were shifting, rearranging themselves into something new. A title was forming at the top of a fresh, blank page beneath.

She had wanted an ending. She was getting a prologue.

Down on the moor, Isla felt the ripple move through her. It was the echo of her own choice, reflected back by the source. She looked from her mother’s fierce, expectant face to the dark doorway shrinking in the distance. She thought of the gendered cartography of her thesis, of how she had always mapped the spaces women were forced into, or vanished from. Here was a space a woman had carved for herself. A space beyond the map.

“If I stay,” Isla said, her voice finding a new solidity, “it’s not to be your daughter. Not in the way that word means back there.”

Her mother’s smile was a swift, bright thing, like a knife catching the light. “Good. Here, I am Elspeth. You are Isla. We are weavers at the same loom. That is a harder, truer thing.”

“And the Garden? The Curator?”

Elspeth’s face hardened. “She is a weaver too. But she weaves shrouds. Beautiful, perfect shrouds. She will see your choice as a threat. She will try to mend the tear you’ve made, to force your story back into its assigned shape.” She picked up the newly finished basket and handed it to Isla. It was cool, surprisingly light, and stronger than it looked. “Your first lesson. To make a thing is to define a space. To define a space is to create a world. She deals in endings. You must learn to deal in beginnings. Even small ones.”

Isla took the basket. It felt right in her hands. A vessel. For what, she did not yet know.

“How?” she asked.

Elspeth stood, brushing moss from her skirts. “You start by walking the unwritten moor. You listen. You feel the pull of the threads that are not yours. And when you find a story that’s been trapped, a song that’s been silenced, a door that’s been sealed shut…” She looked at Isla, her peat-grey eyes holding the last of the light. “You use your craft. Not to give it an ending. To give it a *next*.”

The hook in Isla’s chest was no longer a pull. It was a hum. A tuning fork struck against the bone of the world. She looked back one last time. The oak door was a thin, vertical line of not-quite-darkness. Then, with a finality that was neither a slam nor a whisper, but simply the sound of a page turning, it was gone.

She was not in the Garden. She was not in Glasgow.

She was in the source.

Above, the stars were coming out, not as distant pinpricks, but as close, watchful eyes. They were not the stars of any constellation she knew. They were the first words of a language she would have to learn.

Elspeth was already moving, a silhouette against the starry vastness, heading away from the pool towards a low, steady glow on the horizon—a light that promised hearth, not archive; a home, not a museum.

Isla MacLeod stood, the woven basket in her hand. She took a deep breath of the raw, uncurated air. Then she followed.

Behind them, in the now-sealed Garden, the Curator stared at the new page in her ledger. The title had finished writing itself. It glistened, wet and dark, as if inscribed not in ink, but in the water of a primordial pool.

It read: *The Weavers at the Loom of the Moor*.

And beneath it, the page was not blank. It was a mirror. Reflecting back at her was not her own masked face, but Isla’s—determined, unafraid, and walking into a story with no end in sight.

The Curator closed the book with a sound like a verdict. She walked to her window that was not there and looked out at her perfect, dying world. The symmetry was beginning to fray at the edges. A wind was rising—a damp, heather-scented wind from a place she could not control.

She had a choice now, too. She could fight to mend the fraying. To find Isla MacLeod and force her, and the chaos she represented, back into a manageable, tragic shape.

Or she could do the one thing her perfect system feared most.

She could pick up a pen, not to record, but to invent. She could write her own next.

Her hand, the one that had caught the living tear, trembled. It moved towards a drawer in her desk of polished despair, a drawer that had been locked since the first story was filed away. Inside was not a ledger, but a single, blank, unbound sheet of paper.

The hook for the next chapter was not in the moor, nor in the tower. It was in the terrifying, exquisite tremor in the Curator’s wrist, hovering between the prison of the perfect ending and the abyss of an authentic beginning.



---

# Chapter 21

## **CHAPTER 21**

The light on the horizon was not a single point, but a constellation of low, golden embers scattered across the moor’s dark breast. As Isla followed Elspeth’s sure-footed silhouette, the embers resolved into the lit windows of crofts, their panes holding the glow like captured pieces of a kinder sun. The air was a tonic of peat-smoke, damp earth, and the sweet, decaying perfume of bracken. It was the smell of a world that was used, not preserved; lived-in, not curated.

Elspeth led her to the largest of the crofts, a long, low building of stone and sagging thatch from which a plume of smoke rose in a confident grey column against the star-flecked sky. No path led to its door. The ground was simply worn bare by the passage of feet that knew where they were going without being told.

“Welcome,” Elspeth said, her voice softened by the moor’s vast quiet, “to the Spindle.”

Inside, the air was thick with warmth and the scent of stewing herbs, wool, and woodsmoke. A fire roared in a hearth wide enough to park a car in, its light dancing over a dozen faces that turned towards the door. They were all women, their ages a topography from smooth youth to weathered antiquity. They sat in various states of work: some carding raw, oily wool, their hands moving with a rhythmic, pulling grace; others spun thread onto spindles that whirled like captive planets; a few worked at a vast, wooden loom that took up one entire wall, its warp threads taut as the strings of a colossal, horizontal harp. The shuttle, passed hand to hand, was a polished bone the length of a forearm.

The conversation, a low hum of Gaelic and laughter, did not stop. It merely absorbed them, as the moor had absorbed their footsteps.

A woman near the fire, her hair a wild corona of silver and her face a network of lines as deep and meaningful as runes, looked up from her spinning. Her eyes, the colour of flint, found Isla’s. They held no surprise, only a profound, assessing calm.

“So,” the woman said, her voice the sound of stone grinding against stone, yet not unkind. “The one who walked out of a story. Bring her to the fire, Elspeth. Let’s see what the Garden tried to make of her.”

Isla moved forward, the woven basket still in her hand. The eyes of the weavers tracked her, not with hostility, but with the focused attention of cartographers surveying a new, uncertain coastline. She felt herself being read—the set of her shoulders, the Glasgow pallor beneath the moor’s wind-flush, the way her fingers tightened on the basket’s handle as if it were a lifeline thrown from a sinking ship.

“Sit,” the silver-haired woman commanded, nodding to a three-legged stool near the hearth. “I am Mairi. This,” she gestured with her chin to encompass the room, the looms, the women, “is what we do. We don’t archive. We don’t curate. We *weave*.”

Isla sat, the heat of the fire pressing against her skin like a firm hand. “Weave what?”

Mairi’s spindle never ceased its hypnotic whirl. “The frayed ends. The discarded threads. The stories that don’t fit into their neat little books. The Garden takes a life, pins it like a butterfly, gives it a label and a tragic symmetry. We find the thread that was cut too soon, the colour that was deemed unsuitable, the pattern that was too wild. And we weave it back in.”

“Back into what?”

“Into *this*,” Mairi said, her flint eyes glinting. “Into the cloth of what’s real. The Garden’s world is a pressed flower. Beautiful, dead. Ours is a living blanket. It has holes. It’s lumpy. It smells of sheep and sweat and supper. But it is warm. And it can grow.”

One of the younger weavers, a girl with fierce red hair and forearms corded with muscle from working the loom, spoke without looking up from her knots. “She’s got the look of a trimmed thread about her. Neatly cut. You can see the shears’ mark.”

Isla thought of her thesis, her life in Glasgow, the prescribed trajectory of academic grief. She thought of the letter on pressed-bone paper. “I was being trimmed for a tragedy,” she said, the words coming out with more bitterness than she intended. “A cautionary tale about reaching too high.”

Mairi nodded, a slow, grave dip of her head. “Aye. The ‘Scholar Destroyed By Her Own Curiosity.’ A classic. A tidy ending. But you…” She leaned forward, and the firelight carved the valleys of her face into deeper shadow. “You did not end. You *unravelled*. And you came here, trailing threads. Show us what you brought from that place.”

Isla looked down at the basket. She had almost forgotten its weight. Carefully, she lifted the cloth and revealed the contents. The peat-bog ghost, now just a bottle of unremarkable whisky. The cream-coloured letter. The photograph of her grandmother. The small, smooth stone from the river. The torn page from the Curator’s ledger.

A silence fell, deeper than before. The hum of work ceased. The only sounds were the crackle of the fire and the mournful sigh of the wind against the thatch.

Mairi set her spindle down. She reached out, not for the ledger page, but for the stone. She held it in her palm, her thumb stroking its surface. “An anchor,” she murmured. “Good.” She picked up the photograph, her eyes softening for a fraction of a second. “A face to guide the pattern. Also good.” The letter she barely glanced at. “A map drawn by a man who never left his study. Useful only to know the traps.”

Finally, she took the torn ledger page. She did not read the words *The Weavers at the Loom of the Moor*. She turned it over, ran a calloused finger along the ragged edge where it had been torn from the book. “This,” she said, her voice dropping to a whisper that the whole room leaned in to catch, “is a wound. In her world, and now in ours. A door she did not mean to open. It is the most powerful thing here.”

She looked at Isla, and her gaze was no longer merely assessing. It was an invitation. A challenge. “You have the raw stuff. The cut threads. But to weave, you must learn the craft. You must choose your own pattern. Or would you prefer we do it for you? We could make you a warm, simple shawl of forgetting. You could live out your days here by the fire, and the story of Isla MacLeod would be a pleasant, minor pattern in the corner of the blanket. A happy footnote.”

The offer hung in the smoky air, seductive in its simplicity. Isla looked around the room. She saw the contentment in the weavers’ faces, the purpose in their hands. It was a good life. A sane life. It was everything the sterile horror of the Garden was not.

But it was also a life where her story would be subsumed, its particular ache blended into the communal wool. She thought of the Curator’s ledger, of her own face reflected back as a finished title. She thought of her grandmother’s stories, told not to be filed away, but to be lived into.

“No,” Isla said, and her voice did not tremble. “I don’t want a shawl. I want to learn the loom.”

A slow smile spread across Mairi’s face, a crack in stone that revealed a deep, warm strata beneath. “I thought you might.” She stood, her bones creaking like the timbers of an old ship. “Then your first lesson is this: the Garden’s power is in endings. Our power is in the *join*. The place where one thread meets another. Where your story meets mine, meets hers.” She gestured to the women around them. “Where grief meets anger, where memory meets hope. The join is never seamless. It is a knot. A bump. A change in texture. That is where the strength lies. That is where the truth is.”

She led Isla to the great loom. Up close, it was a beast of polished dark wood and tension. The warp threads were not one colour, but a thousand—the grey of a Glasgow sky, the green of hospital walls, the russet of heather, the gold of whisky, the stark white of bone-paper, the deep black of a curator’s robe.

“Your life,” Mairi said, placing a hand on the frame. “Your threads. Already strung. You’ve been strung on this loom since the day you were born. The Garden tried to weave you into a shroud. We will help you weave yourself into something else. But *you* must throw the shuttle. You must choose the weft.”

She handed Isla the bone shuttle. It was warm, heavier than it looked, and fitted to her grip as if it had been waiting for her.

“What is the weft?” Isla asked, her throat tight.

“Your choices. Your actions. What you do next. Every pass of the shuttle is a decision. It crosses the threads of your past, your nature, your circumstances. It binds them into a fabric. Now,” Mairi stepped back, her flint eyes gleaming. “You have a wound in your hand. A torn page. What is your first pass? What is the first choice you weave across the threads of your life?”

Isla looked at the shuttle. She looked at the loom, at the myriad threads of her own history, taut and waiting. She thought of the Curator, alone in her tower, her hand trembling over a blank page. Two women, separated by the abyss between a prison and a moor, both holding the terrible, exquisite weight of a next.

She took a deep breath, the air rich with smoke and wool and possibility.

She threw the shuttle.

It was not graceful. It clattered against the warp threads, and the resulting line of weft—a thick, homespun yarn the colour of raw peat—was uneven, bumpy. But it was *there*. A tangible, physical fact. A join made.

A soft murmur of approval rippled through the weavers. Elspeth, watching from near the door, gave a single, firm nod.

“Good,” Mairi grunted. “A strong, ugly, honest choice. Now. Do it again.”

*

In the tower that was not a tower, in the room with a window that was not there, the Curator sat at her desk of polished despair. The drawer was open.

Before her lay the single, blank, unbound sheet of paper. Its emptiness was a void more terrifying than any of the tragic, completed stories in her ledgers. Those had rules. Symmetry. A predictable, beautiful despair. This had… potential. It was an abyss of *could be*.

Her hand, the one that had caught the tear, still trembled. The tremor was a tiny rebellion in her perfectly ordered system, a seismic event in the stilled world of her body. She tried to still it by force of will, the way she stilled unruly narratives. The tremor deepened.

She looked at her other hand, resting flat and calm on the ledger titled *The Weavers at the Loom of the Moor*. The ink of the title was dry now, but the page beneath was still a mirror. It no longer showed Isla’s face. It showed the interior of the croft. The fire. The loom. Isla’s clumsy, determined first pass with the shuttle.

The Curator watched. She saw the women gather around, not to criticise, but to support. She saw a young weaver with red hair lean in and adjust Isla’s grip on the shuttle, her touch practical and kind. She saw Mairi point to a thread of Glasgow-grey in the warp and say something that made Isla nod, a look of dawning understanding on her face.

It was collaboration. It was mess. It was a process, not a product.

A sensation, alien and sharp, pierced the Curator’s chest. It was not the familiar, comfortable ache of tragic beauty. It was hotter. Rawer. It felt like watching a favourite, precious specimen—a rare, doomed butterfly—not only escape its pin, but melt its glass case, fly to a muddy field, and start pollinating weeds.

It was fury.

Pure, undiluted, magnificent fury.

The tremor in her hand stopped. It was replaced by a steady, terrifying certainty. She would not mend the fraying edges of her symmetry. She would not write herself a meek, introspective next on this blank page.

She would *burn* the moor.

She would take her pen, the instrument of perfect endings, and she would use it to write an ending so absolute, so aesthetically devastating, that it would swallow the Spindle, the weavers, and the upstart thread of Isla MacLeod forever. She would write a storm. A plague. A forgetting so profound it would unravel their clumsy cloth from the inside out.

She reached for her pen, a sleek, black instrument that had never known an uncertain line.

But as her fingers closed around it, the mirror-page in the ledger shimmered. Isla, at the loom, had just completed another pass. This one was smoother. The weft yarn was a different colour—a hesitant, but unmistakable, strand of gold, like the first weak sunlight after a long night. As it locked into place, crossing the thread of the torn ledger page, the Curator felt a corresponding, faint *pull* in her own wrist.

She looked down. A single, fine, almost invisible filament of gold had appeared on her blank sheet of paper. It was not ink. It was thread. It stretched from the tip of her pen, across the void of the page, and disappeared into the air, taut with connection.

Isla’s choice. Her action. Her weft. It was not contained in the Garden. It was reaching back. Weaving into the Curator’s story.

The Curator’s fury crystallised into something colder, more brilliant. This was no longer a simple extermination. This was a duel. A narrative duel across the interstitial reality. She was no longer just a curator fighting chaos. She was an author, challenged by another.

A slow, thin smile touched her lips, the first genuine expression her face had held in centuries. It was not a pleasant sight.

“Very well, Isla MacLeod,” she whispered to the empty, perfect air. “You wish to weave? Then let us weave. Let us see whose pattern can hold. Let us see who gets to write the ending of the other.”

She did not move to cut the golden thread. Instead, she placed her pen against the blank page, at a right angle to the invading filament. She would not write *around* Isla’s thread. She would write *against* it. She would use Isla’s own choices as a new warp in her own design, and weave them into a masterpiece of despair so exquisite it would make the tragedy she’d originally planned for Isla look like a child’s scribble.

She began to write. Her script, usually so flawless, was deliberate, heavy, each word a bar of iron designed to weight down the hopeful gold. She wrote of a wind, not damp and heather-scented, but sharp and glassy, carrying a frost that killed not plants, but memory. She wrote of a flaw in the wool, a blight that started at the join and rotted the cloth from within. She wrote of a weaver’s betrayal, a pattern of suspicion and pride that would unravel their community from the inside.

Each sentence she wrote manifested on the page not as ink, but as a thread—dark, glittering, and cold. They crossed Isla’s single golden filament, binding it, seeking to choke its light. The blank page was no longer blank. It was becoming the battleground.

And in the Spindle, Isla felt a sudden chill, a dissonance in the rhythm of the loom. The warp thread that was the torn ledger page grew taut, then slack, then taut again, as if something were pulling on it from a great distance. The golden weft she had just woven seemed to dim for a heartbeat.

She looked up from the loom, her hands stilling. Mairi was watching her, the old weaver’s face grim.

“She’s answering,” Mairi said, her voice low. “The Gardener. She’s taken your first pass, and she’s making her counter-move. This is the work now. Not just weaving your life. But defending the weave.”

Isla looked at the bone shuttle in her hand, then at the complex, beautiful, fragile tapestry of threads before her. She was no longer just learning a craft. She was at war. A war fought not with weapons, but with choices. Every pass of her shuttle would be a parry. Every colour she chose would be a defiance.

She took a new thread from a basket by the loom. This one was not gold, but a deep, resilient blue, the colour of a deep loch under a winter sky—the colour of endurance. She fitted it into the shuttle.

She threw it.

The thread flew true, crossing the darkened warp, reinforcing the gold, adding a new strength to the pattern.

In her tower, the Curator felt the resistance. She saw a new blue thread appear on her page, strengthening the gold, complicating her dark design. Her smile did not waver. It deepened. She dipped her pen into a well of a darker ink, and wrote of a flood.

*

The hook for the next chapter was not in the chill of the rising glassy wind, nor in the growing warmth of the croft’s fire. It was in the silent, relentless dialogue between two looms. One of wood and wool and living hands, warmed by a hearth. One of paper and ink and desperate will, in a tower of perfect cold. Each pass of a shuttle, each stroke of a pen, was a move in a game where the stakes were the very shape of a soul. The cloth on the loom in the Spindle was growing, a patchwork of peat-brown, gold, and loch-blue, its joins bumpy and strong. The pattern on the Curator’s page was a dark, glittering counterpoint, a thing of terrible, invasive beauty. They were weaving each other now. And the next thread to be chosen—whether by a woman finding her strength in the smell of wool and smoke, or by a woman discovering a terrifying new power in the taste of her own fury—would determine whose reality would finally hold the warp.



---

# Chapter 22

## **CHAPTER 22**

The loom was a fortress. The loom was a battleground. Isla MacLeod’s hands, chapped from lye soap and raw wool, moved with a rhythm that was no longer just craft. It was cadence. The deep loch-blue thread, the colour of endurance, had become the weft of her defence, a steady, stubborn beat against the invasive dark. Each pass of the bone shuttle was a breath held, a prayer muttered, a door barred. The cloth growing on the frame was a testament not to beauty, but to resilience; its joins were lumpy, its texture uneven, a topography of stubborn survival.

In the tower, the Curator’s pen was a scalpel. She had written of a flood, and in the Spindle, the glassy wind had howled for three days, bringing a cold rain that seeped through the thickest stone. Isla and Morag had moved the loom away from the window, where the wind shrieked like a thing in pain. The peat fire spat and hissed at the damp. But Isla had woven. She had taken a thread the colour of banked peat-embers, a dull, stubborn red, and woven it alongside the blue. *Warmth against the damp*, she thought. *A hearth against the flood*. The pattern absorbed it, the cloth thickened.

Now, the Curator observed the new, fiery strand in her page’s counter-pattern. It was a vulgar colour, a peasant colour. It offended the pristine, glittering darkness of her design. She felt not anger, but a cool, surgical curiosity. The girl was not just resisting; she was innovating. Building. The Curator dipped her pen. She did not write of a new catastrophe. Instead, she wrote of a gift.

*

The gift arrived with the tinker.

He appeared at the croft door as the unnatural rain finally ceased, leaving a world washed in a thin, silvered light that felt borrowed from a dream. He was a lean man, all tendons and sharp angles, his face hidden beneath the broad brim of a hat that dripped onto the threshold. His pack, slung over one shoulder, clinked with a sound like distant chimes.

“A fine, damp day for it,” he said, his voice a dry rustle. “I’ve wares. Needles that never dull. Pots that sing when the water boils. Thread.”

Isla, her back aching from the loom, watched him from the hearth. Morag stood in the doorway, a silhouette against the grey light, her arms crossed. The pirate queen assessing a potential boarder.

“We’ve thread enough,” Morag said, her tone flat as slate.

“Aye, the kind that comes from sheep,” the tinker said, a smile in his voice. “Good, honest stuff. I speak of another kind. Found thread. Lost thread.” His head tilted, and his gaze found Isla over Morag’s shoulder. It was a needle-prick of attention. “A colour you’re missing.”

Isla felt it then—a pull, a yearning deep in the marrow of her craft. The loom’s warp seemed to hum, a faint vibration in the quiet croft. She knew, with a certainty that tasted of cold iron, that this was the Curator’s move. Not an assault, but an offering. A poisoned gift.

“Let him in,” Isla said, her own voice surprising her with its steadiness.

Morag shot her a look that was pure, undiluted warning, but stepped aside. The tinker entered, bringing the smell of wet wool and ozone with him. He shrugged off his pack, the clinking subsiding, and from a worn leather pouch, he drew a single skein.

It was a thread of impossible silver. Not the grey of a Glasgow sky, but the liquid, living silver of a moonpath on a still sea. It seemed to hold the light in the dim croft, not reflecting it, but drinking it, and glowing with a soft, internal radiance. It was the most beautiful thing Isla had ever seen. Her fingers itched for it. The loom *sang* for it. This silver would bridge the peat-brown and the loch-blue, it would highlight the ember-red. It would make the cloth not just strong, but glorious.

“Where did you find that?” Morag asked, her voice dangerously quiet.

“Oh, it finds its own way,” the tinker said, laying the skein on the rough table. It seemed out of place, a jewel on a bed of burlap. “Tumbled from a lady’s sewing basket in a coach long lost. Waited in a ditch for a century. It wants to be used. It’s *lonely*.”

The word hung in the air. Isla understood loneliness as a physical space, a room in the heart with a locked door. This thread, this beautiful, stolen silver, was an embodiment of that ache. The Curator hadn’t sent a weapon. She had sent a mirror. A reflection of Isla’s own deepest solitude, polished into irresistible allure.

“It’s a trap,” Morag stated, not a question.

“Everything’s a trap, hen,” the tinker chuckled, tapping the side of his nose. “The question is, can you spring it and take the cheese? The lady who… misplaced this… she believes you’ll see the trap and refuse. That your fear will starve your work. That you’ll weave only in safe, dull colours.” He pushed the skein an inch closer to Isla. “But a weaver who fears a beautiful thread is no weaver at all. She’s a seamstress.”

It was the perfect provocation. It cut to the core of Isla’s academic pride, her artistic yearning, her very identity. To refuse was to admit fear, to be small. To accept was to play the Curator’s game on her terms. Isla looked from the silver to her loom, to the strong, humble, uneven cloth upon it. It was honest. It was hers. This silver was a stranger’s voice, beautiful and full of lies.

She walked to the table. She did not pick up the skein. She leaned close, looking at the twist of the filaments. There, almost invisible, was a faint, dark core running through the centre of each strand, like a vein of ink through a precious stone.

“It’s tainted,” Isla said.

“All beauty is, in some light,” the tinker shrugged. “It’s still beauty.”

Isla straightened. The yearning was still there, a hook in her heart. But she saw the hook now. “Take it back to her. Tell her I don’t work with stolen materials. I spin my own.”

For a moment, the tinker’s affable mask slipped. His eyes, in the shadow of his hat, were not a tinker’s eyes. They were old, and empty, and patient. Then the smile returned. “As you wish. A pity. It would have sung on your loom.” He swept the silver back into his pouch, the light in the croft dimming perceptibly. With a nod, he was gone, back into the silvered damp.

Morag let out a long breath. “Well done, girl.”

Isla didn’t feel like she’d done well. She felt scraped-out, hollow. She had won the point but felt the cost. The rejection of that beauty was a little death. She returned to the loom, to her honest, ugly, strong threads. The rhythm felt heavier now.

In the tower, the Curator received the report from her empty-eyed servant. She was not disappointed. She had expected refusal. The first offer was always refused by the righteous. It was the second, third, fourth offer that eroded principle. The gift had been placed. The seed of desire had been planted. Now, she would let it grow in the dark soil of Isla’s deprivation. She wrote, not of a new threat, but of a deepening silence. A loneliness so profound it would make the memory of that silver thread ache like a phantom limb.

*

The silence in the Spindle became a physical presence. The wind died. The rain stopped. Even the crackle of the fire seemed muted, swallowed by a thick, woolly quiet. It was the silence of a held breath, of a vacuum. Isla wove, but the *clack* of the beater sounded solitary, pathetic, a tiny noise in an immense, listening stillness. The colours of her cloth—brown, blue, red—seemed to dull, as if starved of contrast. The lack of the silver was an ache. She began to dream of it, a shimmering path leading away from the loom, away from the croft, into a landscape of cool, perfect light.

“She’s isolating you,” Morag said, her voice too loud in the quiet. She was carding wool, the rhythmic *scritch-scratch* a feeble defiance. “It’s what they do. Cut you off from sound, from sense, until your own mind is the only voice, and it starts to whisper their arguments for them.”

“I’m not listening,” Isla muttered, throwing the shuttle. It felt leaden.

“You don’t listen with your ears in this place,” Morag said. “You listen with your gut. And your gut is hungry for that pretty poison.”

Isla said nothing. Morag’s words were another thread of plain, ugly truth. She wove it in, a strand of gritty grey, the colour of Morag’s hair before the aubergine dye, the colour of Glasgow pavement. *Truth against silence*.

Days bled into each other, marked only by the growing panel of cloth and the deepening quiet. Isla found herself talking to the loom, to the threads, just to hear a voice. She told them about Glasgow, about the peat-ghost whisky, about Professor Finch’s letter that felt like pressed bone. The loom absorbed her stories, the cloth becoming a ledger of her past.

Then, the second gift came.

It did not arrive with a tinker. It simply *was*. Isla woke at dawn, the terrible silence already pressing down on the croft, and went to draw water from the rain-barrel. There, floating on the still, dark surface, was a single, perfect pearl. It glowed with the same inner luminescence as the silver thread. It was not attached to anything. It was just a pearl, an offering on the altar of her need.

She stared at it. It was a condensed tear. It was a moon. It was a trap. Her hand trembled over the water. She could take it. She could pierce it, string it, weave it into the cloth as a single, stunning accent. One pearl. What harm could one pearl do?

*It’s not the pearl*, a voice in her said—her own voice, shaped by Morag’s grit. *It’s the acceptance. It’s the crack in the door.*

With a cry that was swallowed by the silence, she brought her cupped hands down, not to retrieve the pearl, but to sink it. She stirred the water violently until the pearl swirled away into the depths of the barrel. When the water stilled, it was gone. Her heart hammered. She had refused again. But the effort felt Herculean. The pearl’s image was seared behind her eyes.

The Curator, sensing the violent rejection, smiled with genuine pleasure. The struggle was exquisite. The girl was strong. Breaking her would be a masterpiece. She wrote of a memory. A specific memory: the smell of a particular perfume—*Fleurs du Mal*—on a university corridor. The memory of a betrayal not by a lover, but by a mentor. A small, academic cruelty that had festered. She gave the memory weight, texture, and sent it drifting into the Spindle like a leaf on a still pond.

*

Isla was weaving a section of peat-brown, trying to anchor herself in the solid, earthy colour, when the memory struck. It was not a thought. It was an ambush.

The smell of *Fleurs du Mal*—cloying, expensive, out of place in the dusty philosophy department—filled her nostrils. She saw Professor Callahan, her doctoral supervisor, laughing with a visiting professor from Oxford, a man with teeth like a polished graveyard. “Isla’s work is *charmingly* parochial,” Callahan had said, not knowing she was around the corner. “A feminist rereading of the liminal? It’s a thesis, certainly. But groundbreaking? One must have a broader palette.” The Oxford man had chuckled, a dry, rustling sound. The smell of the perfume had mixed with the smell of their contempt.

Isla gasped, the shuttle falling from her hand. The memory was a knife, twisted in an old, half-forgotten wound. It was also utterly, devastatingly true. It had happened. This was not an invented horror. It was her own past, weaponised.

“What is it?” Morag was at her side in an instant.

“She’s… using my own memories,” Isla choked out, the peat-brown blurring before her eyes. “Not just offering pretty things. She’s using what’s already inside me.”

Morag’s face hardened. “Aye. That’s the final garrison. When the enemy is already within the walls. You can refuse a gift, hen. How do you refuse a truth?”

Isla had no answer. The silence of the Spindle was now filled with the echo of that laughter, that perfume. She felt exposed, flayed. The Curator wasn’t just weaving a counter-pattern; she was unweaving Isla’s own. Picking out the threads of her confidence, her intellect, her very history, and showing her how thin they were.

Trembling, Isla picked up the shuttle. She could not weave the peat-brown. It felt like the mud of that humiliation. She reached for a new colour, her hand moving past the blues and reds. She found a spool of a sickly, jaundiced yellow—the colour of old paper, of academic envy, of a coward’s smile. She threaded it.

“What are you doing?” Morag asked, her voice tight.

“Using it,” Isla said, her voice raw. “If she wants to give me my past, I’ll weave it in. But I’ll frame it. I’ll surround it.”

With a fury that was close to despair, she began to weave with the jaundiced yellow. It made a foul, vibrant stripe across the cloth. But around it, she wove a fierce, protective border of the ember-red—the colour of her anger—and the gritty grey of Morag’s truth. She did not hide the yellow. She showcased it. She made it a prisoner in her own tapestry.

In the tower, the Curator felt the shift. The memory-thread had been accepted, but not as a flaw. It had been incorporated as a deliberate motif. The girl was learning the deepest lesson: not to reject the dark threads, but to subjugate them. To make them part of the pattern’s strength. A flicker of something that was not fury, but profound annoyance, passed through the Curator’s perfect composure. This was no longer a game of seduction. It was a duel of interpretations.

She set down her pen. She would not write another memory. Instead, she would write a connection. A bridge. She would use the one thread Isla could not resist, the one memory that was not a weapon but a lifeline. She began to write of a smell of peat and old books. Of a voice, dry as parchment, speaking of unseen bridges. She wrote of Professor Alistair Finch.

*

The third gift was a sound.

The silence broke not with a crash, but with a whisper. It was the faint, rhythmic scratch of a pen on paper. Isla, dozing by the hearth, jerked awake. Morag was already standing, a carding comb held like a weapon.

The sound came from outside the croft door. Not threatening. Insistent. Familiar.

Isla knew that sound. She had sat outside Professor Finch’s office for hours, listening to it through the oak door, a metronome marking the passage of academic wisdom. Her heart, a trapped bird, beat against her ribs. This was the cruelest gambit yet. The silver was desire. The pearl was temptation. The memory was a wound. But Finch… Finch was hope. He was the reason she was here. He was the architect of the unseen bridge she was trying to cross.

She walked to the door, Morag’s warning hiss a distant thing.

“Don’t you open that, Isla MacLeod. It’s not him. It’s a *ventriloquist’s trick*.”

Isla knew it wasn’t him. But it was *of* him. A perfect echo. A concept given sound. She laid her palm against the rough wood. The scratching stopped. Then, his voice, old and dry and kind, exactly as she remembered it from their one meeting in *The Hanged Man*, seeped through the crack.

“Isla?” it said. “The bridge… it’s not in the weaving alone. It’s in the joining of disparate threads. You must use *all* the threads, my dear. Even the borrowed ones. Especially the borrowed ones. A bridge, by its nature, is made of foreign material.”

It was him. It was the essence of his thesis. It was also the perfect, logical argument for accepting the Curator’s gifts. It was the intellectual seduction to complement the aesthetic one. Her mind, trained to follow his logic, leapt to agree. *Of course*. A bridge was connection. It required material from both shores. To refuse the foreign thread was to refuse the bridge itself.

Tears of sheer cognitive conflict pricked her eyes. This was the checkmate. How could she refuse her own purpose?

Morag was beside her, not touching her, a solid presence in the gloom. “He’s a concept, hen,” she whispered, echoing her own words from a lifetime ago in a Glasgow pub. “And concepts can be forged. She’s forged yours.”

Isla leaned her forehead against the door. The urge to open it, to see the ghost of the old professor standing there with his kind eyes and his devastating logic, was a physical pain. It would be so easy. It would make so much *sense*.

She thought of the cloth on the loom. Its bumpy, honest joins. Its defensive, defiant colours. It was not a graceful bridge. It was a barricade. It was a home. And a home could be made only from what you owned, what you’d grown, what you’d fought for. You didn’t build a home from borrowed, beautiful, poisoned stones.

She found her voice. It was a thread, thin but strong. “A bridge is a crossing,” she said to the door, to the ghost, to the Curator listening beyond. “A home is a destination. I’m not building a bridge anymore, Professor. I’m building a home. And I’ll build it with my own hands.”

The silence returned. But it was different now. It was no longer a suffocating vacuum. It was a silence she had earned. The scratching did not come again.

Back at the loom, her hands were steady. She did not reach for a new, dramatic colour. She took up the humble peat-brown once more. But now, she wove it with a new understanding. It wasn’t just dirt. It was foundation. She wove the memory of Callahan’s betrayal into it, not as a separate stripe, but as a dark fleck within the brown, a stone in the foundation. She wove the yearning for the silver as a faint, almost invisible gloss on the loch-blue. She wove the ghost of Finch’s voice into the twist of the ember-red and the gritty grey.

She was not just defending her weave. She was digesting the assault. Making the attacks part of the structure’s strength. The cloth grew, more complex, more integrated, more *real*.

In her tower, the Curator watched the new pattern emerge on her page. The dark, glittering counterpoint was still there, but it was no longer simply opposed to Isla’s work. It was being… metabolised. The girl’s tapestry was becoming a palimpsest, with the Curator’s own dark ink showing through as a layer of history, not as an overriding text. For the first time, a cold trickle of something akin to fear touched the Curator’s spine. She was not losing. But she was no longer the sole author. She was in a dialogue with a will that was learning to write back.

She looked at her page, at the beautiful, terrible story she had composed. Then she looked at the final, untouched well of ink on her desk. It was not black, but a colourless, viscous fluid that reflected nothing. It was the ink of erasure. Of unwriting. The ultimate counter-move. To not add a dark thread, but to dissolve a light one. To not attack the weaver, but to vanish a fundamental thread from her warp.

A slow, frigid smile touched her lips. The duel of interpretations was over. It was time to change the nature of the game. She dipped her pen into the colourless well. She would not write a new sentence. She would carefully, precisely, blot one out.

*

The hook for the next chapter was not in the profound silence that now felt like peace, nor in the solid, growing weight of the cloth on the loom. It was in the empty space on the Curator’s page where a word used to be. It was in the sudden, inexplicable gap in Isla’s memory as she wove—a gap where the smell of her mother’s hair, the specific scent of heather and lanolin that was the very first thread of her being, had simply ceased to exist. The battle for the tapestry had reached a stalemate of strength. Now, the war would move to a more terrifying frontier: the war for what had always been true. The Curator had stopped trying to add to the story. She had begun, with meticulous care, to subtract.



---

# Chapter 23

## **CHAPTER 23**

The cloth on the loom was a continent. It had weight, a geography of ridges and valleys formed by the tight-packed weft, a climate held in the humidity of the wool. Isla’s hands moved across it, not as a weaver’s tools, but as a surveyor’s instruments, taking a bearing on a landscape she was still learning to name. The profound silence that had followed the integration of the Curator’s dark thread was not empty. It was dense, like the air before a thunderstorm that never breaks. It was the silence of a held breath.

She was weaving a border now, a complex braid of ochre and slate grey, a representation of the old drystone dyke that had separated her father’s croft from the moor. It was a memory of boundaries, of the deliberate, patient act of division. As her shuttle passed through the shed, she reached for the sensory truth of it—the gritty chill of the granite, the slickness of the moss on the north-facing stones, the way the wind would whistle a different note through each gap.

But the memory was silent.

She paused, the shuttle resting in her palm like a dormant bird. She closed her eyes, pushing past the image of the dyke to the day she was remembering. She had been seven, maybe eight. She’d scraped her knee on a stone, a bright, stinging bloom of pain. Her mother had lifted her onto the warm capstone of the dyke, had spat—a quick, pragmatic gesture—onto the corner of her apron, and had wiped the dirt from the wound. Isla had howled, more from the indignity than the hurt.

“Hush now, *mo chridhe*,” her mother had said, her voice a low, warm drone against Isla’s temple. “It’s just the land reminding you it’s there. It leaves its mark so you don’t forget.”

Then she had leaned in, her breath smelling of strong tea and the morning’s porridge, and kissed the crown of Isla’s head. And in that kiss, there had been a scent. A specific, foundational perfume that was the bedrock of Isla’s world: the crisp, honeyed purple of sun-warmed heather, and beneath it, the deep, oily, comforting tang of lanolin from the raw wool her mother was always carding. It was the smell of safety. It was the smell of *her*.

Isla reached for it now. She needed it as the anchor for this thread, the emotional truth that would turn coloured wool into a lived moment on the loom.

There was nothing.

Not a blank space, not a void. It was worse. It was a smooth, polished wall where a door had always been. The memory of the event was intact—the scraped knee, the apron, the kiss, the words. But the sensory soul of it, the scent that had been the very key to the memory’s vault, was gone. Eradicated. It was like reading a meticulous, clinical description of a symphony. All the notes were listed, in order, with their correct durations. But the music itself was absent.

A cold, formless dread began to pool in Isla’s stomach. She set the shuttle down carefully on the warp, its click against the wood abnormally loud. She tried another memory. Baking bannocks with her mother, flour dusting the table like first snow. The scent of the griddle, the hot, nutty smell of the oats toasting… gone. Only the visual remained, a silent film.

Another. Her mother brushing her hair before the fire, the rhythmic pull of the bristles, the static crackle, the smell of the peat smoke in her mother’s woolen dress… gone.

She stood up, her legs unsteady. She walked to the small, high window of the bothy and pressed her forehead against the cool glass. She was not forgetting. She was being *unmade*. Piece by piece. The Curator was no longer arguing with her story. She was editing the source material. She was going back into the archives of Isla’s life and burning the original documents, leaving only the sterile citations.

The battle had shifted. It was no longer about strength of will, or complexity of pattern. It was about ontology. What was a self, if not a cumulative library of sensory impressions, emotional echoes, and remembered touches? A woman was a story she told herself, built on a thousand tiny, incontrovertible truths—the taste of a first kiss, the sound of a father’s laugh, the smell of a mother’s skin. Unwrite those truths, and the story had no foundation. It became fiction. It became a tapestry with beautiful, intricate patterns, woven over a warp that had quietly dissolved.

Isla understood the Curator’s move with a chilling clarity. This was the ultimate patriarchal rewrite. It was not the loud, brutal censorship of burning a book. It was the silent, insidious revision of the dictionary. Change the definitions of the words—of love, of safety, of mother—and you change every story ever told with them. You don’t need to destroy the weaver if you can dissolve the threads of her own history, making her doubt the very fabric of her being. The most effective prison was not one built around a person, but one built from the inside of her own memory.
