She returned to the loom. The cloth, which moments before had felt like a triumph, now felt like a lie. A beautiful, coherent lie. If the foundational memories were being erased, what was she weaving? A fantasy? A theory? The Curator was forcing her to author a self from a place of amnesia.

Her hands trembled as she took up the shuttle again. She would not stop. Stopping was surrender. But she had to weave differently now. She had to weave *against* the erosion.

She began a new section, not with a memory, but with the *absence* of one. She used a thread of a peculiar, pale grey, the colour of fog or amnesia. She wove it in a broken, stuttering pattern, a visual representation of the gap where her mother’s scent should be. It was a negative space given positive form. It was a scar in the tapestry. She did not try to fill it with something else. She let the emptiness be the pattern. She was no longer just recording her history. She was cartographing the holes that were being shot through it.

*

In her tower, the Curator watched the pale, gaping scar appear on Isla’s tapestry. She felt a flicker of annoyance, a sharp pinprick in her glacial composure. This was not the expected response. The expected response was confusion, then disorientation, then a frantic, pathetic attempt to re-create the lost memory, which would only result in a weak, derivative facsimile—a forgery of feeling. The weaver’s cloth should have begun to look desperate, incoherent.

Instead, the girl was weaving the void itself.

The Curator looked down at her own page. The colourless ink had done its work. The word “*heather-lanolin-safety-mother*” was gone, not crossed out, but utterly excised, leaving the parchment smooth and unmarked. It was a clean, surgical deletion. Yet on the tapestry, the corresponding space was not clean. It was a ragged, acknowledged wound. The girl was refusing to accept the revised text. She was inserting a footnote of protest. She was, in essence, writing “*HERE THERE WAS SOMETHING*” over the Curator’s elegant erasure.

The Curator’s frigid smile returned, thinner now. A footnote could be deleted too. It was merely a matter of scope. She had targeted a single, potent sensory memory. A tactical strike. The weaver had responded by making a monument to the strike. Very well. If the girl wished to play at monuments, the Curator would give her ruins.

She dipped her pen again into the well of colourless ink. This time, she did not target a specific word. She drew a careful, precise line through an entire clause. A sequence of memories, a causal chain. The afternoon Isla, aged twelve, had decided she would be an academic. The spark of that decision had been a specific conversation with her father, Alasdair, in his book-crammed study. He had been explaining the etymology of the word “liminal,” his hands carving the space of the threshold in the air. The dusty, papery smell of the room, the way the late sun had caught the motes of dust, turning them to gold, the feeling of her own mind stretching to grasp a concept that felt both new and ancient—that was the clause. The Curator blotted it out.

On the loom, Isla gasped. The shuttle fell from her fingers, clattering to the flagstones. A wave of dizziness passed over her, not of illness, but of profound disorientation. She knew, intellectually, that she had a doctorate. She knew she had written a thesis. But the *why* of it, the original, emotional engine that had set her on that path, was gone. It was as if her academic self was a readymade garment she had found hanging in a closet, with no memory of having chosen it or tailored it to fit. The passion behind it was absent. The cloth in front of her, the very tapestry that was her life’s work, suddenly seemed like someone else’s project. A technical exercise.

This was the true horror. It was not the loss of a fond memory. It was the loss of *motive*. It was the severing of the thread between cause and effect in her own psyche. She was becoming a collection of outcomes with no origins.

She bent, slowly, to retrieve the shuttle. Her body felt heavy, borrowed. The lens of gender dynamics, which she had wielded as an analytical tool, was now the scalpel being used on her. Her identity as a woman, as a scholar, as a daughter—these were not innate truths, but narratives. And narratives could be edited. The Curator, the ultimate archivist, was proving that history was not written by the victors, but by the editors. And the first thing any editor in that ancient, brutal tradition did was erase the contributions of the women, or rewrite them as footnotes to the actions of men.

Isla’s fear crystallised into a cold, hard rage. It was a quiet rage, the kind that settles in the bones. She would not let her life be reduced to a palimpsest where her own truths were scrubbed away to make room for the Curator’s sterile, controlling narrative. If the Curator was deleting clauses, she would write in the margins. If the Curator was burning the library, she would become the living library.

She did not try to re-weave the lost motive for her academic life. Instead, she took a thread of a defiant, bloody crimson. She wove a new pattern, right over the grey, amnesiac patch. It was a pattern of questions. A series of angular, interrupted shapes that asked “why?” without answering. It was the visual representation of doubt, of a self interrogating its own missing foundations. It was not a restoration. It was a rebellion. She was using the loom not to recreate what was lost, but to document the act of loss itself. Her tapestry was becoming a record of the siege.

*

The Curator observed the crimson questions bloom on the page. Her annoyance solidified into a cold, professional respect. The girl had spirit. She was not merely enduring the unwriting; she was annotating it. She was turning her own disintegration into a subject of study. This was… unprecedented. And dangerous.

The dialogue had become a vicious cycle. The Curator erased. Isla wove the erasure. The Curator’s act of control became the very subject matter of Isla’s resistance. The more the Curator tried to simplify the narrative, the more complex and meta-textual Isla’s tapestry became. The weaver was no longer just telling her story. She was telling the story of her story being stolen. She had internalised the lens, turned it inward, and was now weaving the dynamics of the power struggle itself.

This could not be allowed. A story about a struggle for narrative control was still a story with two authors. The Curator required a single author. A single will.

She placed her pen down. The well of colourless ink was half-empty. Tactical deletions were insufficient. The girl was too agile, too conceptual. She needed a strategic obliteration. Not a clause, but a chapter. Not a memory, but a foundational myth.

Every woman’s story, the Curator knew, was built upon a private mythology. A core, often unspoken, narrative about her own power, her own worth, her own body. For Isla MacLeod, the Curator surmised, that mythology was rooted in a specific, physical truth: her autonomy. Her right to her own borders. The most potent example of that would be a memory of violation, of a border crossed, and of her own successful defence of it. A memory where she said “no” and made it stick. That would be the keystone in the arch of her selfhood.

The Curator’s knowledge was not omniscient; it was inferential, drawn from the patterns of the tapestry itself. She saw the themes of liminality, of gendered cartography, of the defence of soft borders against hard invasions. She searched the pages of Isla’s life for a memory that functioned as the ur-text for these themes.

She found it. A memory from Isla’s late teens. A walk home on a winter’s evening in Glasgow. A man, a shadow with hands and breath. A threat that condensed out of the fog. And Isla, not with a scream, but with a cold, precise fury. A knee, a crack, a curse fading into the damp night. Not a victim’s story. A sovereign’s. A story where her body was not a petition to be granted safety, but a territory with defended borders. The memory was laced with the metallic taste of fear, the burning in her lungs, and the subsequent, fierce, glowing certainty: *This is mine. You cannot have it.*

That was the myth. That was the chapter title: *The Sovereignty of the Self*.

The Curator took up her pen. She did not dip it into the colourless ink. She plunged the entire well over the page, onto that chapter. She would not just erase the words. She would dissolve the parchment they were written on. She would un-write the event so completely that its absence would not even be a scar; it would be a smooth, unbroken continuity of a life that had never known such a threat, and therefore had never needed to discover that particular flavour of power. A life that was softer, more pliable, more *authored*.

In the bothy, Isla was weaving the crimson questions when the world dropped out from under her.

It was not a dizziness. It was a cessation. A fundamental re-ordering of gravity. She slumped forward, her hands catching the frame of the loom to keep from falling. A vast, quiet nothingness opened up inside her. It wasn't a gap. It was a new landscape.

She looked at her own hands, gripping the wood. They were her hands. But the history in them was different. The muscle memory of a certain kind of flight, of a certain kind of fight, was gone. The subtle, permanent awareness that lived in the shoulders of women who walk alone at night—the low-grade radar, the calculated risk—it had vanished. In its place was a… neutrality. A strange, unearned peace. She felt lighter, but it was the terrifying lightness of a boat cut from its anchor, drifting into open sea.

She tried to conjure the memory. The winter fog, the footsteps, the shape in the dark. It was not that she couldn’t remember it. It was that the memory had no emotional or somatic resonance. It was a dry fact, like a date from a history textbook about someone else’s war. The fear, the adrenaline, the triumphant fury—all were gone. The event was archived, but its meaning was deleted.

And with it, something else faded. The driving urgency behind her thesis, her obsession with liminal spaces and safe passage. Why had she cared so much about borders? She couldn’t quite recall. It seemed an abstract, intellectual concern. The fire in her work, the personal, political fury that had fuelled it, was extinguished. Her tapestry, which moments ago had been a record of defiant resistance, now looked like a complex, but ultimately decorative, academic exercise.

This was the seed of destruction, planted deep in the fertile dark of the “all seems lost.” The Curator had not taken her strength. She had taken the *reason* for her strength. She had not made Isla weak. She had made her… *reasonable*. Unmotivated. She had edited out the foundational trauma that had forged her will, and in doing so, had softened the metal of her character. Isla sat before her life’s work and felt a profound, chilling apathy. What was the point of this beautiful, intricate cloth? Who was it for?

The hook for the final chapter was not in a looming threat or a gathering storm. It was in the quiet, empty hearth of Isla’s own spirit. It was in the way she looked at the shuttle in her hand, not as a weapon or a voice, but as a simple, pointless tool. The Curator had won the war for the past. She had rewritten Isla’s origins. Now, Isla had to face the ultimate question: can a self, stripped of the myth that forged it, find a new reason to fight? Can a woman, her history edited into placidity, rediscover a voice that is not a reaction to violation, but a creation born of something else? The battle was over. The war of will was beginning. And Isla MacLeod had just been disarmed of her deepest, most personal *why*.



---

# Chapter 24

## **CHAPTER 24**

The whisky was a peat-bog ghost, haunting the glass. Isla MacLeod held it up to the weak Glasgow light bleeding through the pub window, watching the amber liquid cling to the sides like a confession. It was ten in the morning. The pub, *The Hanged Man*, smelled of last night’s spilled lager and this morning’s despair, a perfume she was coming to know intimately.

She set the glass down, untouched. The ritual, once a defiant communion with her own sharp edges, felt like a borrowed gesture. A line from a play she no longer remembered the plot to.

“He’s late,” she said, to no one.

“He’s a concept, hen,” Morag replied from behind the bar, polishing a pint glass with a cloth that had seen more battles than the standard at Culloden. “Concepts are never late. They just are, or they aren’t.” Morag was seventy if she was a day, her hair a vibrant, improbable aubergine, her arms a roadmap of faded blue tattoos. She ran *The Hanged Man* with the serene brutality of a pirate queen overseeing a derelict ship. Her eyes, the colour of old slate, scraped over Isla. “You look… tidy.”

It was an accusation. Isla *was* tidy. Her hair was pulled back in a simple, efficient knot. Her clothes—dark trousers, a grey jumper—were clean, unadorned. She looked like a competent archivist, a woman who filed her own rage in alphabetical order. The tapestry, rolled and sealed in a protective tube, leaned against the scarred wood of the booth like an umbrella. A tool for weather. Not a weapon. Not a voice.

Her concept was Professor Alistair Finch. Emeritus Chair of Comparative Metaphysics, University of Glasgow. Author of *The Architecture of Unseen Bridges: A Treatise on Interstitial Realities*. He had written her a letter on thick, cream paper that felt like pressed bone. *Your thesis on the gendered cartography of liminal spaces in Scottish folklore shows a promising, if undisciplined, mind. The Hanged Man, Tuesday, 10 a.m. Do not be late.* The letter was months old, a relic from a self who had cared about such summons. The Isla who had received it had been a creature of furious purpose, her mind a loom of urgent threads. That Isla was gone. In her place was this reasonable woman, who had come here today not out of passion, but because she had, in her new and placid state, simply forgotten to cancel.

The door opened, swallowing a wedge of damp, grey street. The man who entered was a collection of angles wrapped in a tweed overcoat several sizes too large. Professor Alistair Finch had the look of a heron that had been stuffed by a taxidermist with a grudge. His glasses were small, round, and perched on the end of a blade of a nose. He scanned the pub as if it were a disappointing archaeological dig.

His gaze landed on her. He did not smile. He navigated the tables and arrived at her booth, shedding his coat to reveal a cardigan patched at the elbows with leather. He did not sit. He looked at the whisky glass, then at her face.

“You’re not drinking it,” he said. His voice was dry, precise, a scalpel on a tray.

“No,” Isla said.

“Good. It’s swill. Morag! A pot of the Lapsang. The stuff in the black tin, not the floor-sweepings you serve to tourists.” He slid into the booth opposite her, his movements brittle. His eyes, magnified by the lenses, were a pale, watery blue. They fixed on the tube. “That’s it, I presume. The famous tapestry.”

“It is.”

“A textile thesis. A feminine argument.” He said it not with disdain, but with the flat curiosity of a linguist examining an unfamiliar declension. “You proposed that the liminal—the betwixt and between—is not a neutral wilderness, but a space historically gendered female. A space to be traversed, exploited, or feared by the male journeyer. A space where women are either guides, monsters, or the very path itself. Never the authors of the map.”

Isla felt a faint, distant echo. A vibration in a hollow place. “Yes.”

“And you used the folkloric record—the *caoineag*, the washer at the ford, the Queen of Elphame’s shifting roads—to argue that women’s magic, women’s power, has been systematically relegated to these interstitial zones. A containment strategy. Keep the unpredictable forces in the corridors, not the halls of power.”

“Yes.” The words were correct, but they felt recited. They had no heat.

Finch leaned back, steepling his fingers. They were long, stained with old ink. “A compelling thesis. Flawed, but compelling. Your flaw was one of foundation. You assumed the gendered dynamic was the primary architecture. It is not. It is a later renovation. A terrible, gaudy wallpaper pasted over the original stone.”

Morag thumped a small teapot and two china cups on the table. The tea inside smelled of smoke and damp earth. Finch poured, his movements economical. He pushed a cup toward Isla.

“The primary architecture,” he continued, “is about *ownership*. Not of space, but of narrative. Of myth. The most profound violence is not to push someone into the corridor. It is to edit the reason they walked into it in the first place. To take the wound that made them a warrior and recast it as a minor scrape. A warrior without their foundational wound is just a person holding a sword. Eventually, they put it down. They become… reasonable.”

Isla’s hand, reaching for the teacup, stilled. The air in the pub thickened. The scent of smoke and earth became the scent of a void, of archival dust. She looked at Finch, truly looked. His eyes were not old. They were *ancient*. They held a familiar, chilling patience.

“You,” Isla breathed. The word had no fear in it. It was an identification.

“A aspect. A projection,” Finch said, sipping his tea. “The Curator is a role, not a single entity. A function of the Archive. I am one of its librarians. You met my… colleague. The one who prefers a more direct editorial hand.”

“The woman in the void.”

“Indeed. She deals in erasure. I deal in… context.” He gestured to the tapestry tube. “Your work, as it was, was a splinter in the Archive’s flesh. A narrative of resistance growing from a specific, traumatic root. It was dissonant. My colleague sought to remove the root. A crude but effective surgery. She left you… tidy.” He said the word with Morag’s inflection. “But a story, even a pacified one, still exists. It can be refiled. Given a new, harmless context. That is my task.”

Isla felt nothing but a cold, crystalline clarity. The emptiness inside her was no longer a void; it was a clean, well-lit room. And in it, she saw the shape of the trap with perfect, dispassionate vision. “You’re here to give me a new *why*.”

“An *instead-of*,” Finch corrected. “A noble, scholarly *instead-of*. Your thesis, stripped of its personal fury, is merely an interesting academic footnote. I am here to offer you a position. A research fellowship. A quiet life of cataloguing, not creating. You would be an expert on liminal spaces, not a resident of them. You would be respected. Reasonable. The tapestry would be a curious early work, a phase. The fire in it would be read as youthful exuberance, not a core truth.” He smiled, a thin crack in parchment. “It is a kinder fate than oblivion. Most never get an *instead-of*. They simply get a *never-was*.”

He was right. It was kind. It was the peace she had pretended to want in her darkest hours. A world where her pain was not a forge, but a footnote. A life where her gender was a category of study, not a lived battlefield. She could put down the shuttle. She could stop being a needle, piercing through layer after layer of meaning. She could be a thread, lying smooth and untroubled in the weave.

She looked at the tapestry tube. The Isla who had woven it was gone. That woman’s reason—the searing, personal violation that had made borders a life-or-death concern—was a dry fact in a deleted file. That warrior was dismantled.

But the tapestry itself remained. It was evidence of a *process*, not just a product. It was a record of a hand moving, a choice following a choice, a colour placed beside another colour. It was a thing *made*.

Finch was watching her, reading her face like a text. “There is no shame in it, Ms. MacLeod. The greatest human freedom is the freedom to re-interpret one’s own past. We are offering you that freedom. Your new chapter writes itself. A quiet study, a respected career. No more monsters. No more thresholds. Just… pleasant, intellectual order.”

Isla’s fingers touched the cool porcelain of the teacup. She thought of Morag’s tattoos, a roadmap of choices etched into skin. She thought of her own skin, unmarked. She thought of the shuttle in her hand, feeling like a pointless tool.

Then she thought of the tool itself. Not as an extension of a reason, but as an object with heft and shape. A thing that could be held, regardless of why.

“The freedom to re-interpret,” she said, her voice quiet in the pub’s gloom. “Is that what you call it?”

“It is a profound gift.”

“A gift you give,” she said. “Which means it’s a gift you can take away. That’s not freedom. That’s curation.”

For the first time, a flicker of something other than detached interest crossed Finch’s face. Impatience. “Semantics. The outcome is peace.”

Isla looked past him, to Morag behind the bar. The pirate queen was polishing the same glass, her expression one of profound boredom. But her eyes, when they flicked to Isla, held a glint. A challenge. *Concepts are never late. They just are, or they aren’t.*

Isla’s concept of herself was gone. Edited. What was left?

The hand. The tool. The cloth.

She unrolled the tapestry on the table, pushing the teacups aside. Finch made a small, irritated sound. The vibrant, chaotic landscape of threads spilled out—the bloody ford, the dark forest paths, the queen’s road stitched in silver and shadow. It was beautiful. It was meaningless to her now.

“You see?” Finch said softly. “It’s just a thing. A complex, decorative thing. The passion is archived. Let it be. Let *yourself* be.”

Isla ran her fingers over the surface. She felt the ridges of thread, the variations in texture. She came to the final panel, the one she had been working on when the void had taken her. It was unfinished. It showed the entrance to a cave, the threshold suggested by a mere hint of darker wool. It was an opening. A mouth. A beginning with no clear end.

Her fingers traced the cave’s suggested outline. And she remembered something. Not the memory itself, but the *feeling* of the memory’s shape. The hollow where the fear had been. It was a specific hollow. It had contours.

She looked at Finch. “You took the *why*. You took the fuel. But you left me the loom.”

“A loom is inert without a weaver with a design,” he said.

“Is it?” Isla asked. The question was genuine. She was not arguing from passion. She was observing from the clean, well-lit room. “What if the design isn’t the point? What if the point is the *weaving*?”

Finch blinked. The concept, as he understood it, did not compute.

Isla picked up the tapestry. She did not look at the images. She felt the weight of it. The sheer, physical *fact* of it. Miles of thread. Hundreds of hours. A monumental act of *doing*.

The reason for the doing was gone. But the *done* remained.

And in that clean, well-lit room of her self, a new question formed. It was not a fiery *why*. It was a calm, practical *how*.

How does a woman move, when her pain is no longer her engine?

She stood up, rolling the tapestry with slow, deliberate care. Finch watched her, his head tilted.

“The fellowship,” he said. “The offer stands. But only now. Walk out that door with that cloth, and you walk into the wilderness. A wilderness you no longer have the rage to navigate. You will be lost. Not heroically. Just… pointlessly.”

Isla slid the tapestry back into its tube. She fastened the cap. She looked at the untouched whisky, the steaming tea. She looked at Morag, who gave her the faintest, most imperceptible nod—the nod of a queen seeing a sailor finally glance at the stars, not the storm.

“I’m already lost,” Isla said. Her voice was calm. Reasonable, even. “My map was deleted. But I still have my feet.”

She walked out of *The Hanged Man*. The damp Glasgow air wrapped around her, smelling of diesel and distant rain. She stood on the pavement, the tube under her arm. The city moved around her—people with purposes, with reasons, with fires banked or blazing.

She had none. She was a vessel swept clean. A loom, empty.

But a loom has a structure. A vessel has a shape.

She began to walk. Not toward the university. Not toward home. She walked without destination. She observed the thresholds she passed: the door of a close, the gap between two buildings, the bridge over the murky Kelvin. They were just architectural features. They held no terror, no magic. They were… spaces.

And as she walked, she paid attention to her body. The swing of her arms. The solid strike of her boots on the pavement. The weight of the tube in her grip. She paid attention to the act of moving through space. The simple, physical fact of traversal.

She walked for hours. The sky darkened prematurely, the Glasgow gloom thickening into drizzle. She found herself on the banks of the Clyde, watching the water, the colour of cold slate, slide past. It was a threshold, too. A moving line between here and there.

Her hand went to the tube. She uncapped it, pulled out a foot of the tapestry. She looked at the unfinished cave. An opening.

From her pocket, she took her small sewing kit. A needle, a few basic threads. She did not have her proper materials. She had the wrong colours. It didn’t matter.

She sat on a damp bench, the tapestry across her knees. The drizzle beaded on the wool. She threaded a needle with a length of ordinary, grey cotton. She did not think about meaning. She did not think about resistance or gender or ownership of narrative. She thought about the next stitch.

Her needle pierced the cloth near the cave mouth. She pulled the grey thread through. It was a neutral colour. A colour of fog, of uncertainty. She made another stitch. And another.

She was not weaving a story of trauma. She was not weaving a story at all. She was simply weaving. The hand moved. The thread followed. The cave’s outline began to extend, not with dramatic shadow, but with a suggestion of depth in simple, unremarkable grey.

This was not a reaction. It was not a defiance born of a wound. It was something quieter, more fundamental. It was an assertion of presence. *I am here. My hand moves. Therefore, I continue.*

The Curator had taken the myth that forged her. She could not reclaim it; it was gone, a fossil in someone else’s archive. So she would not try. She would not search for a new, grand reason. A new trauma to define her. A new banner to wave.

She would be defined by the act, not the cause. By the making, not the meaning imposed upon it.

The drizzle soaked into her hair, her jumper. She kept stitching. The grey thread built the cave’s depth, stitch by mundane stitch. It was not beautiful. It was not powerful. It was just a thing being done.

And in the doing, a faint warmth began to kindle. Not the roaring fire of old fury, but the low, steady heat of metabolism. Of a body alive and in motion. It was not a reason. It was a *condition*.

She was not a warrior reclaiming her sword. She was a woman, sitting in the rain, sewing. And that, in its utter simplicity, was a territory the Curator had not thought to claim. They had archived the grand narratives of pain and triumph. They had no category for this quiet, persistent *doing*.

A figure approached along the river path. Not Finch. A woman, younger, her coat pulled tight against the damp. She paused, looking at Isla on the bench, stitching in the rain.

“You’ll ruin it,” the woman said, her voice friendly, concerned.

Isla looked up. She looked at the stranger, then down at the tapestry. The vibrant, old work, and the new, plain grey growth emerging from it.

“No,” Isla said, her voice calm. Certain in a way that had nothing to do with reason. “I’m just adding to it.”

The woman smiled, shrugged, and walked on.

Isla watched her go. Then she looked back at her hands. At the needle. At the cloth.

The battle of the past was over. She had lost. The war of will was not about reclaiming what was taken. It was about discovering what could be built on the empty ground that remained.

She was not weaving a new myth. She was weaving the next stitch. And the one after that.

And that, she realized as the grey thread pulled through the linen, was a kind of power no archive could ever catalogue. It was the power of the hand that moves, not because the story demands it, but because it is what hands do. It was a creation born of nothing more, and nothing less, than her own ongoing presence in the world.

The hook for the final chapter was not in a victory, or a reckoning. It was in the next stitch. And the one after that. It was in the quiet, relentless fact of a woman, disarmed of every reason, choosing—simply, physically—to continue. The tapestry would grow, in plain, unheroic grey. And she would grow with it, not from a wound, but from a motion. The ultimate expression of the gendered dynamic was not to transcend it, but to render it irrelevant. To become, finally, neither guide nor monster nor path, but simply the hand that holds the needle. The self, as verb.
